The Red Page
English Literature, 1912
In the universities and the schools English
literature usually stops just where it should
begin; the text-books and the professors
omit the last half century or so. Just when
the student is becoming interested in what
the whole course of English literature is
leading up to, there is a blank; the many-
tributaried river peters out into a desert;
the brilliant procession beautiful with gor-
geous banners approaches the saluting-point
of the Now—and disperses—a mere tangled
mob. English literature ends in a grave;
the text-books decline critically to deal with
any force that is not dead. The historian
is safer in his judgment of Queen Anne than
of King O'Malley. There is something con-
cise and finished in a corpse; anybody can
run his critical foot-rule over a coffin ; and
there is no fear that its inhabitant will
refute our judgments or will creep out of
the pigeonhole wherein we have satisfactor-
ily buried him. Thus Eminent Citizens will
orate at public meetings about the great-
ness of Shakespeare (bones) ; but none of
them could give a critical pronouncement
on the place in the dramatic literature of
Australia that will be occupied by Louis
Esson. Thus, too, Sydney professors give
extension lectures on Browning (a corpse),
but decline to investigate and analyse the
meaning of Bernard O’Dowd. It is as diffi-
cult to estimate the place in literature of a
living writer, unless, of course, he is a dead
living writer like Kipling, as it is to say the
final neat word upon a volcano.. In a living
writer an explosion might occur at any
minute that would quite change his literary
shape. So the writers of text-books and the
professors simply lead our scholars to the
edge of the delectable land of modern litera-
ture—the literature of this decade, the litera-
ture of September 26, 1912—and hastily
write “Finis.”
It is admitted that unless literary text-
books were published every morning in the
year, with possibly an evening edition to catch
up with some new poet, it would be impossi-
ble for English literature to be quite up-to-
date. . At this very moment an unknown
poet in Dungog may be writing an imperish-
able ode, or photo-play, or “Cobra” ad. —
the literature of the future will have its own
forms; but even if he wirelessed his ode, or
gasp, to every literary review to-morrow, he
would have to wait a lustrum or so before he
got into a text-book and was allotted his
tentative place in Dungog and literature.
J*
lnto this unexplored critical country J. M.
Kennedy leads his well-equipped expedition.
His volume, English Literature, from 1880
to 1905 (Swift, per Sydney Book Club), is
admirably written; and he contrives to give
a useful tentative summing up of the main
tendencies of our own time. His division
into chapters, and the space he gives indi-
vidual writers enables us to give a graphic
diagram, of the main and subsidiary streams
of English literature since 1880, according
to the map of J. M. Kennedy. So, by indi-
cating in the size of type the relative im-
portance of each writer, as derived from the
space allotted to each author, we get a result
like this :—•
WALTER PATER
OSCAR WILDE
“THE YELLOW BOOK’’ SCHOOL
Max Beerbohm, Arthur Waugh, Hubert
Crackanthorpe, Lionel Johnson, Henry Har-
land, Arthur Symons, Ernest Dowson,
Aubrey Beardsley, Whistler,
SHAW
WELLS
GEORGE GISSING
W. B. Yeats, George Moore, John Davidson,
Fiona MacLeod, “ A.E.” (George Russell),
Francis Thompson, W. L. Courtney,
St. John Hankin, R. B. Cunninghame Graham, Richard
Te Gallienne, Laurence Binyon.
One name is missing that deserved a
place: Kipling. But Kennedy finds that, ex-
cept as a short-story writer, he is hardly
worth notice. As a poet, his verse is too
temporal, too timely. “Patriotism alone is
not sufficient excuse for a poem, and, unfor-
tunately, most of Mr. Kipling’s poetical
work is not distinguished by the imagination
we can find in men like Ernest Dowson,
Arthur Symons, or John Davidson.... But
as a short-story writer we need have no
hesitation in comparing him with the best
Russian or French masters.”
More astonishing, however, is the omission
of the names of Henry James, Conrad,
Hardy, Meredith and (possibly) Bennett.
But perhaps the author would not call these
writers “dynamic.”
Kennedy’s attitude to these contemporaries
of his is rather irritatingly lordly. His sym-
pathies are with “culture,” but culture occa-
sionally stoops to a sneer.
From the late ’9o’s onward we heard very
little of the literary, philosophical, political
or sociological movements which had been
initiated about the ’Bo’s. Prof. George
Samtsbury, Mr. Austin Dobson, and Mr.
Edmund Gosse have ceased to write with
their former fervor and vigor. Mr. George
Bernard Shaw and Mr. H. G. Wells have
gradually laid less and less emphasis upon
their former extreme socialistic opinions, and
instead of leading us towards the millennium,
they have settled down into wealthy and
thoroughly respectable members of the upper
bourgeoise. If Mr. George Moore and Mr.
W. B. Yeats have not become quite so suc-
cessful in a worldly sense, they do not at all
events write with the same enthusiasm and
passion as of yore. The romantic movement
of the ’Bo’s and ’9o’s, then, soon ex-
hausted itself. A few of its representatives,
like Dowson and Lionel Johnson, died sad
deaths; its foremost figure was put in gaol;
and others, reserved for a worse fate, became
respectable members of society.
Excellent text-book criticism, but open to
the explanation that these romantic leaders
have merely grown old. The romantic
movement belongs to the young, and as these
young writers have not yet crept into the
text-books, Kennedy has as yet no official
cognisance of them
The author does not discuss Shaw, but
Mister Shaw is quite a different person, of
whom it is, of course, possible to say rude
things like these :
All his life he has lacked hanpiness ; that
is to say, he has lacked artistic harmony and
unity, and however much his works may
interest or amuse us, we, as artists, are faced
with the fact that there is an inartistic bit-
terness and restlessness in almost every one
of Shaw’s characters, in almost every page
he wrote.... In spite of all Mr. Shaw’s es-
says on music aud the drama, however, the
bent of his mind was never imaginative, but
always scientific 1 myself would never
condemn Mr. Shaw’s plays as plays by say-
ing that they would undermine the founda-
tions of morality and lead to anarchy, etc.
It is sufficient to condemn them as plays by
saying that they are dull and ugly, and they
are dull and ugly in the first place because
they endeavor to propagate an idea, or to
show up some social injustice, which would
be better propagated or shown up in the
form of a Fabian tract or an essay. For
drama is the representation of some phase
of life by means of action, and Mr. Shaw’s
argumentative style, in the second place,
tends to do away with action altogether
There are, of course, popular elements in
most of Mr. Shaw’s plays. His wit is bitter
and venomous, and might perhaps be re-
garded as a kind of intellectual red pepper
or vinegar to help down the stodginess of the
sociological or economic problem around
which the play is written... .In spite of
these criticisms, however, Mr. Shaw must
have his due; we are indebted to him for
a good deal.... He has made English critics
familiar with various aspects of Continental
thought. .. .It was chiefly Shaw who made
Ibsen, Wagner, Schopenhauer and Nietzsche
familiar to a wide circle. He kept himself
in touch with every dynamic movement
abroad, whether in painting, music, philoso-
phy or economics. It is my personal belief
that Mr. Shaw entirely misunderstood vari-
ous phases of Nietzsche, that he was quite
capable of setting Wagner and Ibsen on a
level with him, and that he failed to per-
ceive the malign effect that the Scandinavian
drama was likely to exercise on art. Never-
theless the fact that he could take an in-
terest in these things at all sets him at once
on a higher level than his contemporaries.
.... From an artistic point of view we can-
not award Mr. Shaw any very high praise.
His works, I think, are destined to perish
before the century is up, partly because they
are formless, and partly because the subjects
his plays deal with are ephemeral... .Though
we must condemn Mr. Shaw as an artist,
however, my sympathies are entirely with
him when I see him pitted against the dull,
stupid, Philistine British public.
Mr. Wells [again the- depreciatory Mis-
ter!] is anything you like—an interesting
personality, a clever writer, a man of bril-
liant scientific imagination, an ardent social
reformer, a leader of English thought, even
—but his works are not artistic works ; not
at ail events in the sense in which we speak
of the works of a novelist like Fielding as
artistic works We may describe them as
the works of a seljf-made artist... .We must
assume that Mr. Wells takes a remarkably
low view of art when he suggests that the
coming ‘ artist” who writes novels is going
to discuss” problems, especially problems
of conduct. But worse than this is to fol-
low, for Mr. Wells tells us that this novelist
of his will not teach, but plead. This is
indeed the very antithesis of art. Art com-
mands. It gives you an imperious message,
but it never pleads... .Mr. Wells wishes the
novelist to discuss various aspects of moral-
ity ; for religion itself and the relations of the
sexes are the two main features summed up
m the word “conduct.” To refute Mr. Wells
it is hardly necessary to quote two of his
contemporaries against him, Oscar Wilde
Arthur Symons, who both held very
definite views on the relationship which art
bears to morality. The moment a novelist
begins to discuss conduct he ceases to be
imaginative, and he Ceases to be an artist.
. Kennedy finds that Wells is not interested
m men except insofar as they serve to illus-
trate a sociological or political principle.
(Surely he forgets Kipps?) And Wells’
women are all “appallingly sexual and hun-
ger for babies.” (Why shouldn’t they?)
And he insists that the importance Wells
attributes to sex is merely a feature of the
middle-class intellect. His humanity is
merely the middle-class humanity, his senti-
ment and idealism merely the reflex of the
sentimental and idealistic middle-classes to
which he belongs. (This is “culture” heav-
ing a nasty brick: we may confidently expect
to see it rebound in Wells’s next novel.)
Kennedy permits himself a little pro-
phecy :
So far as the great public is concerned,
English literaure of the next few years will,
it should seem, be dominated by the novel;
and most of the novel writers will follow the
models of Mr. Wells. Shaw, now having
become a popular dramatist, will doubtless
influence the theatre, particularly through
the one-act play. I cannot see at the moment
any signs of successors to Dowson and
Symons. Crackanthorpe’s tradition may be
carried on; but so-called realists should re-
member that realism is not confined to the
seamy side of life. In the course of the next
few years Nietzsche will, beyond all doubt,
become a potent force in England, as he has
long been on the Continent, and with his
further study here, critics, it is to be hoped,
will come to recognise the sharp distinction
between aristocratic and democratic 'litera-
ture ; form characterising the first no less
than absence of form characterises\ the
second; the one being art, and the other
pseudo-art.
The Montessori Method
Everyone of us has a vivid remembrance
of the utter futility, the long drudgery, the
painstaking muddle of our school-days. Only
when we left school did it dawn upon us
that our education was about to begin.
Schools taught us nothing of value in life
except the elementary, uninteresting facts,
and in teaching those they contrived to instil
in us a deep and persistent hatred of learn-
ing. But now the renaissance of learning
has come—and from the land of the Dago.
Dr. Maria Montessori has organised educa-
tion on new lines, simply by her study of
the psychology of the child and her intuition
and ingenuity as a teacher. The result is
that from a few magazine articles the know-
ledge of her revolutionary method has been
eagerly assimilated by the world. In N.S.W.,
for instance, the new theory is being ade-
quately tested. All this before the inventor’s
book had been translated into English. Now
The Montessori Method (Stokes, New York)
comes to explain in detail the meaning and
the method of this revolution in child educa-
tion. This is no theoretical treatise ; Maria
Montessori has ample proofs to show of the
astounding progress made by the tiny chil-
dren educated on her rational lines.
It is impossible to indicate even generally
the contents of this interesting book—the
Magna Charta of the child’s rights. The
fundamental principle laid down is that of
the liberty of the pupil—such . liberty as
shall permit a development of individual,
spontaneous manifestations of the child’s
nature. _ And liberty is activity. “We do
not consider a child an individual disciplined
only when he has been rendered as artificially
silent as a mute and as immovable as a
paralytic.” It will come as a surprise to
the average parent and the average school-
teacher to learn that perfect discipline holds
in the Montessori schools of children from
three to seven years, without any system of
rewards or of punishments, and with each
child occupied at his particular game. Col-
lective lessons are abolished. Children,
it has been discovered are —children. Our
schools still regard them as diminutive men.
The materials by means of which the
child is assisted in its natural development
are remarkable for their ingenuity and their
simplicity. The child is led to develop first
the muscular-tactile_ sense: the sense of
touch and that of vision are systematically
educated by means of what are regarded by
the child as the most delightful games with
the most entrancing toys. The Montessori
children learn how to dress and wash them-
selves, and how to move gracefully, at ages
when the usual child is a mere wailing,
clumsy piece of perplexed and irritated pro-
toplasm. Most remarkable of all its achieve-
ments of this systein is the ease and certainty
with which the child learns to write and
read. The preliminary training is so scien-
tifically graduated that one day, without
ever having written before, the child finds
that he can write, and does write, and keeps
on writing in a fury of delight. The scene
when the first Montessori class of children
suddenly broke out into writing is drama-
tically described by the delighted author,
bhe explains this sudden achievement of a
new faculty by showing that the child,
trained on her methods, possesses all the
movements necessary for writing. When it
thought of writing, it merely wrote"; and it
could write any word, and in a caligraphy
that w °uld shame most of the contributors
to The Bulletin. For these children had
not to undergo that tedious pothook and
hanger preparation that caused every one
of us to curse the name of Vere Foster. And
it turns out now that there was not the
slightest need for Vere Foster.
As to the speed at which writing can be
taught to infants, hearken to Maria Mon-
tessori :
“Yh e average time that elapses between
'•he first trial of the preparatory exercises
a ?d the first written word is, for children
ot four years, from a month to a
month and a half. With children of
five years, the period is much shorter,
being about a month. But one of
°h r pupils learned to use in writing
all the letters of the alphabet in 20 days.
Children of four years, after they have been
in school for two months and a half, can
write any word from dictation, .and can pass
to writing with ink in a notebook. Our little
ones are generally experts after three
months’ time. Indeed, writing is one of the
easiest and most delightful of all the con-
quests made by the child As to the exe-
cution, our children write well from the
moment in which they begin Our little
pupils, spontaneously and with a marvellous
security, write entire words without lifting
the pen, maintaining perfectly the slant of
the letters and making the distance between
each letter equal.”
Reading comes later—usually a bare fort-
night later. These children, when they grow
up, will have an extraordinary subtlety of
sensorial perception. But there is no attempt
made by this system to produce prodigies
of learning. They are not self-conscious
prigs, but well-mannered, independent and
happy kids.
The introduction of this system into the
State kindergartens is the first move. The
private kindergartens must next be invaded.
But the main obstacle against which the
new method is liable to be wrecked is that
which Maria Montessori met with in her
own first efforts. The children take with
avidity to this new sort of game; but the
teacher, trained in the old foolish methods,
is harder to educate. First, she must be
taught not to teach. The educators must
first be educated. And the routine-sodden
teacher will not find the new method easy
to acquire. The steady study of this re-
markable book may do much to begin her
own education.
The One-Act Play Competition
H. C. Coghlan writes:—
The “Red Page” of 5/9/T2 expressed a
desire to start an Australian One-Act Play
Competition, and I desire to announce my
hearty approval of the “One-Act” part of the
idea. It has been my firm conviction that
the Australian playwright of two, three
or more acts was making a blind man’s
bloomer in obstinately endeavoring to spread
himself over the entire programme, despite
the many managerial discouragements pol-
itely lavished upon him. All along, it has
been obvious to everyone, save the persistent
Australian writer of professionally-unacted
plays, that “Australian” managements, hav-
ing the world to pick from. Could Do With-
out the A.P. and Were Not Taking Any
Risks. They had reared, developed and estab-
lished a “popular” craze for Imported Stuff,
ranging from piffley to passable and back
again, and they have never even bothered to
prove their vaunted “willingness to con-
sider,” by taking the most likely Australian
scripts in hand and re-shaping them to suit
their own ideas of payable production, as is
done in London. Good Australian plays
have missed production for want of a con-
ference between author and management, on
matters practically of stage detail, of which
the latter must have the better knowledge.
Good play-frames or plays, only a few cuts
and touches short of production standard,
instead of being given every chance, have
been read-—when the common courtesy of
perusal has not been refused —always with
the anti-Australian bias pre-dooming the best
effort. In face of all those obstacles, it
would have been wiser for the Australian
aspirant to have long since tackled the one-
act play. Certainly, very few curtain-raisers
are used during the year, but I feel sure that
had the local output' been confined to one-act
plays in the past, the record of acceptance
and professional rendition would have been
more encouraging than it is to-day. It could
not have been less so. And the author of a
proved-good one-act play would have some
chance of being asked for a longer one.
11. C. Coghlan goes on to criticise the con-
ditions of the competition. He asks who
Mr. Gregan McMahon is, and for one who
claims to be interested in the Australian
play, his ignorance that McMahon is the
soul of the Melb. Repertory Theatre seems
incredible. Coghlan finds, too, the award in-
adequate. So do v/e. And the fact that the
“author’s fee” of £2 2s. carries with it the
strong probability of production by one or
more of the three Repertory theatres in Aus-
tralia does not seem to him worth while. He
regards production by a repertory theatre
as a cul de sac. We do not. And one, at
at least, of these organisations pays authors
fees. He moves as an amendment that “the
theatrical managements of Australia com-
bine in offering, through the “Red Page” and
other media, three prizes of £5O each for the
best practical Australian one-act play, all
script to be forwarded to Blank, at Blank
Theatre, under pen names. Well, it’s up to
the theatrical managements of Australia.
His further amendment that the three Reper-
tory theatres should combine to offer £5O
through this page for the best Australian
one-acter, has about as much chance of ac-
ceptance as the first proposal. The Reper-
tory associations are doing excellent work,
for very little pay : in producing Australian
plays at all. But, if Coghlan insists, it’s also
up to them. Meantime, the offer of two
guineas, “author’s fee,” for the best Aus-
tralian one-acter, with the promise of favor-
able consideration, with a view to produc-
tion, by the Repertory organisations, is re-
peated.

SYDNEY: Angel Place.
“The Religious Editor” : The other morn-
ing, when the sun was high and the birds
were singing, came a small boy tapping at
my door. He bore in one hand the crumpled
pages of an obscure Wowser publication, and
in his rosebud mouth a verbal message from
his employer, a wild Sydney artist, who, he
said, was then engaged taking flying leaps
through the ragged remains of his master-
piece. This was the tender little story that
had stung the man of paint and canvas: —
An artist’s modeller of rare ability found himself
stranded in Sydney. Foiled in every attempt to
secure a position, he set to work on a piece of statu-
ary in plaster of Paris, representing “Paradise before
the Fall.” Alas, the piece was of a class such as
cannot be described in these pages. He got an offer
of £6O for it. It was finished on a Sunday morning.
That evening he was strangely led to attend our
“People’s Service.” The sermon that night so im-
pressed him that he went direct to his room and
broke the statuary to pieces. “I have done right,”
said he afterwards to a friend, “though it cost me
much to do it. And now, see how God has rewarded
me. Within 24 hours I received letters from
England, enclosing money to take me home, and I
am off by the next mail to start afresh in the Old
Country.”
The well-meaning but intellectually hopeless
Wowser must be a continual source of an-
noyance to the Supreme Artist who conceived
the Universe and that lost masterpiece we
know as Paradise. The Wowser’s awful
clothes, incredible whiskers and drab beliefs
indicate his complete unfitness for a world
that is all light and beauty—except where
someone has put up a sewer shaft or a hid-
eous tabernacle where people sing through
their noses. His proper environment is a
bleak cindery place that is dirty grey where
it isn’t pitch black —a place where “Alas!”
sounds appropriate, and a sigh seems nat-
ural, and where sustained howls are not out
of place. That environment is Sheol —not
the bright, blazing Sheol of our grand-
fathers, but the cold, deserted wreck of the
gay old foundry that went insolvent and
closed down when the energetic Devil ab-
sconded with the coal money and started in
the vaudeville missionary line.
* * * *
Something from Hobart about a mud-
hole :
Hobart’s historic rivulet is not likely to rest
quiet in its bed much longer. It has already been
moved on more than once, but this time there will
have to be a serious eviction, as it spills into the
Derwent, right in the midst of the new harbor im-
provement works.
Various schemes have been mooted, the one most
in favor being that of carrying the waters off by
way of the Domain, and, in the subsequent improve-
ments, wiping out Wapping, Hobart’s only slum,
which used at one time to be flooded every time the
creek ran high, and whence a coffin (occupied) was
once swept irrecoverably out to sea.
It was this creek (which, by the way, formed the
subject of the General Order that was the second
bit of printing done in the colony) that, in con-
junction with the depth of the harbor, decided
Collins in fixing the site of Hobart. “A continual
run of water,” says Ivnopwood, “which is very
Excellent; it comes from the lofty Mountain, much
resembling Table Mountain at the Cape of Good
Hope.”
At that time the outfall was near the present
gasholder, in Macquarie-street, where, before the
land was reclaimed, the shores of the cove ran, on
one side to Macquarie Point, and on the other to the
sand spit connecting with Hunter’s Island, now
covered by the Old Wharf, but formerly the site of
the Government stores, and the place where the head
of Hunter, the bushranger, was exhibited on a pole
or clothes-prop. This interesting occurrence, be it
said, had nothing to do with the naming of the
island, which was <Hunter’s for many years before
the outlaw’s time. When smuggling was in vogue,
houses backing on the creek found many an oppor-
tunity to turn a dishonest thrippeny, and the little
Steam Packet Hotel, now squatting in the shadow of
jam factories, and shortly to be engulfed, is said
to have known a lot that was profitable and unlawful
on the subject.
In a somewhat rare book —“Varieties of Vice-
Regal Life”—Lady Denison, wife of one of Tassy’s
comparatively early Governors, gives a lively ac-
count of the floods of ’54. There had been a spell
of drought, as droughts go in Tasmania. Then a big fire
licked through Elizabeth-street, sending rows of wood-
en buildings up’in smoke and snuff, and leaving their
debris to choke the rivulet. In due time the drought
broke—with emphasis—and masses of accumulated
rubbish, swept by the flood, careered down the creek,
pounding the smaller bridges to pieces and battering
at the more substantial structure of Wellington
Bridge. A householder, whose dwelling abutted on
the threatened edifice, rushed to Government House
through the rain, and unearthed the Governor. The
latter mustered a gang of convicts, and came down
to personally direct them in the work of salvage.
One or two men were drowned and a lot of damage
was done. At present the “Crick” leads a tame
and mostly subterranean existence. Leaving the Cas-
cades and the brewery, it dives unobtrusively under
the town, with the smell of beer entangled in its
hair, and the arches which hide it from view carry
streets on their backs. Its deflection will give Hobart
some more valuable space to build on.
“The Religious Editor”: What about a
League for the Suppression of Outrageous
Female Hats? The suburban lady who con-
structs a cheap burlesque of a hatter’s freak
and carries it solemnly round on her head is
dreadfully numerous. Usually she is ob-
served sitting grimly on the opposite seat of
your tram; and she always glances round
suspiciously to make sure no one is grinning
at the wild joke on her head. She naturally
feels nervous about it, because she is per-
fectly well aware that it is a Daring Experi-
ment —something which will either strike
beholders speechless with admiration and get
talked about, or provoke scornful comments.
Great agony of mind and back parlor labor
go to the making of these portable accidents ;
and they are borne into the rush and turmoil
of town in the spirit which animated the
young man who bore the banner with the
strange device up the mountain side. It is
possible that men, given the same variety of
stuffs and architectural license, would oon-
struct equally hideous head-coverings. For-
tunately, though, all their hats are heaped
up cheaply to pattern by specialists, so they
are unable to build themselves nightmares, or
to translate their vain imaginings into con-
crete form. The suburbanette who sets out to
enslave the city with a hat like a camel’s
hump swathed in yelvet, and decorated with a
large feather and two green roses, is a public
nuisance, because she is the bearer of an
elaborate jest which must not be laughed at
on any account. The most unreasonable and
riotous and generally incredible examples
nearly always roost above a miserably anx-
ious countenance upon which is written large
the query: “What do they think of my
Hat?” This sort of tiling should be sup-
pressed, hence the suggested League. Per-
haps tbe Pure Food or Noxious Weeds Act
might be amended to include all hats con-
demned by the Minister for Grace and Ele-
gance.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Song of Hope.
Pluck to-morrow from its socket,
Draw the teeth of yesterday,
While a coin is in our pocket
Let the whole world keep at play ;
Nothing has been seen hereafter,
Nothing will be seen before,
All the happy plumes of laughter
Deck the hearse beside the door.
Let us sing a song of sorrow
To the jig the fiddles play,
And some joyful dirges borrow
From the gods whose hair is grey;
Then with lifted cups a-jingle
Let us make the welkin ring,
While our wedded fingers tingle
To the tune the crickets sing.
Up! The wonder moon is beaming
Young delight on land and sea,
All the planet worlds are dreaming
Wild, ecstatic dreams of me;
All the little stars a-twinkle
In the very darkest blue,
Are from A to Z a-sprinkle
With thought-melodies of you.
All the little birds a-cluster —
Harlequin and Arquebus—
Feel in ancient, roosting fluster
Nervous-tender hints of us;
And along the wide commotion,
At the frail night’s silver hem,
Sweetheart, have we not a notion
That is nebulous to them?
Kiss me, sweetheart, in the morning,
Kiss me where the roses pine,
All their sweetness I am scorning
For the heart that beats with mine ;
Pluck to-morrow from its socket,
Gouge the eyes from yesterday,
While a coin is in our pocket,
Sweetheart, sweetheart, let us pay!
N.S.W.
CURSE O' MOSES.
What Matters?
We see the ship of our dreams go down,
Our best ambitions take* fright and scatter,
Our dearest hope is a trull of the town....
What does it matter?
Our trust in friendship’s a broken reed.
Love pauses a moment to fool and flatter,
Then sells "us to Pelf, at the bidding of
Greed....
What does it matter?
Youth’s pennons flame joyously down the
sky,
But the vultures gather to tear them and
tatter.
Useless 1 to curse as these hours slink by. . . .
What does it matter?
There’s a din in our hearts the dull day
long,
As the grim, strong devils besiege them and
batter.
We are sold to the dogs for a wanton’s song. .
What does it matter?
MAOEILAND.
FRANK MORTON.
The Blue Pacific.
The Blue Pacific hoards her gems,
And all her sands are gold ;
Her clear, green eyes are deep and wise,
And she is many-souled.;
The sun-god dallies on hrir breast,
A roserwreath on his brow;<
Her parting kiss glows warm as his,
A sacrament, a vow!
.Always by white, unwearied scouts
Her beaches are patrolled,
And o’er her graves the sea-mew raves
Above her sands of gold.
Her sky-line sweeping ever east
With funnel-smoke is dark,
Where ships that sail from Sydney trail
The San Francisco bark.'
Where mile-long rollers crumble on
The shifting, smothered beach,
And -froth and brine of surfing-line
Lure, threaten and beseech,
A thousand reapers swing their scythes,
When winds blow nor’ by west,
And big seas suck and drive and duck,
While swimmers swim their best.
While rumble of eternal surf
Beats on the bannered shore,
Out in the swirl the surfing girl
Undaunted, dares the more ;
Where chuckling shallows dart and rove
The anxious matron squats,
With tawny elves that sun themselves,
Her hair in tangled knots.
The onward march of great white hosts
Of tumbling seas is met
Upon the marge by charge on charge
Of surfers brown arid wet;
The cynosure of college-girls,
The beach Apollo struts,
And Doris, cool and beautiful,
A dainty figure cuts!
The Blue Pacific is a maid
Of passion, mood and whim;
She sings a strong, imperious song,
And chants ballade and hymn.
Her woman’s heart- betrays the brave,
And tricks the foolish sw'u'n;
The damsel she, for jealousy,
Snares witlua foamy chain!
To thrilling music surfing men
" Sing still the old. sweet theme;
While in the swirls ungirdle# girls,
Whose dreams are lyric, dream !
And east to California
.. .. The Blue Pacific runs.
Where maidens slim and lovely swim
And beckon Austral sons!
The Blue Pacific lures her prey;
A siren, never old,
Her heart is wise in artifice.
Her gems are set in gold ;
She lures her love with mystic sdng.
He hastens, wonder-led,
And ere her kiss completes his bliss
She flings him backward, dead!
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
Jt is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin/’ is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official alxvays is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence ” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it ivill be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 3, 1912.
BULL AND HIS ETERNAL FRIEND,
JONATHAN.
One day recently High Commissioner George
Reid was moved to be upstanding on his
feet, and to make the usual remarks about
“the two great branches of the Anglo-Saxon
race,” and the indissoluble bonds by which
they are united. It is a pleasant old dinner
platitude, this concerning the perpetual
friendship of Britain and the United States,
and it is one that has seen much service.
Hands have been perfunctorily shaken over
it; flags have been perfunctorily waved over
it; drinks have been drunk over it, on the
general principle that a drink is a good thing
at any time, and any platitude is sufficient
excuse. It is a sort of habit on the part of
British people to assume that war between
the two big English-speaking communities is
impossible and unthinkable, and that “blood
is thicker than water,” and that when John
Bull in his top boots has his national
back to the wall, Jonathan will be there
beside him, fighting earnestly through his
nose, and taking, for his alleged parent s
sake, any chances that are going. It is a
pretty legend, yet some people have their
doubts. There are people who are capable of
doubting almost anything.
* * * *
Certainly the big republic has never been
Bi’itain’s ally in any practical sense. it
has never even been a firm ally in a paper
sense. It has fought two wai’s with the
Bull country, and in each case the result
has tended to enlarge the U.S. head, and to
swell the U.S. vanity, and to depreciate, in
common U.S. opinion, the value of Bi’itain
as a friend. Other wars have been averted
by a hair’s breadth. Frenchmen and Spani-
ards, and Turks and Portuguese and Ger-
mans have fought great battles as Bull’s
comrades in arms, but nothing of the kind
has ever happened with the Yankee commun-
ity. This reduces the so-called United States
friendship to the merest theory. There have
been border disputes between the two na-
tions, and they have been submitted to arbi-
tration, and the invariable result has been
that the republic has come off best. As a
general rule it has explained, in a fairly
definite fashion, that it had to win, and that
it wouldn’t take any notice of the verdict
unless the said verdict was in its favor. In
other w’ords, it tossed with a two-headed
dollar. This has made arbitration l-ather an
unfriendly sort of jest so far as Britain
was concerned. And in commercial
matters the land of G. Washington
is the most successful enemy Britain
possesses. Its policy is to sell all the goods
that are possible; to buy hardly anything
in retui’n; and to strip Bull of gold in ex-
change. Bull buys from his cousin Jona-
than, goods to the amount of about
£122,000,000 per annum, which is not far
short of the amount he buys from France,
Russia and Gei’many combined. And he sells
to his cousin Jonathan goods to the value
of about £56,000,000 per annum, which is less
than the amount of his sales to Germany
alone. It is the worst case of an uneven
balance of trade in the Bi’itish commercial
x’ecoi’ds. So long as the States find their
best market in Bi’itain, and are not expected
to buy in retui’n to any corresponding
extent, and so long as the verdict,
when an arbitration is on, always goes
their way, and so long as the adjacent
British province of Canada supplies a good
field to be exploited by the States’ surplus
population, there is peace and passive good-
will, of a temporary sort. But there has
never been active goodwill—not, at least, on
the American side. The good offices of Bull
helped to keep the ring clear during the
Spanish war, and prevent the decrepit little
Power getting assistance, and such services
are thankfully received. But they are not
thankfully returned, and there is no reason
to suppose that they ever will be.
The familiar theory re the “two branches
of the Anglo-Saxon race” is interesting in its
way. It is good enough until it is closely
inspected. Then it begins to fade to some
extent. The first beginnings of the great
republic were certainly Anglo-Saxon—bit-
terly hostile Anglo-Saxon. In the days of
the War of Independence, and for some time
before and after it, to be an Anglo-
Saxon of British sympathies about Boston
or New York was to be an outcast and
a wild beast. The American Reisrn of Ter-
ror never occupied the place in history which
the French one did, but that was solely for
lack of opportunity and picturesque inci-
dents. It didn’t lead to the public execution
of two sovereigns and some princes, and
many dignitaries. Nor did it chase the
proudest nobility of Europe wholesale into
exile, so that a stack of peers and
potentates had to wander, abroad for
half a generation, weeping, in poetic lan-
guage, about their woes. It didn’t abolish the
Church, abolish religion, suppress God by
Act of Parliament, create a new calendar
with new names for the months, and other-
wise advertise itself by gaudy upheavals. But
for merciless slaughter, proscription and con-
fiscation, the dealings of the early Anglo-
Saxon republicans with those of the race
who remained loyal to Britain, overshadowed
all the French records of the time of Robes-
pierre, Danton, Hebert, Collot and
Billaud. These bitterly hostile Anglo-
Saxons were strengthened later by a consid-
erable French and Spanish element, which
had no British racial affinities whatsoever.
Then there was gathered in such a vast mass
of Irish, all moving westward to escape from
British rule and British conditions, that
there is now more of Ireland in the States
than there is in Ireland itself. The German
was a great immigrant to the States for a
number of years, when poor old Freetrade
Germany was a good place to get away from;
and nobody suspects the German of any
desire to spill his blood in Britain’s quarrel
for sentimental motives. Also, there came
Scandinavians, Poles, Russians, Jew’s, Ital-
ians, Austrians, Bohemians and sundries.
Out of L 041,570 immigrants who entered
the United States in 1910 (the last year for
which figures are available), it is reckoned
that about 60,000 (say about one in 16)
were Anglo-Saxons, as the term goes—dis-
satisfied Anglo-Saxons, who didn’t think it
worth while to stay in Bull’s country and
defend it on the spot, and who will probably
feel even less interest in it, now that they
have left it, than they did before they left it.
And this sort of thing has been going on for
very many years. Certainly English re-
mains the official language of the U.S.
though the bulk of the population is no
longer of English descent. The Irishman,
when he arrived, spoke English of a sort,
and he was too busv hustling for a job as
policeman or alderman to demand the adop-
tion of his ancient speech at Washington.
And, among the babel of French, Spanish,
German, Swedish, Russian, Hebrew, Italian,
Polish, Ruthenian, Bohemian, Moravian,
Slovak. Servian, Croatian, Roumanian, Fin-
nish, Dutch, Greek, Flemish, Bulgarian,
Basque, Romany and oddments, no one non-
English language stood out at any time so
far beyond all others as to be able to assert
itself and compel official recognition. So the
hereditary tongue of a small minority pre-
vails, partly because it was first on the scene,
and partly because, among perhaps 30 lin-
guistic minorities, one must prevail. English
is taught in the schools, and is, in a manner
of speaking, holding its own. But as a
spoken language it isn’t exactly London Eng-
lish, and it is becoming less like the Picca-
dilly speech as time goes on.
* * * *
To put the matter shortly, the elements
which make up the population of the United
States are five-fold. There are (1) the descend-
ants of English, Scotch and Irish convicts,
who started with bitter grievances against
Britain —the country which sold them into
slavery; (2) the descendants of early colon-
ists who turned insurrectionists and devel-
oped traditions of furious hostility to Bri-
tain; (3) people, and the descendants of
people, like the Irish who quitted the Bri-
tish Isles full of rage and loathing; (4)
people, and the descendants of people, who
left the British Isles, not in active hostility,
but in disgust because they found those isles
not much ,of a country to live in; and (5)
the enormous mass of people from the Euro-
pean continent to whom Britain is of no
more real interest than is Egypt or Timbuc-
too. (Niggers and Chinese ai’e omitted from
the calculation on general principles.) These
are not very promising materials out of
which to create an Anglo-Saxon brotherhood.
They tend to blank indifference, to
the least. Then there is a circum-
stance which makes for active hostility.
Canada is the only British possession which
marches side by side with the home territory
of a Gi’eat Power. The United States is the
only Great Power which is cribbed and
■ cabined and confined by a mere thinly-
peopled, ill-defined, ill-defended foreign
colony on its home borders. Canada, to the
man of the United States who takes stock
in old Anglo-Saxon traditions, represents
failure, and is a constant reminder, not of
actual defeat, but of very modified success.
The revolutionists of Washington’s time
set to work to clear the British flag out of
America, and the work is not yet finished.
The Dominion is unredeemed Yankee land,
just as Lombardy and Venice used to be un-
redeemed Italy, and as Holland is unre-
deemed Germany. The fact that the non-
success of so many years ago has never been
forgotten is evidenced by the eagerness with
which the States reached out for Alaska
when Russia put it on the mai’ket, and the
enei’gy with which they pressed old and
dubious Russian claims until they secured
from the feeble British Government about
half the Pacific coast-line of Canada. Cana-
da, if it combines with the United States,
can develop westward without hindrance. If
it doesn’t combine with the United States
half its western territory is cut off from the
sea by an intervening strip of foreign land.
The Spanish American republics have been
able to forgive Spain because they won all
along the line, and their old misti’ess was
cleared out bag and baggage. The big North
American republic has never been able to
forgive Britain, because it only won half a
victory, and its old mistress didn’t wholly
clear out. Probably, among all the Great
Powers of the world. the U.S. republic is
the one that is least likely to be a reliable
friend to J. Bull at an emergency. It has
exactly the same grievance against him as
J. Bull had against the Boer States —or
rather, it has the same grievance as he would
have had against the Boer States if they
had been in the North of England instead of
in Africa, and Great Britain had been split
in two parts, kept asunder by a foreign
authority. And it is the only Great Power
which has any such grievance.
The Monroe Doctrine is an expression of
the fact that the United States considers
European Powers as undesirable neighbors.
It sets forth that the possession by a Euro-
pean Po\yer of any fragment of American
soil is so objectionable that any further
movement in that direction will be regarded
as a hostile act. The republic doesn’t want
any more European companionship, and
from that it may be inferred that it doesn’t
appreciate the European companionship it
already possesses. It has already taken steps
to shift the weakest of the objectionables by
shunting the Spaniard out of Cuba. The
Dutchman and Frenchman in Guiana,
and the British in Guiana, Honduras,
Canada and certain of the islands, are
still tolerated because the favorable op-
portunity for shifting them hasn’t arx’ived.
Among these neighbors Britain, as the
nearest and lai'gest objectionable, is the one
that weighs most heavily on the U.S. chest.
So the chances are that Bull’s difficulty
will be Jonathan’s opportunity.
THE SLUMP.
Last week the Sydney share market (which
in this respect is exactly the same as the
Melbouime and Adelaide share markets)
showed the same old downward tendency.
There is. very little change in the movement
nowadays. On Monday one stock showed a
slight rise, and two showed a drop. On
Tuesday one showed a rise of per share,
and eight stocks fell to the aggregate amount
of £1 11s. lid. The week showed that 11
securities had risen a little, while 33 had
fallen a good deal. And business is almost
dead. Yet the seasons are good, and the
prices of the produce which Australia has to
sell are high. Immigrants are poui'ing in.
Wages are bigger than they have ever been
before in the memory of anybody now living.
And even while stocks are sagging away so
miserably, the companies which they repre-
sent are generally making fine profits. It is
true that the country has been boi’rowing in
a lavish fashion, but for a long time it has
done most of its borrowings locally. The
hideous blunder of the late ’Bo’s and eaidy
’9o’s of last century, when “the importation
of British capital” became a madness, and
then the withdrawal of it produced the worst
crash in Australian history, has not been
repeated as yet. In fact, for some years
past, the total amount of Australian public
debt owing in England has actually de-
creased, and the outflow of money to pay in-
terest has decreased likewise. The State
revenues are higher than they ever were
before —which, by the way, is an old, familiar
symptom. In evei’y Australian State, just
before the great crash of 1893, the State
revenues were higher than they had ever
been before. Still, the sign may be a good
one, just as it may be a bad ohe, and, while
the joy lasts, it is a pleasant thing to know
that revenues are large, and that pi’ices are
lai’ge, and that wages are large, and that
crops are good ; and on the face of thinvs
everything in the garden of roses should
be lovely. Yet it certainly isn’t. As
already mentioned, signs of creeping paralysis
are visible in the share market. Other signs
reveal themselves in the desperate straits
to which these Governments that are enjoy-
ing the most splendid revenues on recoi’d ai’e
put to for money to keep their loan madness
going, and in the fact that they are buying
scraps of cash at prices that would have been
regarded as preposterous a very few years
ago. •
To put the matter shortly, a feeling arises
here and there that this country is get-
ting near to the edge of a precipice. Its
plain, simple, obvious trouble is lack of
money—the only kind of money which speaks
all languages and is equal to all emergencies.
The banking returns are a good test, for the
banks hold all the people’s money, except
such loose change as the community requires
to go on with. At June 30, 1911, these insti-
tutions contained £64,824,186 in cash (in-
cluding Australian notes), as security for
£212,439,058 due to the public. At last
June they had only £54,694,355 cash and
Australian notes as security for £212,560,856
due _to the public. It is the most sudden
decline ever known hereabouts in so short a
time. Already , the financial institutions are
selling securities in order to get in money,
and are refusing to make new loans, and put-
ting up loan rates, and offering higher prices
to attract deposits, and getting very few new
deposits even at the advanced rates, for lack
of money to be deposited. Very soon, if the
decline in the gold supply continues, they
will begin to call in advances, and then the
boom will sag horribly. Last year the banks
lent out an additional £14,000,000 or so at a
fairly cheap rate, to men who wanted to
build, to buy land, and to start new enter-
prises. If they have to stop doing this sort
of thing, then the bottom falls out.
If anyone asks where the money lias gone,
the obvious answer is that it has gone to
pay for imports. This country has reached
such a point in the importing mania that
during the first eight months of 1912 it
bought from abroad £13,870,000 worth more
goods than it scld abroad, and it exported
all the gold that it produced, and a great
deal of what it Lad on hand, and a lot more
that it borrowed, to make up the difference.
In 1905, the average wages paid in N.S.W.
factories (men, women, boys and girls all
included) were barely £72 per head. In
1911, despite a great increase in the propor-
tion of comparatively low-wage female labor,
the average was well above £92. There has
been no corresponding increase in the tariff,
and the duties which held a reasonably fair
.balance between an English worker at £4O
and a N.S.W. one at £72 don’t hold any kind
of balance between an English worker at
£4O and a N.S.W. one at £92. Yet Minister
Tudor has the silly effrontery to ask, when
applied to for increased duties: “If we give
the manufacturers increased Protection, what
are the workers going to get?” The obvious
fact that the workers have very considerably
“got” already is consistently ignored by
Tudor and his colleagues. And the output
of Australian goods is severely hampered in
another direction. It is now the amazing
policy of the unions in many trades to so
limit the number of apprentices that (bar-
ring the importation of workmen from over-
sea, to which many unionists strongly object)
Australian industries are not only forbidden
to, expand, but are doomed to gradual ex-
tinction for want of skilled operatives.
So, partly because Australian labor is too
dear to compete, under a mockery of a tariff,
with English cheap labor, and partly be-
cause, in many industries, Australian work-
men are too scarce to supply the goods re-
quired, the huge inflow of cheap labor pro-
ducts continues, and the corresponding out-
flow of Australian gold continues, and
through sheer local scarcity of cash the
Slump grows day by day. The rich Tory
importer is doing remarkably well, but that
is poor consolation. If events keep moving
along the present lines, and no new Kal-
goorlie or similar unforeseen circumstance
is discovered to give an artificial boost to the
situation, it is safe to prophesy that shortly
the Laborite who sunk the fiscal issue will
find work at the present rate of wages grow-
ing scarce, and that the rich Tory who voted
for Freetrade because it was English and
respectable, will find his lands and securities
worth a great deal less than they are now.
Already investment stocks that are worth
the price they commanded three months ago
are about as rare as hens’ teeth. Even such
tremendously solid securities as the shares
of the Bank of N.S.W. and the Commercial
Ranking. Co. of Svdney are on the down
grade, simply through the desire to realise
caused by the scarcity of money—in other
words, through the vanishment of the money
that has gone away to pay for cheap labor
goods. And many stout gentlemen, with a
life-long faith in the importation of cheap
goods, feel a good deal cheaper in conse-
quence.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Wanted, Government Audit of Life
Assurance Offices.
If the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society loses the number of its mess (and
such a thing, though improbable enough, is
not wholly impossible), or if it suffers so
severely in repute through its present
troubles that it will be years before it re-
gains its old standing (which is quite pro-
bable), the case for Government audit and
valuation of life offices will be greatly
strengthened. Private Enterprise always
falls back on the State, if the thing be at all
possible, in an emergency, but where State
audit and valuation and supervision are pro-
vided as a regular thing, it can do this quietly
and as a matter of course, and no scandal en-
sues. When they are not provided for, and
a Bank of New Zealand or a Colonial
Mutual Life Society rushes, or is dragged, be-
fore Parliament, amid suspicions and re-
criminations, there is a scandal, and scandal
is bad for business. It is fully 15 years
since people began to remark that the
Colonial Mutual didn’t look quite so well as
some of its contemporaries. The large
amount of uncollected interest upon invest-
ments was mentioned in this paper before
the end of last century. Attention was
drawn now and again to the high expense
rate, the very moderate rate of interest rea-
lised, the enormous number of branch offices
that the society maintained in order to scrape
together a not-very-extraordinary amount of
business, and so on. Where there is no
regular State audit and State valuation, the-
only thing a financial critic can do is to draw
attention to these things. He can’t ask the
State auditor for an explanation of anv
points that seem doubtful in the office s ac-
counts, for there is no such person. He can
ask the institution, but then no one seriously
expects an institution to say it isn’t feeling
well. As for the society itself, it can hire
special auditors to go through its accounts,
and refute any charges and suspicions: but


the mere fact that it selects and pays the
special auditors makes the public reva
them with a dubious eye. And if it desires
to do something extra vigorous, it may de-
mand a Government investigation, which is
equivalent to saying that it is under a cloud,
and must take unusual means to get that
cloud dissipated. And if the Government re-
fuses to go out of its way and interfere, then
the cloud remains—in fact, it is a bigger
cloud than ever, because it has been so widely
advertised—and people may even suspect
that the application for an overhaul was
bogus, and was refused by previous arange-
ment. The case of the Colonial Mutual Life
Society has been dragged, all of a sudden,
into publicity in the Victorian State Legis-
lature. It is alleged that, owing to some
initial blunder in the constitution of the
society, it is really a proprietary affair, and
that the position of the directors has
always been dubious. It is also alleged that
the institution has sundry valueless assets
in its balance-sheet, and that the directors
have resorted to unexpected means to pre-
vent the intrusion of new men—-men who
might possibly overhaul the alleged office
skeleton, and drag its alleged bones to light.
If the charges are proved the result will be
very bad for the policy-holders. If they are
not proved, they will be very bad for the
policy-holders, because the memory of the
suspicion will remain. In a country where
any respectable sort of Government holds
an annual audit and valuation of life assur-
ance offices, troubles of this sort can’t ac-
cumulate. If there is a trouble it is found
out within 12 months—before it he° time to
become a very bad affair, and while it is
still easy to apply a remedy, and to do it
quietly and without scandal. There is no
need for a frantic Premier to bring in a Bill
to abolish the old directorate, and to appoint
a new one, and to upheave things generally.
It is quite possible that this affair of the
Colonial Mutual may be only the first of a
series of alarms and suspicions. Therefore,
it is time that the Australian Government,
which has charge of insurance legislation,
became upstanding on its feet and did things.
The matter may not be so popular and easy
as the Baby Bonus, but it is quite as im-
portant. The £5 the baby gets at birth is
very little compared with the amount it may
not get at the old man’s death, if his policy
proves rotten.

Some Studies in Provincialism.
In the misty future, when the Navigation
Bill gets into committee, F. W. Bamford, of
Queensland, proposes to move that Papua be
excluded from the coasting trade provisions.
Or, to put it in another way, he is going to
urge that those vessels plying to Australia’s
island possession shall be allowed to carry
nigger crews. This attempt to slay a great
ideal (for it should be understood that, if
exemptions are granted in one case they must
be granted in others) will be made in the
alleged interests of a mere handful of whites.
According to the annual official report, the
whole of the European population of Papua,
as at June 30, 1911, was but 1032. Of these
775 were males. About 10 per cent, of the
latter are planters, who travel little ; another
10 per cent, are miners, who travel less ; and
then there is a big crowd of operatives, ship-
wrights, plumbers, hotelkeepers, etc., who'
trayel hardly at all. On behalf of the infini-
tesimally small residue —the Melbourne Club
type of settler, and the Hotel Australia
variety of pioneer— Bamford is willing and
anxious, seemingly, to bury Australia’s best
hope of an effective mercantile marine in the
blue sea. He does not stand alone in this
respect. On every hand members of the Na-
tional Parliament are to be discerned bat-
tling like tigers to emasculate the Navigation
Bill for the benefit of the pettiest imaginable
local interests. The Westralian representa-
tive is hostile, in many cases, to the Coasting
Trade provisions, because he fears that they
may discourage the big oversea liners, which
are, under existing conditions, a convenience;
to those of his constituents who visit the
Eastern States. The Tasmanian fears that
his State’s apple-exporting trade may suffer,
if the foreign shipping cos. are asked to em-
ploy white in place of black labor. Also, he
dreads the effect on the Tasmanian tourist
traffic, of the possible cutting out of Hobart
from the itineraries of the deep-sea liners.
The Victorian, and more particularly the
Victorian who inhabits Melbourne, is per-
turbed lest the new Bill may have the effect
of alienating the affections of the Dutch
craft which ply there at present. It is a
matter of inter-State, or, rather, inter-city,
jealousy. All the Island trade, with the excep-
tion of that done by the one Dutch line which
extends to Melbourne, is in the hands of
Sydney. To retain that twopenny boon, nine
“public-spirited” Melbournians out of ten are
prepared to let the question of a white ocean,
so far as these shores are concerned, go to
blazes. It is a miserable position at best,
and it hasn’t even expediency to justify it.
Papua is not going to be isolated, even if
the worst befalls, and the ships which at
present trade there refuse to go white. No-
thing is surer than that the Australian Gov-
ernment will be able easily to arrange ser-
vices of white-manned steamships, either by
subsidies or by inaugurating a nationalised
line. Tasmania’s apple shipments will not
be prejudiced, whichever road the coasting
trade provisions go; and, as regards the
tourist traffic, it is likelier that a shortage of
foreign liners will operate to work up a big
local passenger-carrying trade, with all its
attendant advantages, than that it will kill
the existing vogue Tasmania has, as a holi-
day resort. But the typical provincial]st and
the furious State Righter see .no'ne of these
things. The light or faint glimmer ahead
on which their eyes are tirelessly fixed, is
compounded of about three ha’porth of pres-
ent trade, and somewhere in the neighborhood
of another three ha’porth of jealousy con-
cerning the various “rival States.” To, keep
the trade and exasperate the rival States,
numbers of these patriots are prepared to
go any lengths, from prejudicing their coun-
try’s defence scheme to handing the Com-
monwealth over, lock, stock and barrel, to
discolored aliens.
More Wild Tales from the Churches.
“Why don’t the Masses frequent the
Churches?” clamors the Right-Thinking Per-
son every now and then, in tones of pained
stupefaction. On recent clerical form the
answer would seem to be that the more the
Masses are educated—the more they grow
up in an intellectual manner of speaking—
the less taste they have for crude fairy tales.
The modern parson seems to be developing
into an absolute specialist in the matter of
incredible bellowdrama. When he isn’t find-
ing glittering, but unspecified, public-houses
full of small, inebriated boys, or unnamed
haunts where anonymous maiignants machin-
ate ceaselessly to overturn Throne, Faith
and Marriage Tie, he is mostly putting his
shifty finger on some other highly-elusive
social horror which no one but himself is
able to locate. The average member of the
Masses finds it tiresome to be habitually de-
feated in the hunt for even so unsavory a
thing as a social horror. When suspicion be-
gins to dawn that the horror was never
there in the first instance, weariness turns
to annoyance, and the divine who has, as it
were, fathered the unpleasantness loses
caste, and, in consequence, . congregations.
The latest sample of holy sensationalism
emanates from Rev. S. D. Yarrington, of
Sydney, who claimed last week that the citv
was creeping with opium dens or “hells,”
in which white girls “endured a living
death.” The position he took up over the
affair was typically clerical. In the first
place, he asked for money wherewith to carry
the Word to the opium-enthusiasts. On its
being represented to him, by the Chief Secre-
tary and the Police, that a better plan would
be to indicate the hells, with a view to giv-
ing the authorities a chance to wipe them
out, he was shocked. If he did such a
thing, he explained, and ceased to play the
role of particeps criminis, the malefactors
would never trust him again, and his mis-
sionising efforts would be nullified so far as
they were concerned. In any case, it would
be impossible to exterminate all the dens or
remove the girls from them. In brief, the
only way to deal with the tragedy was to
supply the Mission people with funds, and
leave them to wrestle in prayer with the
aliens and their Caucasian fellow-sinners. It
is difficult to conceive a more imbecile atti-
tude than this. Rev. Yarrington has been
toiling in the slums, Bible in hand, for
years now. By his own admission, they are
full of ruined white girls, whose downfall
was brought about originally by o"bium smok-
ing. According to him, the Chinese who
wishes to compass the destruction of a white
woman first makes her an opium fiend —and
that part of the story seems likely enough.
Furthermore, the deadly work is still going
on. That is to say, weekly and daily, more
girls are being lured to Chinese dens, more of
them are being initiated into the opium habit,
and more of them are qualifying as consorts
for the Mongol. And the only remedy the
Rev. Yarrington has to suggeit is a free
use of the Bible and the hymn-book and
prayer—moral prophylactics which, by his
own showing, have done little good in _ the
past. What wonder that the authorities
have turned the eye of doubt on the mis-
sioniser’s allegations, and have charged the
good man with the fault of allowing his
imagination to grow super-heated? There
are some abuses which, it is almost univer-
sally conceded, cannot be satisfactorily at-
tacked by means of prayer. For example,
disorderly houses, the plague, gaming estab-
lishments, diphtheria, two-up schools, sly-
grog shops, coiners’ dens, and bad drains.
A parson might contend in prayer with such
calamities till he achieved lingual paralysis,
and never exorcise one smell or turn one
soul from Beelzebub unto repentance. The
police, health officials, etc., by cleaning up
these evils, do a tremendous lot of real good.
And so with opium dens. If, as Rev. Yah-
rington asserts, the latter are, so to speak,
universities of the most hideous forms of
vice, establishments where clean white
women graduate into drug-fiends and para-
mours of coolies, their utter extermination
ought not to be delayed for a second. They
should be burst into hurriedly and dyna-
mited, and the plough drawn over their sin-
ful and insanitary ruins, and. the sites
should be sown with salt. The idea of pre-
serving them as fields for the exercise of
prayer by the representatives of the Churches
is too bizarre for the most- demented of
farces. The N. S. Wales Chief Secretary
and the Inspector-General of Police really
did Rev. Yarrington a kindness when they
ciuestioned the accuracy of his amazing tale.
To know of such horrors, and to refuse to
disclose their whereabouts to those best fitted
to smite them, would be infamous: to prattle
about them on the strength of hearsay evi-
dence, or less, is merely parsonical, and
darned parsonical at that.
The Loyal Irish Rebellion.
This letter is from a Scotchman: —
The time is at last ripe and has arrived when
Ulster rises to strike a blow, thwack or other bump
for loyalty, arid the memory of the glorious, pious
and immortal person from Holland, who fixed things
to the satisfaction of the Scotch-Irishmen around
Belfast. Also the said bump has already been de-
scribed in the cables, and sounds about as terrible as
the noise of the fall of a potato from Heaven on a
still night. This is how the fearful news arrived in
Sydney -
“The members of one Unionist club, carrying
dummy rifles, and those of another club wearing
khaki ’ uniforms, paraded the streets of Lisburn (7i
miles south-west of Belfast), where Sir Edward Car-
son addressed a vast open-air meeting yesterday.
The Irish Unionist leader read the covenant, and de-
clared that if the Government persisted in its fatuous
and nefarious policy he and those associated with
him would go to any length in the defence of Ire-
land.”
As the beginning of an armed protest against the'
long-promised Home Rule, those dummy rifles and
khaki uniforms are much, more cheerful than might
have reasonably been expected, and the main feature
of Carson's address appears to have been the absence
of the whoop and assurance which have characterised
his 6000 or 7000 addresses on the. same subject, re-
cently delivered in the same district.
There is a good about movement
that is remarkable, it isn’t an
Ulster movement, some parts
of Ulster of some of the general
scheme of things, deal. To
assert that it is _a mov3WHB F Protestant against
Catholic is to juggle with truth and veracity, and
fair statement arid reality, and hard solid things of
that kind. The bulk of Ireland’»insurrectionary leaders
Were more or less Protestant; the last great Irish re-
bellion was the work of Protestants in one of the
most Protestant counties, and the Home Rule move-
ment to-day has- the 1 ’ support of thousands of Protest-
ants, who in faith and practice are much nearer the
genuine article than the noisy Orangemen who wear
khaki uniforms and carry dummy rifles. Nominally,
the Orangeman is strong on upholding the Bible, but
the upholding' is mostly done with a fife-and-drum
band and copious draughts of bad whisky, and ex-
presses itself in the simple formula, “to Hell with
the Pope.” As long as the Roman person is cursed
sufficiently often, and with plenty of vigor, there
seems to be a general agreement that the Bible and
the cause and the country will be all right.
People who say that the present Irish commotion
is a race war are also strangers to the thing
Ananias didn’t regularly use. Recent writers declare
that the most Celtic part of Ireland, and, indeed, of
the whole British Islands, is just that very region
around Belfast and Londonderry where the dummy
rifles are in evidence. Celts went to Scotland as
members of the old Irish Church; stayed there as
Catholics, and came back to Ireland as Scotchbv-
terians of the John llnox brand; but they hadn’t
changed their identity, or their race, or their drink,
or the color of their whiskers to any remarkable ex-
tent. The Irish people certainly are not all the pro-
geny of one patriarchal father, but they are just
about as uniform in breed as a race need be, and
there is nothing which will help one end of the
country along without being good for the other:
Anyhow, the betterment of Ireland isn’t a matter
of religion or race, or any of the noisy and senti-
mental things that help to make most news copy.
What Ireland lias been suffering from is the absence
of industries, the drain on its wealth caused by ab-
sentee landlordism, and the rule of an unsympathetic
foreign Government, which never tried in the least to
understand what it wanted or needed. More or less
Bible, or Carson, or khaki uniforms, or dummy rifles,
or fife-and-drum bands, or recollections of King Wil-
liam or Napper Tandy, won’t help it forward or
backward the value of one blighted potato. What it
wants is more food, more clothes, better houses, more
mills, more soap, more hope, less talking, a thump-
ing graduated land tax, and —but that will probably
happen in the millennium —a thoroughgoing Protec-
tion for its manufactures which would lift it to a
status of real industrial activity. It is the poscihil-
ity of these things that the sanest and best- of its
people see in the promise of Home Rule, and Car-
son’s armed rowdies are not likely to scare them out
of their hope by any number of dummy rifles.
Joy for the Bush Pressman.
Dear Bulletin, —Do you think there is any
hope of Section 21 of the N. S. Wales Early
Closing Act (1912) surviving the slings and
arrows of enraged Fatte? ' It runs this way:
No person shall, in the metropolitan or Newcastle
shopping district, cart or deliver by vehicle, or permit
any other person in his employment to cart or deliver
by vehicle, any goods, wares or merchandise, or
materials whatsoever, before half-past seven o’clock
in the morning, or after half-past seven o’clock in
the evening on any Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday or
Thursday, or before half-past seven o’clock in the
morning, nor after half-past nine o’clock in the even-
ing on Friday, or before half-past seven o’clock in
the morning,' or after half-past one o’clock in the
afternoon, on Saturday of any week.
If this goes through, I shall fall deliriously
over my own feet in a wild rush to buy a
certain country daily which is wilting, at
present, .from no other cause than the com-
petition of the metropolitan sheets. And
presently you will hear of your old friend,
no longer as a skulking insolvent, but as a
species of backblocks Northchffe or
I-learst. For if no on,e is permitted to cart
anything before half-past seven in the morn-
ipf or’ after half-past seven in the evening,
the Sydney morning dailies which now leave
for the country at 3 a.m. or thereabouts, are
obviously doomed so far as their back :
blocks business is concerned. What a
superb vista it opens up for the rural or
Cow pressman. Affluence and ink-
stains walking hand in hand through the
country districts for the first time in our
rough island story! Western newspaper
managers proudly rejecting the local under-
taker’s advt. and simultaneously bumping up
the charge for adulating the pub and
eulogising the craft ,of the village drains-
expert by 50 per cent.! The bank manager
and the editor taking beer together, as
equals! The highly-salaried reporters elected
to the golf club and heartily tendered the
mitt of friendship by the indigenous nobility!
It all seems so much too good to be true
that I intend to delay hypothecating the few
valuables I possess (as a preliminary to in-
vesting in tlie newspaper concern above-
mentioned) till I see what the deliberations
of Parliament bring forth. Meanwhile. I
remain, in a state of absolutely blithering ex-
citement, yours ever, A. William-Cann.
The Sorrows of Mrs. Hughes.
The Argus recently babbled a column wail
from Mrs. Hughes, the president of the Aus-
tralian Women’s National League. The bur-
den of Mrs. Hughes’s lament is the perverse-
ness of Sam Mauger, one of the three
selected Victorian Liberal candidates for the
Senate. Sam persists in regarding himself
as selected, and is stumping the country
with great energy, although Mrs. Hughes
declares that her league cannot stand him.
The reasons Mrs. Hugh'es give for her dis-
like of Mauger are the following:—
(1) His polities are, as his friends tell us, of a
decidedly radical brand.
(2) On one occasion he voted for preference to
unionists.
(3) He finds “there is no room in Australia for in-
dividualism.”
(4) He actively supports a policy of Protection.
Apparently reason No. 4 is the chief one, as
Mrs. Hughes plaintively asks why Samuel
cannot agree with the league’s plank, which
runs:—
To give support to the present fiscal policy with
DUE regard to all sections of the community, and to
support the establishment of a Board of Trade for its
adjustment on PRACTICAL lines.
This, says Mrs. Hughes artlessly, is “clear.”
It is clear in one sense, that is, it means
anything or nothing. What is the “present
fiscal policy?” Also, what are “practical
lines”? Quite the best feature of Mauger’s
candidature is the fact that he is a staunch
Protectionist, who will fight to secure duties
that are efficient; and this, it seems certain,
is the cause of Mrs. Hughes’s anguish.
What will happen with regard to the Liberal
candidates no one knows. The two leagues
that are against Mauger chose Peacock in-
stead, without asking Peacock whether he
would accept the nomination. Peacock, evi-
dently not in love with the honor, promptly
declined it; and the leagues retired crest-
fallen, amidst the jeers of their enemies.
Mrs. Hughes’s long lamentation closes with-
out any indication of what her precious
league intends V do, so the momentous ques-
tion is still hanging where Mahomet was
buried.
