The Bogus Leader.
It is the custom of N. S. Wales Laborites
to condole hilariously with Liberalism over
its Wade. The flamboyantly virtuous, but
depressing and maladroit and humorless
Mount Kembla-ite is pointed to b - " State
Labor enthusiasts as their Party’s White Hope,
so to speak. It is claimed in this quarter that
he possesses a dazzling variety of lethal
punches—his habit of posing as the mar-
tyred victim of Speaker Willis ; his unique
gift of saying the wrong thing at the most
inopportune time possible; his genius for
perpetrating tactical blunders, and so on —-
and that all of them are employed constantly
for the benefit of the Labor cause. Labor
Ministers are understood to mention Wade
in their pravers, beseeching Providence that
he may be spared for many years to direct
the forces opposed to them. There is, no
doubt, a lot in it. Labor in N. S. Wales
could hardly have a more satisfactory char-
acter in the battle-van of Liberalism than
C. G. Wade, unless, indeed, it were Wood
or Fitzpatrick, or the gentleman who at-
tempted, the other day, to hit the Speaker
with a bottle, or his friend who aims books
and such-like missiles at the foe in moments
of controversial stress. But is the N.S.W.
State Labor party any better off in this re-
gard? Palpably not. Considered from the
standpoint of a leader, James McGowen is
fully as calamitous to Labor as G. G.
Wade is to Liberalism. In fact, it is argu-
able whether he isn’t more so. He ds
equally dull, equally inarticulate, equally in-
capable of enthusing anybody over any
single issue ; and he also resembles Wade in
the matter of congenital Toryism and hope-
less want of imagination. He lacks Wade’s
GuMMiDGE-like attitude of misery and an-
guish as regards almost every fresh political
development; in fact, he is a notably cheery
and optimistic old party, as beseems one
whose conscience, digestive apparatus and
bank balance are all in good order. But he
makes up for. these pleasing qualities by
being the most incorrigibly tired citizen in
politics to-day. He is the John Weary of
N. S. Wales, only more so. Nominally he i*s
Premier and Chief Secretary; in point of
fact he is a splendid and glittering loafer
and the State’s most convincing example of
a politician who has lost his Dash. When he
was Treasurer. Carmichael was not only
the entire financial voice of the Cabinet,
but, to all intents and purposes,, the Premier
to boot. Now that he is Chief Secretary,
Flowers, M.L.C., evolves hospital schemes,
attends to social reform matters, grapples
with police grievances, and in general
attends to the work which his leader
should be doing. The problem of who
is really Premier is one of the Ma
State’s darkest mysteries just now. When
the Federal Treasurer has to be conferred
with, Carmichael undertakes the job. When
it is decided to put through the Increase of
Salaries Bill, Holman is called on. When
the matter of the Jervis Bay railway arises,
Beeby is handed control of the fort. A
Hansard proof of Orders of the Day
(17/9/’l2) is before the writer. It dis-
closes the fact that, while on that date
Holman had charge of a couple of dozen
measures, Beeby of six, Griffith of five,
Carmichael of four, and so on, the Premier’s
part was the handling of two unimportant
amending Bills. Which is fairly typical,,
and likewise absurd. McGowen is a dull,,
estimable man personally, but as a leader he
begins to assume the dimensions of a colossal
jest—an even worse one, as aforesaid, than
C. G. Wade himself. He corresponds to an
editor who does not edit; a hotelkeeper who
never goes near his pub; a shipmaster
whose days and nights are spent on dry land..
If the State Labor party doesn’t want more
dissensions that have yet ag tated it, and if
it doesn’t yearn for more discredit than has
hitherto smitten the cause, it will kindly but
very firmly and promptly jettison McGowen.
the leader who has ceased to make even a*
faint pretence to lead.
The British Way.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No politician who is not blinded by prejudice will
refuse to admit that Britain is a better guide to
follow where politics and journalism are concerned,
than Australia. —Maoriland paper.
Let us take the British way,
Follow where our fathers led,
Though the years are somewhat grey
And old systems somewhat dead;
Let our fashions all be set
As they used to be observed
When the strong Plantagenet
Mostly got what he deserved.
Journalists should be the same,
Politicians should not range,
Let us simply play the game
Without any thought of change;
Lion-hearted Richard knew
Naught of graduated tax
When old Cohen’s teeth he drew
To provide his battle-axe.
John the Magna Charta signed
By compulsion, it is true,
And he didn’t seem to mind
If the whole blamed public knew •
But the leaders in the press
Were anonymous as cows
In the days when knights would dress
As we clothe our stoves and ploughs.
In the days when, clad in steel,
Foemen met in deadly shock,
With a spike upon each heel
Urging on the battle-crock,
He who published any news
Did it with a conscience clear —
If the fightinr person’s views
Were his own, he need not fear
Splendid pageants came and went,
Leprosy was somewhat rife
People mostly paid their rent
With a dagger or a knife,
Splendid whiskers filled the land,
And from justice hot and raw
Kings administered by hand
Slabs of hard unquestioned law.
In Australia no one cares
Much about the ancient way,
There the scarcity of prayers
Gives the Wowser thino-s to say ;
They have grown un-British quite,
Wandered from the ancient path,
And a number take delight
In the wet and modern bath.
One tradition have they kept,
One that fills our souls with fear,
Ancient as the Saxon Hept-
Archy is their love of beer ;
But they have the awful hide
To expunge from off the slate
All their reverential pride
In the ages that were great.
Shall we follow where they lead?
No, by Cohen’s whiskers, no !
Let us go at British speed
If we really need to go.
Norman William has been dead,
Very dead for quite a while.
Ours to mark the thing he said —-
Backward march! And try to smile.
N.S.W.
PAT O'MAORI.

Re Suffering Woman.
Deab Bulletin, —The arrogant misconduct
of the suffragettes in Britain has brought
about a complete exposure of the “inequality
of the Sexes” fiction which is even now the
stock-in-trade of the hard-trodden sistern
from a platform. The heaviest Parliamentary
weapon that woman has been provided with
in Britain is the Married Women’s Act of
1895, which enables her to get separation and
maintenance and legal custody of the chil-
dren from a husband who assaults her, under
maddening provocation or otherwise. If an
eternal diet of “tongue pie” drives a hus-
band from his home he can be sued for
maintenance. He can get a divorce from his
wife only on the ground of adultery, and
even then he may have to provide her with
means of support. He can get a ffigal separ-
ation only on the ground of her habitual
drunkenness, and the loss of her unsober
society leaves him still liable for payment of
her board and beer money until she drinks
herself to death. On the other hand, a wife
can forsake her home and kids with im-
punity. If she is giddily inclined, she can
pick a quarrel witli her moderately disre-
spectable hubby and go and live with mother,
or abide with her late lodger’s convenient
sister, at the expense of the husband she has
chucked up. If she possesses money of her
own, she may pledge her husband’s credit
for “necessaries,” whereas he cannot pledge
hers. Though she have £lO,OOO a year in
her own right she need pay nothing for
maintenance or education of her children, as
long as she has a husband who can be sued
for the cost thereof. Her husband is civilly
liable for her tortious acts, such as libel,
lie is liable in civil law for any damage she
may inflidt on the maid-servant within his
gates, or on the horse or ass she hires from
a livery stable, although his income may be
small and hers may be large. Woman is
now “the spoilt child of the Law,” and all
the hard-faced champions of their mostly un-
reflective sex are well aware of the fact.
Hence their arrogance. Single or married,
woman is treated as though she were
always the injured party when she takes
action against a man, and as though she
were much to be pitied when he takes action
against her. A youth may be punished for
seducing a girl many years older than him-
self. When she is convicted of a crime
against the Law, the Law shows her a con-
sideration which no man could hope for.
Also, society shows her as much considera-
tion. The woman caught thieving in a
drapery store is rarely prosecuted, and when
prosecuted she is likely to be discharged as a
kleptomaniac. Dishonest servant girls are
privileged malefactors in comparison -with
dishonest men servants. The man caught
stealing a hammer from an ironmonger is
prosecuted and sentenced to six months. Sen-
tences of imprisonment passed on women who
insist on being inexcusable are light as com-
pared with sentences passed on men, and
when they get into prison the same old con-
cession to'their unfair sex is granted again.
In fact, all the Right—apart from the pre-
cious Vote —that modern woman has been
denied in English-speaking countries is the
right to know exactly how she stands in the
scheme of civilisation. She ought to have
had her position explained to her at school.
Just now she is having some wholesome
truths hurled at her by the newspapers, and,
what is of more importance, her male rela-
tions are beginning to feel strange yearnings
for the very Equality which the female agi-
tators would be darned sorry, to get.—Yours,
etc., O.K.
“Tangita Ivino” : The party to which Pre-
mier Massey belongs once staked its politi-
cal shirt in opposition to land taxation. It
fought hard for the old Propertv Tax, and
having lost the battle, went down for' 20
years. At last it has come back to office,
and one of the first things it has done is to
propose an increase in the graduated Land
Tax. This goes to show that democracy does
get forward a little bit sometimes. Even a
Tory party with a majority can’t afford to
be too reactionary Massey wants cash
badly. The graduated tax will bring him
some. He also wants to look as if he meant
business. The graduated tax looks remark-
ably like business. Of course, it isn’t, but
what’s the odds, as long as John Smith
thinks it is? A person with £lO,BOO, taxable
balance, will pav £9 instead of £B, and a
person with £200,000, taxable balance, will
pay £OO6l instead of £"5000. Which looks
like a great deal of extra money, but isn’t
in reality much more than if the present
writer paid 4d. instead of 3d. for every 65th
beer he consumed.
The overcrowded and financially-embar-
rassed hospitals of Sydney, which live as
best they can on doles, cadges, hospital Sat-
urdays and such like, got another bad ad-
vertisement last week, when a girl named
Daisy Hallam cut things short at the age of
21. She was a chronic sufferer, just bad
enough to be constantly miserable,' and yet
not so bad that a hospital bed could
be spared for her while worse cases were
waiting. She was a working girl, and
couldn’t afford the expense of a pri-
vate sanatorium. So she applied at the
cemetery to see if a bed was vacant there,
and for once her luck was in. Quite a com-
modious, subterranean four-poster with grass
curtains happened to be available.

“The Woman,” now at Sydney Royal, is
the reverse side of the “Get-Rich-Quick
Wallingford” gold brick. And it looks more
like real life than the Criterion idyll of the
two Fiji nephews who accidentally made
simple Battlesburg with their covered carpet
tacks. The bowelless politicians of the
Royal production might have been boiled
down by the Self-Made Merchant, and put
on the market as ITime Washington Lard.
In a word, they are genuine and substantial
hogs. William C. De Mille’s stage story
could be told in two acts; but the curtain
falls like the guillotine on the cross-examin-
ation of the stubborn little telephone girl
who knows All, only to rise again on ex-
actly the same scene. The interval, of
course, is useful for drinking purposes. The
play is elaborately woven round a strong
central idea — -i.e., that the boss grafter’s
daughter (who is also his trusted lieutenant’s
wife) _is the unknown woman with whom
Standish, his well-hated political opponent,
was indiscreet in the wild young days. The
fiercely moral and, therefore, dangerous
Standish is on the eve of attaining power,
and the desperate grafters have laboriously
"athered up all the fuses of his explosive
past save the name of his lady companion.
Knowing that his first instinctive move will
be to warn her of the impending danger, the
ruthless old politician bluntly tells him the
position, and leaves him, reiving on the
’phene girl, at whose elbow he'has bellowed
his villainy, to later supply him with the

marry her after “the honeymoon that never
came through the Custom House,” and that
she refused. He, therefore, considers that
he has a right to go ahead. And go he does,
with great success, while the grafters bully
the loyal litle ’phone girl and stumble slowly
towards the unpublishable truth. The com-
nany is uniformly good. Hilda Spong has a
trying hysterical role as the compromised
woman; but she acts throughout with
charm, and handles well the scene in which
she tries to bluff the shrinking ’phone girl,
who has ventured to come and warn her of
her danger. Florence Gleeson is heavily
burdened with the part of Wanda Kelly,
the hello lady; but it is full of opportunities,
and the clever little artiste doesn’t miss one.
She has the centre of the stage when the
curtain rises, and she is there alone when it
falls. William Desmond, as the grafting
Hon. Jim Blake, supplies an impressive
sketch of an experienced and unprincipled
political cigar furnace; and Harcourt
Beatty, with his brisk style ”nd rasping
voice, is happilv placed as Mark Robertson,
his lieutenant and son-in-law. Sidney Stir-
ling rather lacks animation as the naturally
unheroic Standish. His polished and dis-
tinctively English style, too, is rather a han-
dicap ; but his work is clear-cut throughout.
Three middle distance characters are very
capably filled by Tlios. Lloyd (Ralph Van
Dyke), Robert Whyte, jun. (Hon. Silas
Gregg), and Herbert Leigh (Hon. Jim Neli-
gan). There is much “hon.” in the play,
even if there isn’t much honor.
“The Girl from Waybaek” is once more
at Sydney Palace in place of “What Women
Suffer.” The management allege that “The
Girl” has returned for the Spring horserac-
ing. It seems a reasonable excuse.
At the head of the National Amphitheatre
bill are the trick cyclists, singers and dan-
cers, who elect to be known collectively as
“Les Bow.” Why “Les Bow” this paper
doesn’t grasp, any more than it compre-
hends the programme’s references to their
“dainty Parisienne dances and songs.” Music-
hall French seems to be getting more bizarre
even than menu French, and that has been
recognised universally, for years, to be fully
as weird as M.L.A.’s English. It is even
worse than the language of Chaucer’s best
girl, who spoke good French “aftfer the scole
of Stratford-atte-Bowe,” because she had
never met French as it. was spoken at Paris.
The Jourdane Quartette, and Corrie and
Baker continue to elude the decayed vege-
table of the hostile critic with the ease of
accomplished artists. Hamlin and Mack
and The Serenaders are doing their swan
song-and-dance, amid loud applause and
general grief. Miss Beaumont Collins and
her co. have revived their one-act comedy
sketch, “Bedfellows.” That is to say, the
management terms it a comedy. After look-
ing the word up in Webster the writer de-
clines to commit himself in the matter.
The wearisome Tivoli first-part is dying
a natural death. The old order is changing,
and now two or three big turns are in-
cluded. In the near future, proprietor
Mclntosh intends to renovate the part alto-
gether, and put it on an equal footing with
the more popular Part 2. There is quite an
army of newcomers this week, including
Nella Webb, a pretty Yankee-French sou-
brette; Ronald George, an instrumentalist of
some tonnage; Alberti Marini, the Quinlan
grand opera tenor; Taylor and Arnold, a
pair of Yankee comedians full of nonsense;
and comedians Blake and Granby. The best
of the bunch is NelD Webb, whose items,
half-sung, half-spoken in a pretty twang and
a slight French accent, catch on wonder-
fully. The De Serris troupe put their stage
clothes in their purse on Monday night, and
will appear no more in Sydney. Among
others present are La Freya, the French
artist, who dresses in a smile and a colored
light, the Three Sisters Macarte, and the
Rallis and Wilson Trio.
Zigomar has been holding the fort for
Spencer’s this week with “The Petrol
Pirates,” or “The Deeds of the Daring Des-
peradoes.” This is another of the detective
films, with their headquarters in France,
wherein, until the last 50ft., the detectives
look like being beaten on points, but at the
last 2Jft. win on a knockout. This picture’s
chief feature is an exciting motor-car chase,
Police v. the P.P’s., or D.D’s. “The Funeral
of the late General Booth” wanders slowly
across the screen, to the accompaniment of
many weeps and sobs from the Salv’army.
The Funniest Man Competition is in its
second stages, and, so far, there is no rumor
that the Comic Australian is leading bv
three lengths.
“The Queen of the Redskins” is still at
home at the Adelpln, Sydney. At the time
of writing, there is no mention of how long
her Majesty will remain in residence there.
Possibly she will stay until the supply of
black paint and burnt cork gives out.

This scribe begins to wish that Walling-

is tired of recording the fact that the adven-

Adelaide Tivoli is showing some first-in-
Australia turns. Thomas Bentley does the
familiar Dickens-impersonation: business, and
does it finely, holding his house all the time.
Also, he gives a lesson in the art of make-
up, dealing with his handsome Ilarcourt-
Beatty sort of face in full view of the audi-
ence, and converting it into the face of
Uriah Ileep or Montague Tigg, or even
Quilp. He is a Dickens enthusiast, doing
nothing else, and is anxious to see “Fellow-
ships” established all over this land. (Vic-
toria has two or three already.) The other
newcomers to Australia are the Keltons—
father, mother and a Girl. They blow music
by mouth or hand on cornets and other
things, and are perhaps—though it is a big
thing to say—a little more expert in ham-
mering harmony out of the xylophone than
anyone else yet seen here. This house is
now under Mclntosh control, with manager
Marcus in charge.

Melbourne Princess’s at present houses
“The Wearing of the Green,” a play in which
the costumes belong, to the seventeenth cen-
tury, the furniture to the twentieth, and the
brogue of some of the company not to any
age or to any time that the writer knows
of. This mellowdrama is as innocuous as
the “poems” of Ella Wheeler Wilcox, or the
recently-stained sword of Field-Marshal (or
is it Corporal?) J. Macarthur Onslow. It
is the sort of entertainment to which any
girl may take her young man with perfect
safety. Even should the said young man be
an incurable Orangeman, it is unlikely that
his anger will be roused by Allen Doone’s
unimpassioned crooning of “The Wearin’ of
the Green.” A. Doone gives one the impres-
sion that he would be quite capable of ren-
dering _ the Call of Brunhilda, from “The
Valkyrie,” as' though it were a lullaby, or
of cursing his oppressors in the accents of a
draper’s assistant expatiating upon the merits
of the latest and sweetest thing in camisoles.
On Saturday, “Sweet County Kerry” is to
replace “The Wearing,” etc., and it is under-
stood that Mishter Doone, with the aid of a
guide book, has compiled further ditties
anent the beauty sphots of dear ould Ire-
land, and intends giving the Lakes of Kil-
larney and the Rock of Cashel a well-earned
rest.
* * *
Minnie Ivaufmann, who rides a bicycle,
and Chinko, who juggles, also Fred Bluett,
the gentle, smiling vocalist, are the latest
arrivals at the Opera House. But the great-
est arrivals are the Schenk Bros. Their
strength of wrist, sense of equilibrium and
art of doing wonderful things in a casual
manner, command the admiration of this
paper, and almost put it in a humor to forgive
Mdlle. Margo, “the mystic lady,” for her
collection of antique “deceptions.” D’Arc’s
Marionettes are the least inhuman that ever
were, in this country, though not the fun-
niest, and Will Whitburn continues to be
droll on his old and original lines.

innards of Africa and becoming a bogus
Arab, gave to the narrative an element of
absurdity that even warm picture support-
ers found hard to swallow. “His Besetting
Sin,” a yarn of a man who gambled unwisely
and too often, appealed very strongly to the
right-thinking multitude ; while the “kinema-

In “Man’s Genesis,” run off at Melbourne
Olympia on Saturday, Spencer became the
historian of a past, when Man’s commerce
with his brother Man was limited to casual
embezzlement of his tucker and his women.
Those were the times when the person with
the best dinners and the shapeliest girls was
the strongest ruffian in the land, but Spencer
shows how a mere weakling genius invented
the stone hammer, and thus substituted mili-
tary skill for mere ignorant brute force. The
only other photo-drama that need be men-
tioned was “The Equine Spy,” a U.S. war
story, testifying that the gee-gee, despite
the evidence of punters, can occasionally be
regarded as an intelligent animal.
* * * *
Literary drama had two nights out at
Melbourne Athenaeum Hall last week. John
Galsworthy’s “The Pigeon,” described as a
fantasy in three acts, and accepted by its
confused admirers as whatever they like to
think it, was the first of the Repertory Co.’s
strange ventures.
* * * *
The name character is a credulous man,
with the ideal artist nature. His philan-
thropy runs riot. He gives house-room to a
young flower-girl of easy virtue; a flippant,
ragged Frenchman, who smokes his cigar-
ettes and philosophises in broken English;
and an aged dipsomaniac. As to whether
the cynical foreigner secretly derides his
benefactor’s credulity, or respects him for
his goodness, the play is not explicit. Any-
how, all efforts to induce the three pick-ups
to work are crowned with dismal failure.
They prefer to take life as it comes, without
imposing any labor conditions on it, and the
only use the flower-lady makes of her billet
as domestic servant is to lead the footman
astray. None of these wastrels is so con-
vincing as Mr. Burns, the simple-minded
degenerate in “Passers-By.” The play has
no ending, and ho philosophy to speak of,
apart from the dictum that a dangerous
loafer in rags might have been a non-dan-
gerous loafer in purple and fine linen, which
is of no special interest to a community that
mostly lives and toils somewhere between
the two extremes.
* * * *
Gregan McMahon gave good dramatic
effect to the character of the futile French-
man, who clothes his philosophy in grime
and wreathes his dogmatic utterances with
cigarette smoke. Miss Hungerford (who is
Mrs. Mac) made a picturesque figure of the
flighty flower-girl who tries to drown herself,
but is rescued by a myrmidon of law and
order. T. Skewes was very passable as the
aged drunk, and Miss Isabel Handley, de-
spite her acidity of voice and manner, was a
satisfactory daughter to the exaggerated
philanthropist.
The revival of “John Gabriel Borkman”
brought very little new blood into the audi-
ence. Public demand was not forthcoming

The Repertory Co. shone considerably in
this sad entertainment. Miss Rose Seaton
as gloomy Mrs. 8., might have been Susie
Vaughan most of the time. Kingsley Norris,
as the busted banker, had the voice, pose’
and style of Julius Knight in “A Royal
Divorce.” His seedy , frock coat and pathetic
trousers were powerless to conceal the imi-
tation of Knight’s Napoleon. Miss Jolliffe,
as the lady Hamlet, maintained her aunty
emotions at boiling point, and the giddy
grass-widow of Mrs. JoSke was fair to see
and sweet to listen to. Also, there was a
dolefully droll old man who made a noise
like a nutmeg-grater.

Concerning “On Our Selection,” at Mel-
bourne King’s, it seems desirable to mention
that seats cannot now be booked for more
than five weeks in advance, because the
season is limited to the . period afore-
said. And as regards “The Quaker Girl,”

Senator Millen (N.S.W.) is making a
deafening low, just now, concerning the
alleged stuffing of the Commonwealth elec-
toral rolls—presumably by the corrupt ad-
dition of Government supporters. In which
connection compare the following figures
Estimate by Commonwealth Statistician
of N.S.W. population (31/3/’l2) =: . . 1,687,760
Calculated number of adults (56 per cent.) 945,000
Less calculated number of ineligibles .. 25,000
Total adults available to vote . . 920,000
Number of voters enrolled . . . . 935,000
Considering what a tremendous job it is to
count a population so scattered as that of
N.S.W. (a population which is largely on
the move, by the way), and considering, fur-
ther, what a stupendous task it is to enroll
the adult population as voters, any unbiassed
person would say that these figures were sur-
prisingly close.
A new form of “joy-riding” :
It transpired yesterday that the name of the victim
of the motor-car accident, near Windsor on Saturday,
was George Nixon. A driver’s license issued to L.
H. Greig was found on the body.— S. M. Herald.
Is this sort of thing general? It is bad
enough to be upset, and perhaps killed, by
George Nixon, driving on his own qualifica-
tions. But if one sits behind a speed of
50 miles an hour, and his guarantee of safety
is that driver Nixon has Greig’s guarantee
of competence about him, it seems like a
taking of needless chances. By the way, a
well-meaning ass, seeing in a paper that
someone named L. H. Greig had been killed,
hurried off to break the news to the latter’s
mother. She is now searching for that
officious sympathiser, figuratively speaking,
with a meat-chopper—which is the favorite
weapon of the fool-killer. One of the most
salutary of social axioms is: “Never break
the news to mother till you have perused the
death-certificate.”
Living is merely a habit —so is lying.
He who lives long, lies long; and he who
lives longest is a devil of a liar. At least,
that is the general rule. Russia’s centenary
celebrations of the battle of Borodino have
brought to limelight an aged farmer, who
says he is 120 years old, and that he was
forced by Napoleon, who had captured him,
to pilot the Grand Army through Poland on
its disastrous trip to s|oscow. Russia is a
wild, credulous, illiterate country even now;
but a century ago it was even more so. The
old man, if he has any documentary proof in
his breast or bomb pocket, is probably using
the credentials of his grandfather. As The
Bulletin has remarked before, the accred-
ited. 100-year-old is extremely rare. The
vanity of obscure ancients sometimes drives
them to add 20 or 30 years to their age, when
their contemporaries have trundled away to
the cemetery, or have become too doddery or
hazy in the head to argue about it. Younger
folk refrain from scoffing through politeness,
or dread of the inevitable fit of toothless
lury; and so the unsupported assertion be-
comes an established fact, and is duly re-
corded on a proud and eloquent tombstone.
A man of 120 may not be impossible; but he
would at least be difficult The maddest fea-
ture of the yarn, however, lies in the sug-
gestion that the great and far-seeing Nap-
oleon, the man of wiles and dodges and maps
and strategy and all manner of skilful pro-
positions, plunged into Russia, relying on the
chance that he might capture an agricul-
tural lout of 20 who had prooably never left
his native village to pilot him and his 600,000
men across a dead flat country of the most
friendly description. That discrepancy is so
vast that even a Russian pressman should
notice it.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Melbourne Savage Club, by way of expressing fond
affection for brother Albert Bailey in his prosperity,
has booked the whole of the d.c. at the King’s for
one night a week or so hence. An array of boiled-
shirted “Savages” and their womenkind in the d.c.
will not be calculated to lend any bush atmosphere
to “On Our Selection,” but as a display of superior
patronage of Australiahn drama for Australian’s sake
it will be impressive. More impressive than oppor-
tune in fact. The “Dad” play can crowd the house
every time without any assistance of that sort. Many
a struggling local production has felt the want of it.
Thomas Augustine Barrett, professionally known
as Leslie Stuart, composer of “Florodora” and other
musical comedies, has been under examination in the
London Bankruptcy Court. Liabilities, £12,075;
available assets, £1875. Causes of insolvency—-
living above his income and losses through the
piracy of his popular songs. He estimates that the
piratical printers and publishers robbed him of
£20,000. “Florodora” was the most successful of
his pieces. The profit from it up to date is ,£21,000.
“The Silver Slipper,” “The Belle of Mayfair,”
“llavanna” and “Peggy” made an average profit of
£3400. “Captain Kidd” was his only failure. The
Jews now draw 90 per cent, of his royalties. Bar-
rett was a boy of 14 when he was appointed organist
of the R.C. Cathedral in Manchester, and he spent
10 years in that city “teaching and composing
church music and songs.” • Then success came to
him, and swelled head followed, also extravagance
came after swelled head. And now T. A. Barrett is
apparently worth less than in the days when he was
a boy organ-grinder in the R.C. fane at Manchester.
“One Who Pays”: Re “On Our Selection” and the
tremendous boom it has created all over the Com-
monwealth, its outstanding virtue is its optimism.
Kendall, Gordon, Marcus Clarke, Lawson are pessi-
mists all. Rudd comes with the welcome relief of a
man falling over a dog at a funeral. Bailey gets
into the very skin of Dad. His creation ranks with
Williamson’s Stoefel and Emmet’s Fritz, plus the
virtue of being our own flesh, blood and bone. Won-
derful make up. Walks straight out of the books.
Bailey has regularly churned Dad in. More re-
markable even than Irving shrinking into Robes-
pierre, and stodging into Napoleon.

(B. 19/9/’l2). It was in the ’6o’s —which was the
year the firm of Baillie and Butters suddenly retired
(or should one say, fled ?) from business in Melbourne.
An old burlesque—“lxion”—-was being played at the

who with Harwood, was in the caste, introduced this
verse
Oh where and oh where has my little dog gone
Oh where and oh where can he be ?
Perhaps, like Butters, he has put up his shutters,
And cleared away off to Fiji.
Thin humor and Kiplingesque rhyming, I grant you,
but that is a way this brand of art has ever had.

£l4OO per night. If that is his price we shall pro-

After the vanishing lady, the disappearing donkey.
This is the latest illusion of Maskelyne and Devant
at St. George’s Hall, London. A well-groomed and
intelligent-looking hee-haw, animal is brought on the
stage. Anyone who likes can go un and pat the
beast, so as to make sure that it is real ass and
blood. The- cabinet is open to general inspection.
The donkey is led, or, rather, pushed, into the
cabinet. The doors are closed for a second or two,
and when they are re-opened the cabinet is empty and
there is no donkey. This is a vastly more difficult
trick than the vanishing lady. The latter is an in-
telligeiji, creature, who knows how to work her in-
geniolfslyiisontrived but innocent-looking chair, and
to sink unperceived down the cunningly-concealed
trap-door. But the donkey is a cantankerous beast,
who donks under protest, and with bitter recrimina-
tion in his eye. And yet there have been more
wonderful shows. Witness the disappearing bank of
1893.
“Harrison O.” (his unsupported opinion given for
what it is worth) : “John Gabriel Borkman,” which
has just been revived at McMahon’s Repertory Theatre
(Melbourne), is surely one of the best examples of
Ibsen’s craftsmanship. As soon as the curtain goes
up on the first scene —-Mrs. Borkman’s sitting-room—-
a maid brings that lady a card which heralds the
entry of her sister and life-long rival, Ella. Within
two minutes of the commencement we are confronted
with an intensely dramatic situation. The drama
embodies histories, complete in all essential particu-
lars, of Borkman, his wife and his sister-in-law, yet
the action of the four acts is confined to one even-
ing, Borkman’s last evening on earth. Ibsen, by
his skilful use of the retrospective method, tells the
story backwards, and never loses his grip for a
minute.. The dramatist who, after studying the
works of the Norwegian craftsman, begins his play
at the beginning, deserves a bad end.
“Burton’s Circus” was once a familiar phrase all
over Australia. Its proprietor seems to be still on
this side of the Jordan. William Burton, “London’s
Oldest Showman,” has just been interviewed. Age
84. When a boy he went to sea as a stowaway on
the barque Essex, bound for Melbourne. Landed
there, possessing 55., to go on with. Fell in with a
man who was running a small circus, and joined
him. Learned how to ride horses and do acrobatic
tricks. At 24 had saved enough money to start a
circus of his own, and toured Australia with it, doing
well at Ballarat, Bendigo and other goldfields. Took
it to India and Japan, and returned to Australia with
the biggest circus ever seen there, including the first
troupe of Japanese wire-walkers and tumblers. “A
splendid show,” the old man called it, and it re-
tained its popularity for years in Australia and
Maoriland, the takings sometimes realising £250 a
night. After that came unlucky speculations, booms,
wild cats and bad times. Old Burton, at 84, is still
picking up a precarious livelihood as a showman in
the streets of London —assuming, of course, that he
is the same old Burton.
“Haresfoot”: William Winter, of New York, to my
mind the best theatrical critic living who uses the
alleged English language, has just published a volume
of intense criticism of Shakspearian performances he
has seen. it is real criticism, too; not merely a
series of agreeably written notices. Most critics are
peacocky, trying to show what fine fellows they are,
like the War Cry with General Booth getting 20
times'as much space as the Almighty., Winter thrusts
a hand into the gauntlet, and his volume is by far
the finest of its kind I have ever met. He dissects
the Hamlets with skill, and divides honors between
Booth and Irving. Despite the enthusiastic praise by
Dickens of Fechter’s Prince of Denmark, Winter turns
that actor firmly down. The interesting fact is re-
corded that Sarah Bernhardt’s Hamlet lasted six
hours on the first night of its production in Paris.
The critic holds that the greatest Othellos have been
E. Kean, Brooke and Salvini. It is now up to Jack
Johnson to try the part. About Brooke he says that
his finest characters were Othello, lago, Richelieu
and Virginius. Brooke’s unrivalled voice had broken
up or down or something before he came to Aus-
tralia. At his best his. performances were almost
operatic in charm of elocution, and he had a
statuesque beauty of attitude which was beyond
description. One curious feature of Brooke’s work
was its variability. He could never be twice alike,
while Charles Kean would always do exactly the same
thing at the same time.
W. G. R. Sprague, the London architect, who is
son of Dolores Drummond (the actress who made her
debut in Coppin’s Olympic, Lonsdale-street, Mel-
bourne, away back in the ’Bo’s, and who was the
leading lady to G. V. Brooke), has a big practice in
theatre construction. He is now supervising the re-
construction of the London Alhambra, for a long time
the home of the ballet, and an army of workers are
busy night and dav, so as to make a bright, new
theatre out of the dingy, dilapidated, old one, within
a couple of months. Also, Sprague is building an-
other gorgeous theatre in * Shaftesbury Avenue, close
to the Palace, which is to be called the “Ambas-
sadors” —a pretty big mouthful, and not particu-
larly felicitous. Furthermore, he is devising new
theatres for Bristol, Coventry and a dozen other
provincial cities. Asked “what about the new Lon-
don theatres'for Oscar Asche and H. B. Irving?”
Sorague replied, “Mr. Asche’s theatre w'll be pro-
ceeded with ere long. The date of starting Mr. Irv-
ing’s is not yet fixed. But all in good time.”
There are ructions in the house of Hammerstein,
the New York Jew who has given London a magnifi-
cent opera house that it doesn’t want. His sons are
in partnership with him in the show business, arid
they strongly object to the money they make in New
York being mopped up by the old man to pay his
losses on his ill-starred London venture. Willie
Hammerstein, manager of New Victoria Roof
Garden, which has proved a veritable'gold mine, is
the leader of the domestic or kitchen revolt. He re-
fuses to allow any of the profits of the roof garden
to be diverted to what he calls the “education of
the Londoners in grand opera.” Abraham, the
youngest of the Hammerstein scions, agrees with
Bill, and has been expressing his mind even more
freely about the old man’s “indiscretions in Lon-
don.” The latest telegrams indicate that the boys
will insist on their downtrodden parent running the
London opera house in future on “American vaude-
ville lines.” It is hardly worth while being the old
man in these days.
“Circle”: I may be about five years late in making
the discovery, but I now realise why the postcard
actress always bares her milk-white teeth. As seen
from the front row, gold-stuffed tusks are a gleam-
ing distraction. Every mime should chalk on his or
her make-up box, “Wear none but the best teeth.”
The gaze of the auditor-seems to centre mechanically
on the lips of the character who is speaking; and
the slightest defect becomes an irritant. As well
play Lord and Lady Montmorency in busted boots as
bite off splendid lines with inferior mouths.
(Continued on page 36.)
The man with the gun is always danger-
ous. and the discarded lover is worse than
the ordinary man, because of his nervous-
ness and excitability. So when young Rob-
ert Davies, of Port Melbourne, found last
week that his lady love had given him the
cold shoulder, and subsequently set about
cleaning and fixing up his breech-loader,
there was every chance of trouble. The
trouble duly arrived when he reached his
lodgings that evening. His ex-srirl, a friend
of the family, was in the dining-room con-
suming supper with one of the daughters.
Davies retired to his room for a space, then
emerged with the gun, and, seeing the two
girls standing at the dining-room door, fired
at them. They dropped—the ex-best girl
hardly damaged; the daughter of the house
so frightfully wrecked that it would have
been better for her if she had died on the
spot. She was, at latest, in hospital awaiting
the end. Having made such a mess of
things, Davies put in surer work with his
next shot by placing the muzzle of the gun
in his mouth and pulling the trigger. There
was hardly enough of him left to hold an
inquest on. These discarded lovers are an
unreliable harum-scarum lot. Davies would
have soothed his own troubles just as effec-
tively if he had killed himself first and shot
the girls afterwards; but the shootists never
seem to think of that.

I don’t aspire to eminence
As lofty as the leading gents. !
For what’s the good?
Detractors would
Deny me “vast intelligence.”
Mummers as stars who reign,
Possess —it’s plain—
No brain!
Their ads. and posters are immense,
And though they show some grains of sense
When quoting terms,
Alas, poor worms!
They’re minus “vast intelligence” !
They get the fattest parts,
Enslave the hearts
Of tarts !
But off the stage, free of pretence,
They’re beings absolutely dense,
When they should shine
Or give some sign
Of having “vast intelligence.”
So I’m glad I am no star,
The sneering par.
Would jar!
And my limelit omnipotence,
Might—to my sorrow—give offence
To those who write,
And stars indict,
For lacking “vast intelligence.”
VICTORIA.
EARDLEY TURNER.

WINES
A CRUSHER.
“I got an awful knock this morning
"Go on. How did it happen? ”
“On my way to the links an old lady asked
me if I could mend her umbrella while she
watted, and how much would I charge."
The Yellow Pup is a loud animal in his
native backyard; but he seems to be as
pathetically futile there as elsewhere. Accord-
ing to a cable, “a Unionist demonstration at
Ballyroney, County Down, was marked by
great enthusiasm. All the Orange Lodges
paraded the streets with drums which were
beaten vigorously by stalwart men with their
coats off, the din being terrific.” It isn’t the
thinking dog that makes the most noise. The
Pup had ever a loud yelp, but he hasn’t as
much bite in him as an old age pensioner
with inferior false teeth. It has never sunk
its alleged fangs into the Pope’s leg—it
hasn’t even torn the gaiters off a bishop, or
bitten the hand of a hedge-priest. It just
lurks behind the Walls of Derry and beats a
drum with its stalwart tail —the least in-
tellectual end of an animal, which always
seems to travel in the dust cloud at the latter
end of Life’s procession.
Adelaide badly wants to know whether it
was the horse or the man that was tired of
lifeV Or was it both? A tram-car was
scratching for its life along the Unley-road.
It had its time-table to consider, and it was
a case of “root hog or die.” A horseman
appeared, riding straight at it, between the
rails. It was naturally assumed that he
"would turn off. When he seemed rather long
about it, the driver rang the bell hard. In
another second he rang like a madman. Then
he rang like perdition, and dragged at every
handle at once, and shoved on the brakes and
everything else, and backed till the street was
red-hot, and dodged for his life. But horse
and rider simply galloped into the front of
the car —they didn’t even utter a cry of hor-
ror while doing it —and broke their two
necks.
Years ago, when Parkes was consul, and
'N.S.W. politicians used to fight like devils
in the refreshment room, someone advertised
briefly in a Sydney paper: “I, John Smith,
am not the John Smith who was fined for
drunkenness yesterday. (Signed), John
Smith.” Something of the same sort hap-
pened last week, vide this advt. in the S. M.
Herald
We, the undersigned, are not the Mrs. Bearparks
who lost the pawn-ticket of ring.
(Signed), Mrs. G. and H. Bearpark.
There was no adddress on the brief chron-
icle, and the circumstances under which two
Mrs. Bearparks lost a pawn-ticket and two
more were wholly unconnected with the deed,
remain unexplained even unto this day.
It was suggested (very respectfully, of
course) to the Tas. University Council re-
cently that the reformed spelling adopted
by the Victorian Education Department might
be worthy of consideration by the Speck’s
Uni. authorities; but the suggestion almost
apoplexed that erudite body. One indignant
owl ruffled up his neck-feathers and remarked
that the present spelling was practically
settled in the time of Dryden, and as it had
acted very satisfactorily ever since, there was
no need to alter it in any way whatever.
Why the objector accepted the spelling of
King Charles’s time, and ignored the in-
finitely older, and, consequently, more sacred,
spelling of King Alfred, is a mystery. An-
other venerable gent, uprose in anger, and
quaveringly hoped that “until these extraor-
dinary orthographical variations were more
generally adopted, the University of Tas-
mania would decline to encourage them in
any manner.” So the Speck’s University
will continue to spell in no more modern way
than that deemed permissible by the com-
pilers of the Oxford Dictionary. The rest
of the world may spell as it dash well likes.
Tassy will be no party to the sacrilegious
poisoning of the wells of ye olde gentylmanne
hight Chaucer.

“Harrison O.” : The wages of sin may be
death, but what of the wages of saintliness?
Another of the periodical shrieks anent the
sweating of expounders of the Word has
gone up in Melbourne. The bloated Angli-
can Church, to which Fat largely belongs,
and the coffers of tvhieh are swollen with the
rents of valuable city lands that were
granted to the church by past Governments,
is a particularly outrageous offender in this
respect. At the Diocesan Synod of 1910 a
motion was passed opining that every official
soul-repairer should be guaranteed at least
£2OO a year. At that time nearly one-third
of the parsons in charge of parishes in the
diocese were receiving less. The list in-
cluded laborers in the Vineyard who were
in receipt of £195, £194 and £lBO per annum.
The synod’s resolution has not, so far, been
given effect to, and in the diocese of Mel-
bourne at the present time there are 26
wearers of the holy choker who are annex-
ing. on an average, £166 a year. There is,
also, one person who snatches brands from
the conflagration at £122 per dozen months.
It would be interesting to know how much
this is per brand. Outside the Melbourne
diocese things are even worse. Verily, Chris-
tianity covers a multitude of sins.
The plaint of “Gentleman Two” :
One of the side-results of the' R. D. Meagher row
has been to make public the fact that the N.S.W.
Law Institute is not the potent and authoritative
institution it has for so long tacitly represented itself
to be, but a more or less irresponsible body. It ap-
pears that the concern is not authorised by Parlia-
ment ; it has no charter; no genuine legal status;
no anything. Only a paltry percentage of solicitors
so much as pretend to owe it allegiance. Of these,
about 10 per cent, take sufficient interest in its
existence to turn up and vote at the election of
officials. Its position is analogous to that of a union
within a union—a very small, tired union inside a
very large one. Supposing a handful of wharf lab-
orers suddenly announced to their union that they
had been formally elected by a handful of members
as the officials of the Bag-carrying Institute; and
that, in their Bag-carrying Institutional capacity
they proposed to deal with any wharf toiler who
misbehaved himself,, to the extent, possibly, of de-
priving the said toiler of the means of earning his
living for all time. In such a case there would cer-
tainly be general ructions. Probably the first to
talk of the tyrannous villainy of such conduct would
be , th ! ™ enib ers of the Law Institute, and the quick-
est off the mark in the matter of urging Parliament
to interfere would be the more affluent Gents. One
The strong position that the Law Institute has been
able to arrogate to itself is an interesting example
of the way the public yields to audacious preten-
sions when the same are buttressed by the proper
brand of gravity and pompousness—also, preposter-
ousness. ’ F
WHAT WOULD YOU DO?
“Yes, very, pretty; but Tm afraid you're gelling
me on the wrong side of the ledger."
“Well, isn't that better than getting on the
wrong side of me?"
Paris Nesbit, K.C., made a sporting at-
tempt the other day to get an Adelaide dealer
in railway return-tickets out of a £5O fine
imposed by Judge Buchanan. Whether there
is anything morally wrong about these sales
is a question, but the Law says they are not
to be made. The difficulty is to get proof.
In this case the Railway Commissioner had
authorised one of his own officials to try jo
buy tickets on the quiet—which he did with-
out difficulty. Therefore, argued Nesbit on
appeal, the Commissioner had authorised the
sale! Appealing is hard work in S.A. Su-
preme Court, because there are only three
Judges. The one who tried the case doesn’t
think he was wrong, so it means convincing
the other two against him. In other words,
the appellant goes before a jury of two, and
has to get a unanimous verdict, or else lose.
In this instance, Judges Way and Murray
agreed with Buchanan that, even if the
Commissioner’s act had been “un-British,”
as Nesbit declared, it did not make the un-
lawful sale lawful.

“Melb.” goes off like a motor-cycle or a
string of Chinese crackers about matters of
bedroom and domestic interest: —
A man does not look for a woman under the bed.
The woman occasionally neglects to look for a man.
One night a student of Fitchett’s Methodist College
didn’t. She found one, or rather a boy, a chap of
about 16. Hubbuboo and ululation, as their favorite
classic poets say. The fellow escaped, but has since
been lumbered, and awaits magisterial consideration.
He was in the college before. Seen upstairs by night
at the end of a corridor, but jumped through a
window, and slid 20ft. down a waterpipe. Some
years ago one of these Romeos worried Miss Tripp’s
boarding school in Prahran. A cat-like, nocturnal
balcony-clamberer was he. Even after he had been
warned by the beaks, there was a midnight cry of,
“There he is again!” In the early days of the
wedded life of Queen Victoria and Albert they were
annoyed by the intrusions, at Buckingham Palace, of
the celebrated boy Jones, nick-named, In-I-Go Jones.
It is alleged that he was dragged from under a sofa
on which Vic. and Albert had been cooing. Gibbon
Wakefield, the Australian and Maoriland coloniser,
was handicapped through having been actually con-
cerned in two abductions. The last one got him a
term of, imprisonment. He prigged away Miss Turner,
an heiress, from a fashionable boarding school kept
by maiden ladies of the moft horrible respectability,
and took her to Paris.

The title of “alderman,.” with any special
fur trimmings and flummery attaching
thereto, has been abolished in Victoria, un-
less the House of Rich Landlords rejects the
Bill to that effect which passed the Leg. As-
sembly last week. A pathetically humorous
incident of the decision to blot out alder-
manic distinction was the presentation of a
document from the Gaud Mayor and his be-
robed brethren, begging leave to come and
make representations before the bar of the
House. Their prayer was refused. The
House lost its chance of hearing the City
Fathers trace the origin of their ancient order
of gobblers back to early Saxon days, in that
dear old Mother Country where all Austra-
lian symbols of human greatness come from.
They might at least have argued forcibly that
the term “alderman” is a merciful absurdity
for a Gaud Mayor to fall back upon when his
term of supreme hoffice has expired. The
drop from being a gaud to the rank of mere
councillor _is like the case of the field mar-
shal who is degraded to the status of a com-
missariat mule.
A SUGGESTION FOR THE THEATRE
MANAGER.
The conductor of the London Gaiety Theatre is said
.to have decided to insert a clause in his contracts
with actresses to prevent them marrying during the
currency of their engagements.
Dear Bulletin,—l notice that the Rev. S.
D. Yarrington, the Sydney holy sensational-
ist, says he could bring to Minister Flowers
“white girls who have been rescued from
these hells [i.e., the alleged Sydney opium
dens], and who know of what they are
speaking.” The proceeding, says Yarring-
ton, would make Minister Flowers shed
tears. Well, why doesn’t the holy man do
it, instead of talking so much, and thus wipe
out any suspicion that he is a manufacturer
of false evidence? If the Minister wants to
weep I will lend him a bucket. —Yours, etc.,
Anti-Chinaman.
A JINGLE OF FALSITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Continuing, witness said Truelove was cranky tem-
pered when she was in Sydney. When he saw True-
love in Melbourne nine months ago, he could see she
had been dyed chestnut. All the dye was off her
body, but it was not off her tail.
“Have you seen horses dyed?”—“Yes, 20 or so
in Sydney, and I am sorry to say they mostly came
from Victoria.” (Laughter.) “They are nearly all
dyed chestnut.”
Dyed horses and ladies defeat us all;
Disgrace and blue ruin on them attend ;
Our dreams and our castles to pieces fall,
And Death gallops in at the bitter end ;
Your heart and pocket-book ever defend,
Or sure as a gun you will suffer loss.
The Devil has nothing more he can lend
To chemical lady or chestnut boss.
Oh, the safe, hedged life may upon you pall,
But fasten to it like a leech, my friend ;
Go mumble and dream in your tidy stall,
Nor make of mad Joy an explosive blend ;
Dyed horses and ladies a home will rend
And up to the heavens a rooftree toss.
There is hardly comfort enough to spend
On chemical lady or chestnut boss.
Let the minxes go and the fielders call;
To hard-up repentance their pathways tread ;
The bailiff will wait in your mortgaged hall
And duns on your doormat their hate expend ;
They yield you a very poor dividend—■
Hairs grey as a badger’s—coats green as moss.
The Lord in His goodness our youth defend
From chestnut lady and chemical hoss.
N.S.W.
THE OFFICE WOWSER.
THE SQUARE DEAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One clergyman at a Baptist gathering in Bendigo
declared that he knew several ministers who, after
30 years’ service, were receiving 30s. a week.
We mortals are content to pay
Top rates for boots, and cheese, and hay,
And bread and meat, however dear;
We ante up for fish and beer.
But see how paltry are the doles
We,give to savers of our souls.
The preacher man is gaunt and grim,
And shabby are the togs on. him ;
The grocer man is sleek and fat,
And wears a shining guinea hat.
We pay our brethren well enough
If they deliver us the stuff.
VICTORIA.
S.S.

With the confidence of long immunity, the
Melbourne burglar grows careless these
times. Two of the gentlemen lately spent
several hours in a boot factory, selecting and
packing up the better class of the stock of
raw material, humped it down a long yard,
and then heaved the bundles over an Bft.
fence into a right-of-way, where a mate stood
handy with a “borrowed” horse and cart.
Twice O’Gallaghan’s exhausted subordinates
tramped past the end of the lane, but the
burglars burgled on. Another housebreaker
found on the door of a house he was visiting
a memo, stating “The grocer called.” “Ditto
the housebreaker,” he wrote, and proceeded
to collect a handy swag at his leisure. But
these artists get too confident. They will yet
rob O’Callaghan of his assurance, or Mur-
ray of his night’s rest, and the loss will
rouse the forlorn victims to do something.
* * * *
Those Welsh settlers whom Victorian
Rich Landlord Bloomfield Rees persuaded to
desert far Patagonia, and settle in Victoria,
are causing worry to the Melbourne quaran-
tine officials. _ They brought with thern some
£l2OO worth of lucerne seed, which is in-
fested with the disease or parasite or disaster
commonly known as dodder, and this is
much the same as Upper House. The offi-
cials desire Ihem to take it away and boil it
or burn it. or at least keep it out of the
country. It is to be hoped the officers will
remain firm. Australia has enough per-
sons, let alone plants, afflicted with dodder,
also potter, totter, blither, slumber, stagger,
shikker and so forth. What it would wel-
come is a consignment of the seed of bogger.
A heavy crop of boggers-in is urgently wanted
between AcFlaide and Darwin.
A POINT OF LAW.
If Moore bad entered the house, as he pretended,
through a wide-open window (though he had to scale
it with a ladder), that entry would not have been
burglarious, and his attack on Mr. Syme would not
have been a capital offence. — Legal point.
FRESH-AIR ENTHUSIAST : “Help! Burglars!”
BARKER BILL: “ Burglars? No fear, Miss!
I'm rehearsing the balcony scene from
‘ Romeo and Juliet?”
Victorian Speaker Madden gave words of
advice lately to a troop of Boy Bouts.
The main thing for a scout to do, he said,
was to observe and find out what the enemy
was doing. This could be found by the foot-
prints of their horses’ hoofs not, let it be
observed, by the tails of their horses heads.
Also, it was useful to be able to tell the
height of a tree—the scout should put his
back to a tree and look between his legs, and
walk till he could see the top. Then he had
the height.” Possibly! George Reid or
Spruce Smith might practise the feat with
advantage; but it must either be a very
short tree or a very long walk.

“Side-lights” : There has recently been a
suspicion of an echo of a whisper that the
S.A. Marine Board has stirred in its sleep
and murmured something about thinking of
doing something to remove the corpse of the
barque Norma, which was wrecked about
four years ago and has been waiting ever
since at the Semaphore anchorage for a
chance to poke a finger in the pie and the
plates of some innocent and unsuspecting ship
that wanders round that way. The wreck
has done some damage at least twice already,
but it was nothing that a few thousand
pounds couldn’t put right, so the Board con-
temptuously ignored the incidents. Any-
how, the Board is now said to have discov-
ered that there is a wreck lying round some-
where, and I Know that.the 'nformation will
call forth loud applause and hearty ringing
cheers from the harassed mariners who have
been dodging this particular death-trap for
the past four .years.

“John Flop” writes :
O’Callaghan’s cop does not lead a very happy life.
Recently 17 of him were lined up for having the
rooms at the Russell-street (Melbourne) barracks un-
tidy. The pffences consisted in leaving three dead
matches under one bed, a piece of orange peel under
another, and- permitting dust to remain on a con-
stable’s locker; also, one whs charged with not hav-
ing his window blind at the regulation height. They
were let off With a caution, and warned that they
would be beheaded if there was a repetition of the
offence. In the case of the Hop who didn’t have his
window blind “at the regulation height,” there wasn’t
a cord on it, and hadn’t been one for six months, and
so the cop couldn’t null the blind .down. The second
one, who dropped three dead matches on the floor,
did so while he was looking for some of his clothes
which had strayed, and which he couldn’t find with
the light O’Callaghan provided. Another constable
was jumped on because he hung his clothes on the
wall when he was changing. Yet he didn’t have any
other place—but the floor—to hang them on, because
there were 11 men sleeping in the room originally
built for six, and there weren't enough lockers to go
round. And yet O’Callaghan seems to be perfectly
satisfied with the state of things.
The up-to-date burglar is a man of fore-
sight. Fie doesn’t rush crudely at premises
and hit them with an axe or crowbar; rather
he is a Sikes of large discourse, looking be-
fore and after. Fie likes to inspect the locks
of buildings and strong-rooms while they are
under construction, and to get duplicate kevs
of safes that are for sale, and then trace these
safes to their destination when they are sold.
Then doth Sikes lightly come and lightly go,
like the fox that was caught in the end by
his light fantastic toe. A recent unfortunate
accident gave away the game. A big and
wealthy Sydney firm is having its building
altered, and a new strong-room put in.
That room will be worth plundering when it
is in use, so Sikes came by night, walking
gently, like a feather blown before a zephyr,
and took moulds of the locks. Then, by ill-
hap, someone disturbed him, and he fled like
the tatters of a defeated army, without wait-
ing to obliterate the traces of his visit. So
all the locks will doubtless be changed, or else
the firm will take all its money to the Safe
Deposit every night, and keep nothing in the
strong-room except a tiger.

A mere gent, who tried to collect Costs
like a Gent. One was sternly turned down
by Adelaide Insolvency Court the other day.
As agent, he attended faithfully and well at
the financial obsequies of a farmer, and asked
accordingly that he should be paid his “fair
and reasonable charges out of the estate,” as
provided by the Act. Now the Act distinctly
says “solicitor or agent.” The Court, how-
ever, reckoned that to let any stray agent
blow in without qualifications or “restraints
such as those imposed on the legal profession
in the interests of the public,” would be a
“freak in legislation.” Wherefore no order:
no costs; no nothing.
An Art note from Melbourne: —
If there is any Vic. painter to whom Ma Nature
should feel truly grateful it is John Mather, who has
just suspended HI pictures for exhibition at Mel-
bourne Athenaeum. John presents Ma’s finest views
in a pious and seemly manner, and there is nothing
in his treatment of his themes verging on impiety
or even impertinence. He deals with decorous wave-
lets, sculpturesque cliffs, well-behaved cloudscanes
and so on, and turns out liis works like art supple-
ments. “Reflections,” his most pretentious picture,
depicts a promenade of beach greying into night
with a couple of human figures to give it civilisation.
The other big canvas, “The Golden West,” fairly
well priced at 100 guineas, is a dying sunset sensa-
tion, treated as a sort of atmospheric anti-climax.
The sketch of a patriarch, probably Methuselah, is
an abler portrayal than the snapshot of a lady in a
kimono ; but there is an effort to paint something
above the commonplace in “Fernshawe,” a perspective
of gaunt gums and purple distance. “On the Buffalo
River'’ informs you that two or three kine desire to
drink, and “Chinese Gardens, Brighton,” is a simple
pictorial description of mostly vegetable beds in
parallel rows. The trees in the background, how-
ever, are interestingly limned, a remark which might
apply to “Under the Banksias” and “Gum Trees,
Heafesville.” One doubts whether the blare of the
afterglow in Nos. 29 and 57 is entirely convincing,
but the bits of Williamstown here and there do more
than justice to that average town.
It will be a great day when the Curates’
Revolution breaks out, and bishops run for
their lives, with sharpened collection plates
flying behind them, and the dread cry of
“Down with the gaiters!” ringing in their
ears. The infuriated worm will turn on oc-
casion, so the lower-deck isaint should be
capable of at least one revolution. And he
has received plenty of provocation. Some of
the worst sweating goes on in the Church,
which institution usually contrives to pay
its heads well, but is bitterly economical with
regard to the meek underlings. A blizzard
of protest is likely to be raised by the latest
idea for gilding the refined gaiters. It con-
sists of an Ecclesiastical Bill to provide a
retiring allowance for Clarke, of Melbourne
--the imported dignitary who came from an
English post, at £3OO a year, to take com-
mand in Victoria at just five times that
figure. For some time past he has been
getting £2OOO, and some Anglicans reckon
that he should be able to provide for his old
age out of his salary. No person expects an
elderly Archbishop to chase after Old Harry
at a high speed; so, with ordinary luck,
there should not be much unsalaried old age
to provide for. If his Grace does not reject
the proposal when it is brought up in Synod,
there will be hostile mutterings under the
curious flat hats that cover the minds of the
hungry-lookiug lower-grade clerics —the rank
and file of the toilers in the vineyard.

“Why don’t the new men join me now?”
The leader cried, and clasped his brow.
“With paper drums I’ve made a row,
But ev’rything seems dead!
The path is clear, the cause is right,
But only vet’rans join the fight!”
A Voice came singing through the night,
“Because you’re Out!” it said.
“Because you’re D.own and out!”
“Not for all time!” the leader cried.
“We’re pressing close the other side ;
Into their places we may glide—■
Of that there is no doubt.
At any moment they may fall,
For their majority is small.”
“But Gregory,” the Voice did call,
, “Please don’t forget you're Out!
They’re In and you are Out !”
“Where are the stern, devoted men
I ever seek with voice and pen?”
The leader claspecl his brow again,
And wildly gazed about.
The Voice said: “Liberals of weight
Would all be Ministers of State :
But kerbstone bumps and scorn they hate!
. They don’t like going. Out!
And all your men go Out! .
Go OUT!!
O-U-T—OUT! ! !”
The leader, groping, sought his hat;
’Twas plain he had not thought of that.

DISORDER & UPROAR.
AN IMPENDING VACATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The telephone operators of both sexes at the
-various exchanges in Sydney are up in arms at the
posting of a roster in the G.P.O. Exchange to the
effect that the hours are to be increased from next
Monday. The suggestion was at first made that a
strike should be organised, and the users of tele-
phones in Sydney appeared to be faced with the
prospect of a suddden stoppage of all telephonic com-
munication. — Sydney Telegraph.
There hangs upon the wall
The mouthpiece of the town ;
I like it not at all,
Yet dare not tear it down.
It has two silver bells,
Some metal and some wire ;
It clatters and it yells
When silence I desire.
It clatters and it yells,
Until 1 fiercely go
And still the trembling bells,
And loudly cry “Hell- oh!”
Then swift along the wire
There comes a buzz and cough,
And crackling sounds of fire ;
And someone says “Jting off!”
I ring, and straight depart,
Though I would like to stay,
And ope my bursting heart,
And all my curses say.
Then, when I’m settled quite
In some cool room remote,
The boxed electric blight
Sounds its accursed note.
I leap to do its will;
I run, and puff, and blow ;
Then my poor lungs I fill
And once more yell “Hell- oh!”
“What number, please t” she raps.
(I do a Highland Fling—
I blither!) “Well!” she snans,
“I wish you wouldn’t ring!”
Dear Girl! Her ears are sharp!
She hears the faintest “ting,”
She’s like a trembling harp—-
Until I really ring.
Until I really ring
Enough to wake the dead,
That dear responsive thing
Is to my number wed.
I ring and ring again !
I rave and ring like mad !
No answer comes. Oh, then
“Engaged!” would make me glad!
And she may go on strike
And cease to make a fuss?
This sort of news I like!
A rest for both of vs!
N.S.W.

CIRCUMSTANCES ALTER
SANDY : “So they’ve feenished th’ buildin' and

“J. B. Cow” : I do not know if the Most
Blasphemy-Creating Job competition recently in-
augurated in your columns by the Religious Editor
is still raging, but if so I would like to enter the
business, craft or profession of yarding camels. The
labors of Hercules are a trifle to it, and the task of
Sisyphus fades into nothingness by comparison. The
misshapen brutes which I endeavored to yard be-
longed to Murrin-Murrin (W.A.). I got them as far
as the wing, which consisted of a dog-leg fence run-
ning out about 800yds. from the gate. In fact,
with a little persuasion, I lured them to within
50 yards of the gate. There they stopped
and firmly demanded an explanation. I called
up my reserves, viz., two boys (one black
and one white and both fairly well mounted). I
also demanded assistance of the blacksmith and his
wife and a tribe of assorted curs. We all advanced
uproariously. The shuffling horrors simply sneered.
They then walked straight at us and through us,
and though earnestly entreated with loaded whips,
saplings, stones and language set off to the open
country. It took three of us five solid days of riding
with fresh horses every day to run them to a stand-
still. Being thrown severally and roped they were
dragged or towed to the enclosure, which is the
most unsastisfactory way of yarding beasts that I
know of.
“Machete”: I see that “Bromide” (B.
12/9/’l2) upholds the bike as against the horse for war
purposes, and denounces me as “a carpet bushman,”
or “a George-street backblocker,” for arguing to the
contrary. In point of fact, I am nothing of the
sort. I was born and bred on a Western Q. station.
I spent much of my time till I was 25 in the West,
and after that put in 24 years Cliow-championing in
South Africa, followed by three years of all-round
experience in Papua. But that hardly affects the
issue, which is a mere matter of common sense.
Does “Bromide” imagine that, if he had to get for
his skin, on a bike, through an upper Barcoo gidyea
scrub, or through the heavy sand of the Balonne
cypress pine and belar belts,, that he could elude
mounted men ? Even in the open, provided it was
tussocky country, he would be backing a loser. The
western biker rides roads or pads whenever he can.
It is possible for him to cross country in a fashion.
He doesn’t sprint over it, though. No one questions
“B.’s” claim that the bushman makes good fighting
material. But that is not to say he is of value
against a thoroughly trained man, and it is against
the most thoroughly trained and efficient of modern
I soldiers that the Australian will have to go up. If
they had only been moderately disciplined, the Boers
would have carried Ladysmith and Kimberley, and
have occupied Capetown and Durban within a month
of the commencement of the S.A. war. The guerilla
fighter, as a campaign winner, is not worth considera-
tion. Attrition invariably wears him out in the
end. It is in the Defence enactments; in the staff
colleges, and the rigorous enforcement of military
training that Australia’s fighting chance lies. Her
untrained bushmen will undoubtedly annoy the
enemy, as sandflies do. Like sandflies, they can be
smoked off, or otherwise provided against.
“Yarrrnn” : Last week, went to “an even-
ing” round near the Blowhole (N.5.W.),. and had a
great time. A funny man showed a trick. Taking a
safety match-box and four matches, he stuck two in
the end of the box, and then wedged one in between
them, standing like a horizontal bar. Then he asked
the company which of the up-
right matches would burn out
first if the cross one were
lighted, in the middle. The
company didn’t know, or
didn’t care to say. With the
fourth match he then set fire
to the match between the two
vertical ones; and, when it
had burnt quite through the
middle, the two lighted ends
flew across the room into an
old lady’s lap, burning a de-
cent-sized hole in a new silk
dress. The old party was
wild, and the funny fellow
looked silly. I was reminded
of a happening at Jembaicum-
bene (N.S.W.). Out on the
plain there, where once had
been a dense forest, a trunk
of an old turpentine had be-
come jammed between the
tops of two standing stringy-
barks. We boys used to climb
up and walk along the cross
piece. Then, one stormy after-
noon, lightning struck the tur-
pentine in the centre, setting
fire to it. The two lighted
ends jumped, and one end flew
through a shepherd’s hut,
200yds. away, impaling the
cat, two packs of cards, one
pannikin, one billycan and
other cooking utensils and
things too numerous to mention. The
other half was never either seen or heard
of. Quong Tart, who used to walk the
turpentine with us, was in the habit of
telling this very true yarn; and would
get very wild when nobody would believe
him. And at Moruya (N.S.W.) a limb
of a dead tree was once burst by light-
ning, and a gigantic splinter was hurled,
by the hurricane blowing', through the iron roof
and calico ceiling of a cottage. It impaled and
killed a boy in bed. Last time writer was there the
hole in the roof was patched with kerosene tins, and
it (the cottage) did duty as a post-office.
“Yarrum” : It is amusing to note how
some of these South Coast .(N.S.W.) State quarry-
men are helping the mean cocky to be a little
meaner, and to escape paying a decent wage. I
know a man who hires out his three children—a
girl aged 14 and two boys of 12 and 10 years re-
spectively-—to do all the milking for a neighboring
cowman at 3s. each per week. Mr. Cocky is thus
getting work done that he should have to pay £3
a week and tucker for. The cow is fast spreading
her queenly self all over Labor in the Illawarra dis-
trict. The children attend school, after milking,
and doze and sleep over their lessons most of the
time. I know. Truly the way some of these for-
tunate toilers stick to their more scattered, and,
consequently, less fortunate rural workers is mar-
vellous to behold !

“Kola”: The Melbourne Argus proudly
affirmed, in a recent issue, that all the immigrants
are fine practical men. They may be fine, in a sense
—one I came across was so fine that, when he stood
sideways, you could hardly see him—but here are
some of my experiences. An immigrant strolled over
to his Australian neighbor, and asked him to come
and have a look at a sick hen. “She don’t seem to
be able to stand,” he added. The Australian went
over, and found that the hen was clocking, and of
course wouldn’t leave the nest. He patiently ex-
plained, and gave the immigrant a setting of prize
egg's. Later on, he heard that 10 chickens had Deen
hatched ; but, on going across to inspect, he found
the hen with only two. “Yes,” said the immigrant,
“there was 10 hatched all right, but I thought two
was as many as the hen could rear, so I killed-the
rest.” I drove out to another place with a man who
was interested in separators. We were delayed, and.
it was after 10 when we got home. The separator
man wanted to see the immigrant separating, and he
was annoyed to see all the cows in the paddock.
“I thought so,” he growled; “the cream’s halfway
to town now.” However, we drove up to the house
of one of the men—there were three of
them —and a frowsy person came to the
door yawning. “Come in,” he said, “and
have a cup of tea. I’m going to bring the cows
in now.” The same three men, after vainly endeav-
ing to drive a heifer into a particular bail, threw
and roped her, and dragged her to the bail. The ,
antics of the immigrants in our part of the world
furnish the farm-houses with half their winter en-
tertainment.
Dear Bulletin, — There probably isn’t
another creature extant —-mankind always excepted—
which has a more retentive memory or a greater
capacity : for love or hate than a camel. By the
slightest action, good or evil, one may make a friend
or an enemy of the beast for life. Go among camels
as a perfect stranger, and you will find yourself
regarded with curiosity. Also, you will be looked
upon with some suspicion, a certain quantity of dis-
trust, and not little superciliousness. Further, you
may observe a guarded expression conveying the
notion that you might not be a bad poor sort of
devil, after all. Right from the start you will be
given the benefit of the doubt. No open hostility
will be shown you; neither will the slightest over-
ture savoring of amity be made toward you. You
will just be put on your trial then and there, and
on your showing during the first five minutes of
acquaintance, so will the record be kept against
you for life. It is an embarrassing business being
introduced to camels. You feel you are the cynosure
of every eye—each a calm, critical, comprehensive
sort of eye—and you know the responsibility rests
with you, whether you are to be their friend or foe.
There is hardly any middle course. The first time I
went among camels I bore with me a feeling of
uncertainty. I didn’t know whether they’d eat me,
or merely wither me to death with superior glances.
I was greatly agitated and felt strongly called upc"
to do something. Quiescence offered no way put
So I took a firm hold of my immaculate reputa-
tion, together with my hope of a life hereafter with
one hand, and, with the other, I timidly stroked a
cow-camel’s ear. A sort of sigh, or gust of relief
immediately ran through the kneeling throng. /
wasn’t a bad sort of poor blighter, after all. So
spoke the voice of cameldorn.—Yours, etc., MaUjAßa-
poonie.

THE PERFECT IMMIGRANT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There came to us an immigrant,
Maroney got him very cheap.
The new-chum seemed to be content
With seven bob a week and keep.
He said that he could shear a treat.
And fence a bit, and ride for mails,
Could kill a sheep, and dress the meat.
Could drive the engine, stack the wheat.
Or take a hand at splitting rails.
His name was Ben, his dialect
Was rather quaint. His gall was great.
We gathered be did not expect
To leave his little bed till eight.
Creamed coffee, toast and marmalade,
Dropped eggs, and chops he ordered for
His breakfast, cursed the chambermaid
By whom his blessed bed was laid,
And put his boots outside the door.
Next morn when called to milk, said he:
“Where are the lassoes kept, old lad?”
“ ’N’ what the Divil may that be?”
Maroney was no longer glad.
The new-chum seemed to be amused,
And started to explain just how
A lasso’s made, and how it’s used.
He said he’d have to be excused
If not allowed to throw the cow.
Ben stayed, with us till noon that day,
And when he drew a gun to shoot
A simple Chow out on his way,
Maroney helped him with a boot.
But ere he went did he disclose
An entertaining fact to me:
Of farming life all that he knows
He gleahed at living picture shows
At home in happy Battersea.
victoria.

“Searchlight”: Lightning plays some low
comedy pranks at times. Met a chap once whose boot-
nails had been fused to his nickel-plated stirrup-irons
and yet he remained unhurt. His horse was killed
under him, and the lightning burned a black track
from the animal’s head all down the spinal cord.
Another electric disturbance was once frisking about
a plateau where the writer was stationed, and behav-
ing like the Wild Ram of the Mountains. A bunch
of lightning playfully butted into a cottage, taking
the verandah iron in its : course, cut through the
house, knocked a fowl-house out of the perpendicular,
and finished up with a grand display of red fire on
top of a stump some distance beyond. Two women
in the house were unaware of the happening, but v
clock on the mantelpiece was struck to a standstill.
During the same storm a stump in a paddock was
struck three times within thirty minutes. Now,
what sin had the unfortunate bit of timber com-
mitted that Providence should go for it in that
whole-souled fashion ?
“Reka”: The oft-circulated report that
“Pelorus Jack” has become a dead marine is again
agitating' those that go down to the sea. in ships in
the vicinity of the French Pass (Maorila-nd). He
has not been seen for the past few weeks, and
sailormen are becoming anxious: Some months ago,
a dead white fish, about Jack’s size and something
of his build, was found stranded near Cook Strait.
He wore the same sort of clothes as Jack, but there
was no money in the pockets, so a suspicion arose
that the fish had been drinking, and a robbery had
been done. At the same time there were no marks
of violence on the remains, and no One had heard
the sound of a scuffle on the steps of the sea shore,
or a heavy splash, or the sound of retreating fins.
The deceased, so far as was known, was unmarried,
at least, his widow didn’t claim him. lie was just
about to be buried with great honor at the cost of
the parish, when Pelorous Jack returned from his
visit to the country. It was a case of mistaken
identity, and the bogus dead fish became of no ac-
count, and was thrown over a fence. The returned
Jack escorted his ships with renewed enthusiasm.
He has been protected by special Act for many
years ; ever since an idiotic Englishman, who deemed
himself a sport, shot at him and aroused a tumult
of wrath throughout the country. All seamen have
a superstitious reverence for Jack, and accept his
escort as a mark of favor and a good omen.
Should he depart all Maoriland will mourn his
departure, the tourist will no longer squander the
fare across Cook Strait for the chance of a glimpse
of one of the world’s curiosities, and piscatologists
will tear their hair over the unsolved mystery of his
species. Jack, by the way, is not the only fish
known to fame abroad. In the very early days a
huge tame shark, quite a popular favorite, was a
celebrated character in Port Nicholson, and was
widely known as “New Zealand Billy.” “This
monster,” ends an old chronicle quaintly, “was at
last killed by the enraged father of a child he had
eaten.”
