“Blue E.”: Be “Searchlight” and his
nomination of the tallewong, or black magpie, as
champion curse of the 69 (B. 15/8’I2). I consider
that the whole nine-and-sixty are contained in one
feathered carcase —that of the starling. I was for
years manager of a Victorian orchard, and had many
demonstrations of the awful damage the English im-
portation and his push could do in a few minutes.
Of cherries they would only leave the stones on the
trees, and they could hollow out prime pears until
they looked like misshapen Chinese lanterns. T can't
describe the starling’s nastiest habits here; bu: you
can take it from me that he is the hog of the air.
The vulture and the kite, those old-established corpse-
eaters, are dukes compared to him.

“Werrimee”: Bill Jones, professional ox-
eonductor, was so politically obsessed that he had
named all his team after leading members of Parlia-
ment. The near-side
bullocks were the
Government, and the
off-siders represented
the Opposition. When
there was a change in
the House he changed
the sides of his team,
and consequently had
a lot of trouble with
it for a week or two.
The new Premier and
the new Ministers took
a lot of breaking-in to
office. When he had
a good side, including
several bearing the
names of members
whose politics pleased
him, his fondest hope
was to see those mem-
bers returned at every
election. Sometimes
he took a dislike to a
politician because a
bullock bearing his
name was intractable
or a loafer, or pe r-
liaps the animal would
be named after a
Member of Parliament
who had incurred his
displeasure. In either
case the unfortunate
brute would get more
than his share of whip
and abuse, and the
driver “would see him
• pole-axed before ever
he- would get a vote
from him again.”
When the member he

feated, the driver
would wave his hat
joyfully and cry:
“Hooray! you’ve got
kicked out, you .wast-
er 1 Now you can go
to the beef cask.” And 10 to one the ex-M.P. would
be fattened up and killed.
“N. or M.” : The seeds of many Austra-
lian plants, which germinate the better for being
soaked in boiling water before planting, appear to
have been specially provided with thick overcoats to
enable them to put up a fight against glacial epochs,
the Day of Judgment or any old thing that may
happen along. I have seen healthy clematis raised
from seed 20 years old, and a bush fire always helps
wattle seed to sprout. The most striking instance I
have known of the long-enduring quality of the latter
was in the case where a ruined sixty-year-old hut
had been pulled down and the remains scattered
over the adjacent landscape. No wattles had been
growing in the vicinity for many years, but a fine
crop immediately sprang up from the seeds which
had been long dormant in the mud plaster
of the walls. The growth of rushes where none
have been known before, along the banks of artesian
water courses is more likely to be due to the agency
of birds than to the fact that the seeds have been in
the ground since a time prior to the formation of
the present inside arrangements of the continent.
“F.A.T.”: I want to mention crocodiles, if
“Mallarapoonie” will excuse my being on the earth.
He states (B. 25/7/T2) that the croc, never touches
feathered game. He does. A party of us were out
shooting some time ago, at Lake Chappaginia, about
15 miles from Cape York, and left a dead scrub
turkey, and a mallee hen, likewise gone to Heaven,
on the bank, some distance above the lagoon. On
our returning to pick up the birds, we found them
missing, and investigation showed a crocodile’s
tracks leading to and from the spot. To say that a
fresh-water croc, does not measure more than 7ft. is
to handle truth in a light-hearted and altogether too
careless fashion. In the North-West, crocodiles of
various sizes, up to 15 or 17 feet, travel over miles
of scrubby country to fresh-water lagoons, where
they live on pigs, dingoes, or any old thing that
happens along to quench the dryness of its interior.
One day I noticed a Binghi on the banks, who imi-
tated the howl of a dingo to perfection. His
black female consort stood by with a gun, ready to
get a shot at the crocodile, when it became hungry
enough, or sufficiently interested in the commotion,
to put in an appearance. According to the Bing’s
statement, and allowing for his vague idea of figures,
he had often captured crocs. 12ft. in length in the
vicinity of fresh water. I know from personal ex-
perience that it is dangerous to cross some of the
swamps, miles away from salt water, for fear of fall-
ing over crocodiles, some of which are as large as a
piece of wood, and others almost the size of a log.
It is also a most foolish business to remark in print
that small crocs, do not live in the sea. It is
a common sight to see them at Newcastle Bay, a few
miles from the most northern point of Australia, and
at Thursday Island Bill Brown saw thousands one
night, from a foot upwards, crawling along the
surface of Torres Strait.

EASY ON THE BEDDING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His name was Herb, he was a yob,
A bush-bred youth of twenty-eight.
He hung on to his “stiddy job”
With Goatee Winge at Pennyweight.
Da.v laborer they called the wight,
But Jimmy also worked at night.
“Keep goin’, lad,” old Goatee said,
And Jimmy’s iiours were long and wide.
He got in at one side the bed,
And straight got out the other side;
For ever Goatee firmly stressed
His kindly rule, “A good night’s rest.”
“Oh, yes,” said Herb, “blokes come ’n’ go,
But I hang on all right somehow.
A chap still holds his own, you know.
I’ve been with good ole Goatee now
So many years I ’most forget,
And ain’t wore out me blankets yet.”
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.
Wanted: A Roomy Allotment.
A Heturned Traveller contributes this: —
Just after Japan had successfully swallowed Korea
and bagged Manchuria’s southern end the United
States Government approached Marquis Komura, then
Jap Minister for Foreign Affairs, with a proposal for
a friendly limitation of the flow of Japanese emi-
gants to 'the Pacific coast The Marquis was sympa-
thetic, and announced to both Japan and the U.S.A.
that there was now no need for Japanese emigrants,
who were crowded out of old Nippon by the internal
pressure of numbers, to cross the Pacific. There was
unlimited room for them in the newly-acquired ter-
ritories on the Asiatic mainland. So the arrange-
ment was made. Japanese emigration Was diverted
to Korea and Southern Manchuria, where it could be
of service to the great Imperial idea and help to
build up a bulwark against the Slav, who, everybody
knew, would one day come again. Now Japan finds
that Korea and Manchuria are not the ideal spots
they were thought to be. When the Jap emigrant
went to Western U.S.A. he could always get a living
because he could take a lower wage than the general
population j so it happened that most of the Jap
emigrants to the Pacific Slope were of the laboring
c l ass —the material of which the rank-and-file of the
army are made, and the sort of men \vhich should
be useful in building up the new Nippon on the
mainland of Asia. But bitter experience has proved
that there is no room in Manchuria or Korea for
that sort of Jap. Komura’s idea was to settle these
territories with farmers who would be part peasant
proprietors and part tenants of estates held by large
Japanese companies. Immediately Jap possession ut
the peninsula was established a process of changing
the land from the possession of Korean into that ot
Japanese owners was commenced. Someone high in
office in Nippon had evidently read about how Wil-
liam of Normandy worked the same trick in Eng-
land, and he improved on it, for in about six years
practically the whole of the land of Korea parsed
into possession of people with Japanese names. That
is probably a record. But the trouble was that there
was no room for the Jap-laborer, that good fighting
man He could not compete with the dispossessed
Korean owner, who had to take to healthy toil for
the means to live. And, somehow, the Jap did not
seem to take kindly to the farming life. He became
a rack-renter and a sweater, and many of him re-
turned to Japan and lived on what was extorted by
an agent from the wretched Korean tenants. The
Jap land-owner did not care overmuch for tenants of
his own race either—they could not be squeezed and
sweated to the same extent as could the conquered
and juggled Korean, who usually yielded about 40
per cent, return on invested capital. Of course, all
Government servants are Japanese, and most of the
trade is in the hands of the dominant race. the
Jap exploiter remains in Korea, but the Japanese
working man has departed. There is no room tor
him. When he went to Korea to toil he struck a
cheaper worker than himself. In Southern Man-
churia the situation is even worse. As Manchuria n
still nominallv Chinese property, and there is still the
possibility of a fight, the transfer process could not be
worked, and at farming the Japanese is not in the
same paddock as the steady-going Chinaman. So
the Jap did not go on the Manchurian land. Neither
is the Jap coolie able to compete with the Chinese.
In Korea, all the bookkeeping, etc., is done by
Japanese clerks, but not so in Manchuria, except m
Japanese houses. The Chinaman is a better man in
every respect, a harder worker, more honest, more
reliable, and more capable. At trade the Japanese
merchant and trader find themselves overshadowed
by the Chinaman, who seems to be a better business
man and to be able to undersell his island relative
all the time. The result is that of the 43,000 Japan-
ese in Manchuria, no less than 3000 are workers on
and about the raUway, and the bulk of the others
seem to live on them and on the Japanese army of
occupation. The Japs in Manchuria live by taking
in each other’s washing, except when a Jap blacklegs
and sends his laundry to the cheaper Chinese wash-
emporium, so to speak; and there is no empire-ex-
pansion business about it. Manchuria and Korea are
failures as outlets for the alleged teeming millions of
Nippon, who are said to be crowding one another
into the water. Other outlets have been sought for.
A Japanese paper announced recently that Japan
would take on the r6le of Protector of sundry Central
and Southern American republics against the tyranny
and arrogance of the United States, in return for
concessions of land to Japanese settlers; but when a
start was made at Magdalena Bav, in Mexico, the
tyrannical United States put both its American hoofs
on the idea while it was young. So the problem of
finding an outlet for the surplus Jap population re-
mains' unsolved. What is wanted is a large, healthy,
thinly-peopled land, with a good climate, a decent
rainfall, and far enough away from home to make
return at the first disappointment too difficult to be
lightly undertaken. Korea and Manchuria have been
tried and proved unsuitable. The islands scattered
about the Pacific are not large enough. South and
Central America are sealed up, to be opened only by
a long and desperate struggle. But there is one
other place that fills the bill to a nicety. In fact, it
seems to have been made expressly to fill the bill.
And that Andrew Fisher has gone Cold about build-
ing the direct railway by which it could be made
much less inviting has no doubt been carefully noted
for future reference in the interested quarter. It is
time Australia did something to make Andrew
Fisher realise that it regards the matter as serious.

“Silas Snell”: Bill and .Tim, harvest
hands and farm laborers, are being brought into
unions, and literally dragged into a way of better-
ing their condition, and they needed dragging.
William and James are in some sense responsible for
the rapacity of the Boss Cocky in submitting for so
long to whatever conditions and exactions Hayseed
cared to impose. I was staying at Josiah Kidd’s
for a fortnight, and early in the proceedings began
to take notice of Bill. Bill was a long, gawky,
shambling, red-headed, freckled youth of 22, with a
dromedary nose and disproportionate feet. Bill was
always doing. Tf I got up early T found Bill hard
at it; if I went to bed late, I left Bill among the
shattered harness, or skinning a vast tub of spuds
for the early breakfast. Possibly Bill went to bed,
and there may have been moments when he had no-
thing to do ; but if so these things escaped my ob-
servation. The dull placidity of Bill in pursuit of
his daily round of ignoble tasks irritated me. I
wanted to kick him, and might have done so had
he not been seven sizes too large. Eventually I
spoke to him about his shameful conduct. “WK
don’t you get out of this?” I said. Bill thought it
over. “Wha’ ’s the go-o-o-od?” he said. “Ole Jos,
lie ain’t so ba-a-a-d, yeh lcno-o-w. He gi’s me a few
bob an’ me keep fer jist hangin’ ro-o-ond, ’n’ he’s
promised first reg’lar job that turns up he’ll put me
o-o-on.” I often wonder if Bill got his “reg'lar
job.”

W. J. Russell, who is to oppose Bruce
femith tor Parkes at next general election,
ws born on the Maeleay River 33 years ago.
He stayed there for 14 years, won a bursary
and attended Sydney Grammar School for
three years, and was then offered the glitter-
ing stipend of 2s. 6d. per week to sit on a
high stool. He declined coldly, and appren-
ticed himself to the fragrant and better-paid
job ot plumbing, from which he rose, ulti-
mately. to the dizzy height of president of
the union of Finders-of-leaks-in-pipes. Rus-
sell has been chairman of the Parkes Federal
1..L.C. since that body started active opera-
tions, and at the. recent Referendum cam-
paign he worked like five men and a boy to
secure a “Yes” vote. If he can work like
nine men and five boys, he may bump the
black-labor Spruce Myth person hard enough
to hurt.

A corpse—not that of George Reid—found
the other day in the River Lea (England),
had two words tattooed on the arm, “Yes”
and “No.” Presumably he was a person of
undecided mind, and the << Yes ,J meant that
he was going to drown himself, and the “No”
meant that he wasn’t, and while he was hesi-
tating on the shore he fell in.

What one might, in the language of poetic
imagery, term the wedding of December and
New Year’s Day occurred at Rockhampton
(Q.) recently, when a gentleman of 67 was
wedded to a lady of 79. The thing was a
manage de convenance pure and simple.
Both bridegroom and bride are old-age pen-
sioners, and they reckoned they could live
more cheaply under one roof than separately.
The marriage saves the price of a roof.
“Melb.”: The triangular duel between
Weedon, Farthing and Opitz, in the East
Melbourne State election, roused unusual ex-
citement, particularly through Farthing hav-
ing been unseated on Weedon’s petition. All
three are connected with the hotel trade.
Weedon’s lately deceased father owned and
kept a prosperous Carlton' pub. Farthing
has kept the Austral, in Bourke-street. Opitz
keeps the Golden Fleece, in Russell-street.
Farthing is ex-president of the Licensed
Victuallers’ Association, and Opitz is ex-
secretary. Opitz was a variety show mana-
ger froni 1890 to 1902, taking vaudeville
companies to India, and associated with
Tommy Hudson. Farthing was long a State
schoolmaster, and enthusiastic cricketer and
footballer. He is decidedly the most intel-
lectual of the Beer party. Weedon used to be
a contractor, and was Mayor of Melbourne
from 1905 to 1908.
Port Augusta (S.A.) is now a place with
a future the size of a whale’s family, for
there the railway from Perth eastward and
the one from Port Darwin southward will
meet. But it had to wait a long time for
its crown. When the R.C. Diocese of Port
Augusta was created, the rush for the mitre,
on the part of the clerics, was so mild as to
be indiscernible with telescopes. For ex-
ample, Dr. Kelly, now parish priest of North
litzroy, on being menaced with the distinc-
tion, remarked acidly that he didn’t want to
end his days in Siberia. Ultimately, the
priests of those regions (a mere mitreful, so
to speak) met and selected Father John
-Norton no relation of the journalist, by
the way, but a Victorian boy, educated at
St. 1 at. s, Melbourne, by the Jesuits. And
now. The first sod of the Transcontinental
Railway has been turned at Port Augusta,
and old Siberia will lose its name and fame.
When John Norton wants a coadjutor, let
him send to North Fitzroy, and he may find
one there—a disappointed old man who skied
his birthright for a mess of pottage.
THE NORTHERN TERRITORY
DEVELOPMENT.
Sixty deer are shortly to be taken to Coburg Pen-
insula, m the Northern Territory; 50 of these have
been offered to the Department of External Affairs
by a lady resident at Armidale, and the Melbourne
Zoological Gardens will supply the remainder. The
consignment will leave for the Territory about Oc-
tober 1. The deer will probably be in charge of Mr.
Le Souef, curator of the Melbourne Zoological Gar-
dens, whose services have been asked ior.—Staqnerinn
news item. J
A memo, from the Barrier:—
Went to his last shift, at Broken Hill, Thomas G.
Sweet, manager of the B. H. South Extended. He
was born at Cornwall (Eng.) 56 years ago, and was
connected with mining almost from infancy. Got
a very early start in the mines of Cornwall (they get
m as mere babies there), but moved across B fo
Mexico and South America, and afterwards to Spain
Then he _ went to the West Coast of Africa, from
which, with two companions, he made inland. Three
went m and one came back. Left there (it was a
good place to leave) and worked in Transvaal mines
till he was appointed to Westralia. In 1895 he went
across to Broken Hill, where he was put in charge of
the Australian B. H. Consols, just before the finding
oi the slug of native silver which made the mine
famous. When the Consols closed down and took
over the new B. H. South Extended, he was ap-
pointed manager. After that his record was one of
hard digging and blasting and shovelling.

A SURVIVOR.
Speaking at the Million Club luncheon yesterday
Mr. Trefle declared that a timber famine was closer
than was generally realised. “We should protect our
magnificent forests before it is too late," he said “by
re-afforestation.”
The last lone emetyp!—saved for seed.
Archdeacon Nash, of Victoria, has met
with practically everything except the public
subscription, and that will probably come.
He was recruiting with his family at'Austin-
mer, on the N.S.W. South Coast, and one
night, as he sat with his wife on the bal-
cony, a terrified Mary Anne rushed at them
with her mouth open and the news that the
wooden calamity was in flames. The chil-
dren were thrown out; Mary Anne was
thrown out; and Mr. and Mrs. Archdeacon
threw themselves out. Even the archh
diaconal coat was burned to the ground. The
tinpot town of Austinmer—population one
dog—has been thrilled to its foundations, and
has ever since lifted its beer with a totter-
ing hand.
Died quietly in his sleep, at an Adelaide
hotel, Charles Wallace, journalist, aged
somewhere about 40. He had been on the
Numurkah (Vic.) Standard and the Port
Augusta (S.A.) Dispatch, but had latterly
been working up newspapers on Kangaroo
Island and the lonely but progressive stretch
of country which S.A. calls its “west coast.”

THE UBIQUITY OF BEER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rev. R. Ditterich, of Wesley Church,
Melbourne, who is bidding for the position of
Grand Master of the Associated Wowsers, in the
course of a Sunday sermon fiercely attacked Farth-
ing, the East Melb. candidate, because he was pro-
fessionally associated with beer. Ditterich shed his
benediction on little Weedon. Farthing, in response,
pointed out that he was a publican, the Labor can-
didate, Opitz, was another, and Weedon was born in
a pub, bred in a pub, and was now one of the biggest
shareholders in a local brewery.
The churchman loathes the boose, and he
Still struggles to accentuate
This terrible antipathy
Between the pewter and the plate.
Ilis fervid condemnation rolls
On men who sell the drinks that cheer ;
With equal fervor he extols
Those seeming strict and sober souls
Who are not barracking for beer.
Alas, alack, it’s very rough,
r I he man the parsons so acclaim
For touching not the cursed stuff
Has drink connections just the same.
He won’t announce it from a tub,
But beer is in his family;
He’s second cousin to a pub, '
Or father to a whisky club,
Or nephew to a brewery!
The shameful feature of our day y"°
By Ditterich is expounded here.
The churches, trying as they may,
Cannot divorce themselves from beer.
They scorn one man with holy pride
To give his foe approval fat,
And shout his virtues far and wide,
Then find they’ve cast the pint aside,
But only to embrace the vat.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

Rev. Percy Blanky Hurrah, who is at present
stationed at St. Peter’s, Mud Flat, has received a
call to St. Paul’s, Golden Square.
—Frequent news item.
We labor in the Vineyard for
The glory of the Lord ;
A meek, self-sacrificing corps,
And know that when the Fight is o’er
We'll gain our Great Reward.
With Ancient Nick we scrap each day,
»And strive to vanquish Sin.
But do not seek for place or pay ;
They lie, those godless ones, who say
That we are after “tin.”
Each Shepherd labors hard to fill
St.. Peter's or St. Paul’s;
For each one loves his flock; nor will
He dream of leaving them—until
The gentle oof-Jbird calls!

This company keeps its accounts in a complicated
fashion, which is somewhat irritating. Thus it has
five reserves of one sort or another, also a profit and
loss balance. Four of the reserves are fed by the
open and formal transfer of amounts from profit and
loss, while the renewal and replacement fund just
grows. In order to make the accounts appear square the
amount of this mysterious growing is included in the
profits as shown above, and any amount by which it
may happen to shrivel is deducted. The co. has a paper
or goodwill item of £35,257 10s. lid., representing,
apparently, the amount which it paid to the Brisbane
Electric Supply Co. when it bought out that original
institution. This is an item which is represented
by no visible assets, and when the co.’s franchise
expires, in the year 1940, it will not be allowed for ;
so £lOOO a year ( £6OOO up to date) is set aside
to extinguish it. After the cumbersome manner of
the corporation, this £6OOO appears on one side of
the accounts and £35,257 10s. lid. on the other.
An enterprise which had a better idea of the bless-
ings and advantages of tidiness would vote £1257
10s. lid. one year towards the extinction of its
paper security, and thus take off the ragged fringe of
shillings and pence. Also, it would abolish the cumber-
some “Franchise and Purchase Sinking Fund Account,”
and simply wipe so much every year off the nominal
value of “Purchase Account.” Or else it would, as
soon as the miscellaneous reserves are sufficient, wipe
out the whole paper item and start afresh. Again,
it would look more orderly not to have three ac-
counts —two credits and an overdraft—at the E.S.
and A. Bank. And the Dividend Equalisation item
ought to be abolished. This sort of item ought
always to be abolished. When a company in any one
period pays a larger dividend than it has earned, and
draws on its reserves to do it, it doesn’t matter much
whether the reserve it draws upon is called “Divi-
dend Equalisation” or anything else.
The position has improved considerably within the
last 12 months. The business is growing- in a steadv
fashion; the liquid assets remain about the same';
and the overdraft has been reduced within a year
from nearly £6OOO to less than £3OO. The profits
are the largest to date. Unless Brisbane proves
an unexpected failure, the shareholders should find
themselves, when their lease expires, on as good a
wicket as the shareholders of the Melbourne Tramway
Co. The reserves ( £32,466) are still, of course,
purely nominal, because they haven’t quite overtaken
the paper or goodwill item of £35,257 10s. lid.,
but in another half-year there should be some genuine
reserves, and after that a period of steady accumu-
lation should set in.
* * * *
The City of Sydney is in the market
hunting for a loan of £1,000,000. It wanted
£1,500,000, but had to reduce its demands, owing
to the hunger in the money shop, and even then
it had to go to England for half the money. And
it is reckoned that, including interest and discount,
the cost will not be much less than 5 p.c. p.a. The
reason for the difficulty in raising the wind is obvious
enough. Owing to the combination of high wages
and low tariff, so many cheap-labor goods are coming
m that a fearsome heap of money has to go
out to pay for them. And as a result there was
£10,100,000 less cash in the Australian banks at
June 30, 1912, than at June 30, 1911. The lia-
bilities of the banks to the public increased during
the year by £61,801. Their advances—amounts lent
out to help embarrassed customers along, and avert
the slump, and possibly avert the crash —increased
by £13,438,529, which was something heroic. Their
liquid securities diminished by £1,467,299 —that is
to say, they have been turning securities into cash.
And yet, as already mentioned, their suoplv of cash
has diminished by over £10,100,000. All this money
has gone to pay for cheap-labor imports. At a time
of good seasons, good crops, and splendid prices, Aus-
tralia is steering direct for a great smash, simplv
through its neglect to keep the tariff in some sort
of fair relationship with the difference between Aus-
tralian and English wages. So, naturally, the City of
Sydney has got to pay a disastrous price for its
loan. Money is scarce, because so much of it has
gone away to pay for sweaters’ goods, imported
under the anti-sweating Labor Government to pre-
vent the local Labor elector getting a job; and when
money is scarce it has to be paid for in proportion
to its scarcity. The Labor elector, who has lost his
l°b through the influx of sweaters’ goods, carries his
share of the extra interest bill as a matter of course.
. The position of the City of Sydney is interesting
m a way. That shambling, untidy settlement, with
its narrow streets and hopeless alleys, is being gradu-
ally reconstructed. The unimproved capital value of the
city is about £20,000,000. At the cost of £20,000,000,
or 100 p.c. on the ground value, the place may be
made moderately respectable, though it may 'cost
more than that to make it really modern and up to
date. It would be a vast amount cheaper to con-
struct, at places like Port Stephens and Jervis Bay
and Twofold Bay, new State-owned cities which
would be laid out on the best-known principles, and
would require no immensely expensive re-construc-
tion, and would be up to date from their very incep-
tion. This has long been a matter of urgency, but
now that the money required for the re-construction
of the private-landlord-owned city of Sydney is be-
coming so hard to get, and the cost of it is becoming
so heavy, the matter grows still more urgent. The
bill for congestion is becoming more than the com-
munity can stagger under.

Tasmania taxes the surplus profits as well as the
dividends, and as the profits move up and down a 10
p.c. div. and tax may be smaller one year than
another. The above is a very poor showing
—the profit figures are the lowest on the
table, and that dates back to 1903. Also,
after paying the 10 p.c. div. the amount put
by to reserves is the smallest, with the exception of
1903, when, however, £713 was written off. Then
the “Commisions, interest and rent” item has
slumped from £3350 as at June, 1910, and £3085
as at June, 1911, to £2412 for the past half-year.
With these three items lumped together it is impos-
sible to say which of them is responsible for the big
decrease. It is not likely that rents have gone down
much—property stands in the accounts for exactly
the same as last year. So the only conclusion to
come to is that interest and commissions have
dropped, and that isn’t at all satisfactory for a
trustee co. Sundry creditors total £ 12,553, as
against £13,341 last year; and sundry debtors are
£19,262, as against £20,392 in 1911. There is
nothing to indicate what thesp creditors are, though
it is pretty certain that a great bulk of them are
loans, but for what purpose isn’t stated. If they are
loans, then the capital has to guarantee them as well
as the estates, and the Father of Three will look
twice before he puts his affairs into the hands of a
co. which can’t give trust estates all its attention.
However, as is mostly the case wifjh modern trustee
cos. there is a large uncalled capital to back up its
clients. Also, with a small paid capital, dividends
don t weigh so heavily on the co.’s shoulders, so the
reserves are not sacrificed to the sake of big divi-
dends. Deposits, etc., total £6857, loans on mort-
gage £5525, and property £3258, so the co.’s assets
are practically all liquid.

It is a complicated situation, and only an in-
curable optimist would describe the method of pre-
senting the accounts as satisfactory. This co-
operative concern has so much difficulty in getting
its co-operative shareholders to co-operate that it
levies a fine of £1 on every shareholder who doesn’t
buy goods to the value of £l2 per annum. This
amount is deducted from the shareholders’ capital.
So it comes to pass that in two years the society
has forfeited £7079 of capital, and" after paying no
dividend since the beginning of 1908 it contrives,
with all this forfeited capital, to have £2068 of
reserves. During the past half-year these have been

And there are cheerless remarks in the report.
Especially it is mentioned that the society tried to
sell its premises at auction, but the highest bid
( £26,000) wasn’t satisfactory. Also, that it has
sold off its drapery stock in one act—perhaps be-
cause its genius didn’t incline towards drapery—and
proposes to start a furniture department instead.
Perhaps its genius won’t incline towards furniture
either.
The trouble seems to be that, from the very first,
this concern has tried to carry too vast a load of
real estate on too little business, and on insufficient
capital. Consequently its shareholders have grown
dispirited. At January, 1909, it had 13,283 share-
holders ; now it has 8334. Its sales reached the top
m January, 1908, when they were £82,676; now
they are £67,505. It is an improvement on the
figures of the four previous half-years (the sales got
down as low as £64,106), but it isn’t a vlrv
cheerful figure all the same. The net profits, accord-
ing to the society’s own calculation (which doesn’t
quite square with The Bulletin’s), were £3508 at
""'W 190 / ; now, as shown above, they are only

than they are to-day. No dividend has been paid

drapery job, and proposes to start a furniture iob.

Co-operative Credit Bank. It has an interest in
the Zeehan (Tas.) Co-operative Society. In fact, it
seems to have everything except some ready cash and
a good solid, unpretentious, condensed business.

In some respects these accounts are very unsatisfac-

is wholly unexplained. When £3229 4s. 7d., or id.

only method by which the table of figures can be
made to look coherent is by adding that much to the'
profits. Which is the reason why the last balance-
sheet declares the net profit as £7212, while The
Bullets puts it down as £11,112. The attempt
to distinguish between ordinary and preference divi-
dend has been abandoned. At December, 1911 the
paid capital was £76,220 (the balance-sheet some-

something- not mentioned p.c. Also a bonus was de-
clared, but that was mixed up with the dividend and
bonus on the ordinary shares. Apparently this hash
represents somewhere between 9 and 10 p.c. all
round, but life is short and finance is long, and the

* * * *
The N.S.W. Government has realised,
rather late m the day, that it was a clumsy and cur-
sory community when it devised a Gas Bill, under
which the_ Sydney Gas Co. would pay a rigidly re-
stricted dividend, on rather less than the amount
actually put up by shareholders, while the North
Shore Co. and the Newcastle Co. and others could
turn their reserves into capital, and pav dividends on
a great deal more money than the shareholders actu-
ally paid up. So now there is a scmiie to amend the
Bill, so as to provide that divs. will only be pay-
able on the amount of the paid-up capital as it stood
when the measure was introduced. Even that arrange-
ment won’t hold the balance even between the co
which had, in previous years, transferred some of its
reserves to capital, and the one which had trans-
ferred some of its premium capital to reserves. It
is all a muddle, like the famous Income Tax Bill
which gave the landlord, and even the absentee land-
lord, a huge advantage over the little manufacturing
company that made a mere scrap of profit by em-
Pmymg white Australians at white men’s wages.
That attempt, by an alleged Labor Government to
give special privileges to the most objectionable kind
ot landlord, has never been explained to this day, and
quite a lot of people would like to know what lay
behind it. 3

The position can’t be described as a strong one The
deposits have_ increased very largely in recent years
whUe the capital has not. There are no liquid assets.
Ail the capital, the very moderate and rather dubious
reserves, the deposits and a trifle in the way of bank
overdraft, are buried in the business, and it isn’t the
sort of business out of which much money can be
rescued at very short notice, should a demand set
in. The directorate seems a sanguine combination.
It has certainly made a good deal of allowance for
depreciation since 1899, hut what it calls its reserves
are actually less than they were in that year. At a
time when the boom isn’t looking nearly so healthy
as it did, and when it may look worse almost any day,
the addition of £ll4 to the visible savings is no
lavish provision for the future. The profits are in-
creasing, but they are not increasing fast. The net
income from properties is described as £461. Now
if the “amount allowed for depreciation” is meant
to appear as a real reserve, and not a sum that has
already been swallowed up, these properties stand at
.£15,053, and the return is £3 Is. 3d. p.c. It is
impossible to take that sort of valuation quite seri-
ously. _ And if the “amount allowed for depreciation”
is written off, then the properties are valued at
£12,872, and they return £3 11s. Bd. p.c. In that
case, the alleged reserves are reduced to a humble
£3586, and still the properties, look over-valued.
On the evidence of the figures it looks as if the
Newcastle and County Mutual Building, Land and
Investment Co., Limited, would be a very lucky insti-
tution if it sold its freeholds and leaseholds at their
alleged value, and then put the money into Govern-
ment securities.

The liquid assets might be made to look a little
better by including: £lOO worth of furniture, but as the
society can’t sell its desk and do business on the floor
the furniture is hardly worth considering. The in-
stitution has a big margin on paper between its
assets and its liabilities, but everything depends on
circumstances. The assets may depreciate—they are the
sort that often depreciate badly after a land boom,
unless the lender has been a confirmed pessimist. The
liabilities won’t depreciate. The assets are not of
the liquid sort, and if much of the liabilities are
liquid, and hard-up banks or other lenders begin to
want their money in haste, a lot of things may
happen even to a concern with so wide a margin of
security as this one.

There used to be an electric light company, as well
as this gas co., in the Towers, but the town wasn’t big
enough to support two families, so the gas co. bought
out its rival. The price paid was £6500, of which
£5OOO came from the bank, and, by keeping off divi-
dends for a few years, the co. has reuuced this
plaster to £2034. Gas sales are not what they
should be. From £2629, as at June, 1911, they
have dropped to £2420, as at June, 1912. The
revenue from the electric light branch has been
moving the same way, and has shrunk from £1428,
at June, ’ll, to £1372, for the half-year to June, ’l2.
After providing for depreciation, etc., this leaves a
profit for the electric light section of £242, as com-
pared with £239 for the previous six months. The
co. certainly doesn’t lead a riotous life; though, if
the gas sales keep up, shareholders will be much
better off when the whole of the overdraft is pushed
right off the doorstep.
* * * *
The customs smelting idea of the N.S.W.
Mines _ and Smelting Go. has fallen into the soup
with just the miserable splash that was to be ex-
pected (B. 22/2/’l2). The proposal now is to
option the mine at Breadalbane to an intermediate
company, having a capital of £15,000 in £lO
shares. The purchase price is £3OOO, and one-tenth
fully paid interest in any sufficiently optimistic co.
that can eventually be found to take over the pro-
perty. A condition of the option is that £320 per
month is to be paid by the intermediate company for
the first six months, such sum to be credited against
the purchase price. There is no mention of what
happens* if the option is not exercised.
Another step has been taken in the dreary
negotiations between the Mount Drysdale and the
New Eldorado (N.S.W.). Both these cas. have been
dodging labor conditions for some time past, on the
pretext of hatching a beneficial scheme of amalga-
mation. The directors of the former concern now
state that after “great and serious consideration,”
they dislike the recommendations of the committee
appointed to go into the question. The suspension
expires in November next, and something must be
done. A new plant is wanted to treat the alleged
large body of payable ore in Mount Drysdale, but
the co. is in debt. The efforts to option the mine
to an English company have so far proved unsuccess-
ful. A re-construction or a voluntary liquidation is
suggested.
Y Water tin co. (N.S.W.) was suspected
of being a failure when it first started work, because
borehole tests averaged (o.Blb. to 0.91 b.) less than
lib. per cubic yard. Last half-year’s average was
1.3171 b. from 333,000 cubic yards. The dividends
declared to date total £26,000. Since the start
1,528,000 cubic yards have been sluiced for 865
tons of tin oxide, showing an average yield of
1.2691 b. per cubic yard.
*' * *
The Homeward Bound, at Yalwal
(N.S.W.), crushed 1330 tons for £l7OO. There is
a quarry 100 ft. deep, and the rock throughout is
gold-bearing, but a great deal of it is low-grade.
That is a matter of history, for it isn’t by any means
the first time that this mass of low-grade auriferous
rock has been worked. Costs have been cut down to
ss. or ss. 6d. per ton, which is practicallv the same
rate as the Alaska Treadwell (U.S.A.). There costs
are $1.22 per ton (nearly 55.) for an enormous out-
put. Recently the Homeward Bound returns have
been running from 20s. to 255. per ton of ore. This
comparatively high .yield is accounted for by the
fact that the crushing stuff is being taken from a part
of the mine near to which some rich yields were ob-
tained 20 years since. There is a big body of pay-
able stone, and 40 head of stampers are at work.
No slimes plant yet.

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. A saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow i6in. by 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

to dry, and smelters are particular about moisture,
but the delay in realising on lead and zinc concen-
trates has been fortunate. The management continues
to regret the shortage of miners, but the output is
large in proportion to the quantity of ore known to
exist in the mine. A subject for genuine regret is
that the mine has been (since No. 9 level was
opened) depleted of 607,829 tons of ore for such
an inadequate profit (dividends, £79,500). That
stupid kind of result has been achieved by forcing
production when lead and silver were selling at low
prices. The share market has been taken care of at
the expense of the mine.
Lead statistics are of interest now the
price is unusually high. For August, Britain im-
ported 16,050 tons of lead, compared with 14,356
tons in July, 1912, and 20,697 tons in August, 1911.
For eight months, British lead imports total 138,651
tons, compared with 150,013 tons for first eight
months of 1911. Germany has sprung into second
place as a consumer of lead, following United States,
with Britain third, so British imports are not such a
good guide as they used to be. Britain bought
38,684 tons of Australian lead in the first eight
months of this year, an increase of only 3797 tons
compared with eight months of the previous year.
In Germany there is a decided preference for lead
concentrates, so it cannot be wondered at that the
Broken Hill Prop., for six months to May 31, sent
16,149 tons of soft lead to India, China and Japan,
while only 21,155 tons were shipped to Europe.
* * * *
Mount Molloy (O.) lias decided to recon-
struct into a No Liability Company of £150,000, in
£1 shares, on a basis of share for share. The 50,000
preference shares are to be exchanged for fully-paids,
and the 100,000 ordinaries for shares paid up to 14s.
The debts of the old co. amount to £9177, so these
and the liquidation expenses will make a fair cut
out of the £30,000 to be raised. In addition to the
cash resources of £166, the co. has four assets.
There is the railway, which it wants to sell to the
Queensland Government, and can’t, as the latter
already has too many railway projects on hand, and
there is a timber concession that doesn’t seem to pro-
duce much coin, judging from the accounts sub-
mitted. The third asset is the mine and smelter, re-
moved 60 miles by rail from Cairns, and the fourth
is a large fund of hopefulness that the third can be
made to yield a profit now that copper has attained
fairly giddy heights. The mine closed down when
copper only brought £55 to £6O per ton, and when
the supply of basic ore gave out. This latter fact
doesn’t seem to be mentioned. There are two parallel
lodes —the western, which is known as the main ore
body, and the eastern. The former has been worked
to 37_0ft. in depth, the latter to 470 ft. The proposal
now is to drive south at the No. 1 level of the east
lode, and to open up the main ore body from 370 ft.
to 470 ft. Also to sink the main shaft a further
100 ft., and open up both the main and east lodes to
the 670 ft. levels. There is not much to do it on, and
the returns from the 24,000 tons of 3 p.c. copper ore
in sight are not likely to swell the coffers to any ex-
tent, if at all. Some sort of mechanical concentra-
tion will be attempted prior to smelting. The report,
though dressed as nicely as possible, seems to have
the rattle of dry bones. This paper hopes for the
future, but doesn’t carry a big swag of expectations.
The boom in metals is some comfort to
battling Chillagoe (Q.). For three months to Sep-
tember 13, 10,738 tons have been smelted for 327
tons of copper, 478 tons of lead, 39,5890 z. silver and
27680 z. gold. Compared with the first two months
of the current half-year there has been a considerable
decline in the quantity of ore. In April, 5643 tons
were smelted for 242 tons of copper, 331 tons of
lead and 24,9570 z. silver. Last month’s return from
3716 tons ore was: 61 tons copper (10 days’ smelt-
ing), 297 tons lead (28 days’ smelting), and
20,7890 z. silver. The Einasleigh mine is supplying
most of the copper ore, and the Lady Duff (Stan-
nary Hills), Crooked Creek and Etheridge mines
supply lead ore. Muldiva and Mungana are also pro-
ducing on _ a limited scale. Reserves are not large
at Einasleigh (71,242 tons), but the mine has yet
to be opened up below No. 4 level. At Mungana, the
Lady Jane mine is still on fire, but the jet of water
at high pressure playing on the heated ore is cooling
the old stopes.

Hampden (Cloncurry) contemplates a
big move. Though only starting with its large new
plant to delve into the ore-reserves at the Hampden
and Duchess, this co., like its powerful, neighbor the
Elliott, has been picking up development shows
wherever a decent one offered. Lack of eash has
been a serious drawback. The recent spurt in shares
indicated that something was doing behind the
scenes. The proposal now comes forward that the
capital be increased to £400,000 by the creation of
100,000 new shares of £1 each. B’g game is being
sought, as an option of purchase has been secured
over the McGregor (Cloncurry) as a going concern.
Shares in the latter company likewise spurted, until
the market has nut a valuation of about £lOO.OOO
on the mine. This seems an excessive price, and, if
the Hampden pays it (there is no mention of price
in the official circular), the profit on the McGregor’s
8 p.c. copper ore, which will have to be transported
25 miles to the Malbon-Duchess line, is evidently
estimated at about £1 per ton. This, of course,
takes no account of the ore to be discovered, or of
the low-grade stuff already developed in the McGre-
gor group. As stated before, the Hampden financial
nosition is too weak for a venture of this kind, but
the milk in the cocoanut lies in the second resolu-
tion, to be put before shareholders at the extraor-
dinary meeting on October 8. This is that, subject
to the increase of capital being decided on, the direc-
tors be confirmed in their acceptance of an offer from
Lionel Robinson Clark and Go. for the sale to them
of 50,000 shares at 50s. per share, with the right to
call for 50,000 shares within six months, at 565. per
share, the commission being 5 p.c. This option is
considerably in advance of Baillieu’s 50,000 at par.
On the assumption that the capital is increased to
£400,000, as shown, the Hampden will have over
£300,000 to splash around in its dream of Cloncurry
conquest. The smelter is now daily adding to the
coffers, and should early extinguish the debts out-
standing. With its team of Hampden, Duchess,
McGregor, Trekelano, Mascotte, Scalper and Answer,
the co. should be carried a long way towards that
dream.
* * * *
The Cloncurry Warden reports in an in-
structive manner about the Argylla group of mines,
some of which have been for many years held as a
freehold. The Warden refers to the sinking of a
shaft on one of the ore lenses which is typical of
this part of the field. On the surface the ore is 2ft.
wide, value 40 p.c. At a depth of 50ft. there is a
width of 6ft., worth 18 d.c., while at 100 ft. the lode
is 26ft. wide, assaying 6 p.c., which, from a copper
gouger’s point of view, is unprofitable. Wide forma-
tions of quartz, ironstone and kaolin contain narrow
lenses of ore, that rapidly become poorer at increased
depth. The small shipments also make it evident
that the extent of the high-grade ore is very limited.
Still, there are many different kinds of mines, some
of which, like Mount Elliott, contain large deposits
of ore that can be attacked by open cutting; while
others, like the Duchess, have quartz-bornite, carry-
ing good values over a few inches in width, down
below a depth of 500 ft.
* * * *
The North Australian Exploration syndi-
cate (Cloncurry, Q.), has despatched 30 tons of ore
from Mount Lindsay. At its Mammoth lease a surface
discovery of high-grade ore to the north of the shaft
has been made. Staubwasser, one of the prospectors
of Mount Lindsay, is endeavoring to get an ore buyer
appointed by the Government, for the Cloncurry cop-
per gouger isn’t too well satisfied with the prices paid
locally. Still, when the metal market is booming the
purchase of high-grade ore at its full assay value
is risky, and the banks will only make limited ad-
vances on ore shipments.
The North Duchess Copper Company
N.L. has been formed in Sydney, with a capital
of £20,000, in 10s. shares, of which 20,000 were
offered for public subscription. The vendor’s conso-
lation was 8000 fully-paids and £2OO cash, in re-
turn for which was given a property of 15 acres, ad-
joining the northern boundary of the Duchess mine,
Cloncurry district. This is a purely development
show, taken over on the chance of the Duchess shoots
pitching sufficiently north to benefit the co. There
are also the chances of other shoots being discov-
ered. There is a shaft 60ft. deep, and this is re-
ported to have tapped the lode; but the sinking of
a new shaft to 150 ft. in depth is contemplated.
At Croydon (Q.), Cuthberts’ Content
crushed 439 tons for 7830 z., value £1495. Viles’
Block, as the result of crushing 691 tons for 12020 z.
(£3255) has declared a 3d. dividend (£600). The
gold in this claim .is worth £2 14s. 2d. per oz.
Croydon gold varies a great deal in value. In the
early days there was so much silver that the smelted
bullion was sometimes worth less than 20s. per oz.
The Thames and Waihi goldfields, in Maoriland, are
noted for the large quantity of silver recovered with
the gold. At Mount Morgan there appears to
be no silver whatever in the blister copper, which is
somewhat unusual, for Mount Lyell (Tas.), Great
Oobar (N.S.W.), Great Fitzroy, Mount Elliott and
Hampden, Chillagoe and Mount Perry all produce
copper containing silver.
At Irvinebank (N.Q.) the Vulcan has
been for many years the leading tin mine. To the
end of the last half-year (August 3) it produced
3 0,944 tons of concentrates, worth £517,531, an
average of slightly over £47 per ton. Tin concen-
trates are now selling at more than double the aver-
age price for 20-odd years past. At the moment the
Vulcan is in danger of sliding back into second
place, behind the Governor Norman, for although
both mines produced 21 tons of concentrates in
August, the Governor Norman’s lot was worth £2117.
while the Vulcan’s was valued at £2067. Like
nearly all tin lodes, the Vulcan is an erratic mine.
Latest discovery is at the 750 ft. level, where there
is a strong make of ore, worth 2J p.c. tin oxide, to
the south-east. So far it has been proved 30ft. wide
and 30ft. long, and the 900 ft. level is being driven
to test its continuity at depth. The make of ore at
600 ft. proved a patch, but helped to provide £6600
(6d. per share) last half-year. During the six
months 4936 tons were crushed for 152 tons of con-
centrates, valued at £14,264. Since the start
£175,230 has been paid.
At present, Blair Athol (Q.), with an
output of over 70,000 tons of coal per annum, is the
most important Australian colliery in the tropics,
but its 50ft. seam extends over a wide area, and it
is only a matter of time before other collieries will
be at work. In Queensland they have such a habit of
praising up everything to the skies, that it is diffi-
cult to form a solid opinion as to whether Blair
Athol coal is equal for steam purposes to Newcastle.
It was alleged that a mixture of quick-burning Blair
Athol with slow-burning Bluff was just the thing,
and Mount Morgan is taking some of the high carbon
combustible from the Bluff for experimental pur-
poses, to ascertain whether anthracite, containing
80 p.c. carbon, can to some extent replace coke in
the smelting furnace. Neither the Blair Athol nor
Bluff coal produces a good coke, and Oallide Creek
colliery, 60 miles S.W. from the port of Gladstone,
has hardly made itself known yet. At Oallide Creek
there are two seams, one 50ft. and the other 16ft.
thick, and the coal has been prospected by tunnels
H miles apart. A sample from the 16ft. seam has
given a satisfactory test.
* * * *
Mount Cuthbert (Q.), to enliven the
gamble, intends to open up its Kalkadoon mine,
which has already produced some high-grade ore. The
shaft is down 86ft., and if the lode prospects well
at the 100 ft. level, more interest will be taken in
Cuthbert’s, which, however, isn’t likely to reach
the producing stage for a long time to come. The
Hampden, with its influential backers to shove it
along, waited long and spent money very liberally
before it got a start.
* * * *
At Charters Towers (Q.) there is less
excitement over Brilliant Deeps, though No. 5 level,
after passing through a blank, has made good again.
The crushing now going through looks well, and
there is every prospect of a good yield. Nothing has
been said about extending No. 4 level further to the
south-west, but it is quite possible that, if the reef
in No. 4 level maintains its present course to the
boundary, an offer will be made to the Brilliant
Block. The latter has continued its long crosscut
at the 2000 ft. level in the direction of the B. Deeps
boundary, but has not succeeded in locating the forma-
tion. B. Block shares are thought at Charters Towers
to be a good gamble, but it is evident that, if deeper
sinking and other work is done, more capital will
be required. An amalgamation with the B. Deeps
would simplify matters,' but Charters Towers cos.
very seldom amalgamate.
* * * *
Brilliant P.C. (Charters Towers) has
been leading the share market. For the week ending
September 21 3000 shares were exchanged, while
transactions in Deeps numbered 2100 shares, with a
weakening tendency. A few inches of mineralised
stone at the bottom of the new shaft, from the
1596 ft. level, has caused a more optimistic opinion
about the eastward extension of the Day Dawn. In
the winze 220 ft. east of the Freeholds boundary, a
drive has been started at 117 ft. below the level.
There is a large body of stone at the bottom of this
winze, and a heavy soakage of water, but the quality
of the stone is considerably mixed, and it remains to
be seen whether the quality will improve in the
drive. The upper section of the reef in the winze
was patchy.
St. Mungo Copper (N.Q.) is 24 miles
from the Duchess railway station, and has a contract
with camel teamsters to carry ore from the mine
at 255. oer ton. In August 132 tons were shipped,
value 24*3 p.c. The mine will increase its output
now that the south-east level from the main shaft
has broken through to the winze, where a wide face
of chalcopvrite is exposed. The proximity of the
railwav and the rise in copper have greatly improved
the prospects of the St. Mungo. The future depends
of course upon whether other lateral deposits are
discovered with further sinking, which, by the way,
will involve some improvement to the primitive
equipment. Obviously a show of that kind is very
speculative, and with 40,000 shares, it takes just
four times as much money to pay a sixpenny divi-
dend as it used to do.
* * * *
An interesting comparison of the condi-
tion of things at the time the Kalgoorlie wages agree-
ment was entered into 2 J years ago and now, appears
in the Westralian daily press. For the eigrht months
prior to the aforesaid agreement coming into force,
the output from the Kalgoorlie field was £2,418,000
or roughly £302,250 monthly, and for the rest of
the State £1,844,000, or about £230,500 monthly.
During this period Kalgoorlie paid dividends of
£577,024 out of the production mentioned above.
Altogether £1,840,976 went in exes., of which 44
p.c. represented wages. In the outside centres, with
an output of £1,844,000, five cos. paid dividends
aggregating £92,230 and £1,751,770 was absorbed
in costs. The difference between now and then is
that Kalgoorlie’s monthly production has dwindled
by £47,250, its dividends by £19,000 and seven
mines are now unable to pay their way, and, as the
rates of wages remained stationary while a larger
tonnage was handled and more men employed, the
wages paid show a book increase of about 6 p.c. On the
outside fields the difference in the figures is: Decline
in monthly output, £69,000 ; decline in monthly
dividends, £700; and several are off the dividend
list. In addition to this the position of every mine is
admitted to be infinitely worse as regards intrinsic
value, and in the interval the Lancefield, after pro-
ducing £828,000 at a loss has closed down and the
Northern and Vivien, with outputs of £1,022,000
and £320,500 to their credits, have followed suit.
Yet optimists are still to be found in the West who
claim that the mining outlook never looked better.
Lupton’s, near Barrambie (W.A.), which
The Bulletin has long regarded with cold sus-
picion, is ailing. The first two or three returns
were well up to official estimates, but, to the sur-
prise of all who were put in on top, the last one
wasn’t. As previously remarked on this page, there
may be bees, and even wasps, in the loveliest garden,
and in that of Lupton’s there seems to be blight also.
The remains of the late lamented Wes-
tralia Waihi were followed with some show of mourn-
ing to their last resting-place, in Adelaide, the other
day. For all the use it was living it would have
commanded more respect if it had died some time
ago. First of all, it misled shareholders over the
value of the Davyhurst property, and then over that
of. the duffer at Norseman. Still, the same considera-
tion and charity that was shown to the living was
shown to the dead, and there was a fair roll up of
financially sorrow-stricken shareholders at the inter-
ment.
Ingliston Consols, at Meekatharra(W.A.),
announces a production to date of about £150,000,
of which a good portion has been distributed in divi-
dends. The syndicate which owns the show is doing very
well, but things could be bettered. As emphasised
in B. 12/10/’ll, what ought to happen is the amal-
gamation of the Ingliston Consols and neighboring
mines into one or two groups. Such cos. would earn
more money for everyone, and the field would get a
better advt. * * *
Reports from Ida H. (W.A.) are as glum
as back numbers of this paper predicted. Costs
still mop up most of the revenue, and the prospect
of the show paying any more dividends is slender.
Good miners refuse to leave other places to live
in the locality where the Ida is situated, and on ac-
count of this and a narrow reef with a rapid dip,
working expenditure continues high, though a bit
less than it used to be. And besides these troubles
ther-e is an impoverishment in the bottom to contend
with.
* * * *
Boulder No. 1 (W.A.) is taking out all
that is worth having before abandoning the St. George
lease, Magnet, to its fate. Since development was
stopped some months ago, 2391 tons of picked ore
have been crushed for a recovery of £5901, of
which £2644 is stated to be profit. For the first
100 ft. or so of sinking the show looked well. Then
it began to get sickly, and at 200 ft. it was only
a spectre of its former self. Only a miraculous dis-
covery of a new shoot of gold could save the concern’s
life, and, as the present owner isn’t keen on looking
for one, its doom seems to be sealed.
More proof of the futility of Chaffers
(W.A.) spending money in searching for one or more
of the Golden Horseshoe’s shoots at 1600 ft. is shown
in the latest progress report issued by the manage-
ment of the latter co. All the faces within shouting
distance of the boundary are in low-grade material,
and, as stated in previous Bulletins, it appears as
if Chaffers will have to dig much deeper to find
the values it is seeking, and it will want a lot of
luck to get them even then.
* * * *
Kurnalpi (W.A.) not being pleasing to
the eye, the Adelaide-subscribed Kurnalpi option
syndicate has decided to give the Binti Binti lease
near Gindalbie (Vosperton) a trial. Some shallow
shafts have been sunk, and a trial parcel of 205 tons
recently crushed about 90s. per ton. The price asked
is £4OOO. There is no water close at hand, but a
supply of some sort was struck some time ago by a
Government bore two miles away. Without crush-
ing facilities on the spot, and with no water close
handy, the property is described by people who
ought to know as useless.
* * * *
More deception has been practised on the
Bullfinch co., of W.A., which fell in at Tooletville
and Leonora, and then went to Greenbushes. believ-
ing that it had at last got a mine. But instead of high
values only 4 p.c. tin was found in the main shaft
workings, ' while in the place of a rich 8 ft. lode,
worked down to a depth of 60ft. only, all the ore
was found to have been stoped out down to 90ft.,
and everything of any value in the show seemed to
have disappeared.
Production is being stimulated in the
West as a result of the high price of tin. Wodgina
is tipped to bloom and boom, and attempts
are being made to window-dress the prospects of
several shows in this and other centres at Pilbarra,
with a view to flotation. Coodardy, on the Mur-
chison, is described by geologist Woodward as highly
promising, and 25 tons of ore from one claim
yielded £9 per ton. A lot of ore is in sight on the
field, and boomsters are beginning to gather around.
Greenbushes maintains a steady *
Mines Minister Collier (W.A.) has, during
his 10 months of office, sanctioned the expenditure of
£9l 000 on water and crushing facilities in outside
districts, and £9OOO on improving the efficiency of
State mills. The work of purchasing and patching
up old plants, however, has not been a success, and,
as far as possible, the Department intends to go m
for new batteries in the future. *
Phillips River Co. (W.A.) has managed
to get a little more exemption in the teeth of strong
opposition. Even the local undertaker rebelled, on
the ground that corpses were not nearly so plentiful
as when the mines were working, and the pub-
keepers whose beer now gets stale waiting for con-
sumption and the agents of absentees who, in the
good days, loaned money on certain portions of
the bankrupt settlement, also took part in the dis-
turbance. So far half a million of English money
has been punk by the co. and its ancestors in the
district, and even with copper at its present price, it
is doubtful if dividends could be earned on the
present capital, plus the additional amount required
to get things going again.
* * *
At the Wallaroo and Moonta’s half-yearly
meeting, in Adelaide, an attempt was made to re-
assure shareholders about the future of this old con-
cern. The directors stated that the £7OOO, which
it is proposed to spend on the Moonta, is only part
of a comprehensive scheme of mine development.
At the end of the half-year (June 30) the cash in
hand and on deposit amounted to £30,000, of which
£20,000 is set aside for development work. The
Wallaroo may “never have looked better,” but min-
ing at 2340 ft. (the deepest level) is costly, and no
statement is available as to the ore reserves. Last
half-year the Wallaroo mine produced 2116 tons of
copper out of 2887 tons from all sources. At the
meeting the yarn went round that the co.’s present
returns were better than they were 20 years ago, but
during nine years to the end of 1911 the co. made a
profit of £184,577 out of old residues accumulated
on the surface. The average profit was £2B per ton
on 6572 tons of copper leached out of the old heaps.
In 1911 the cost of producing copper was £5l 12s.
sd. per ton, but five years before that (1906-1907)
the cost was £74 per ton.
Bore assays are notoriously unreliable.
Tongkah Harbor (Siam) gave the market a surprise
when the Australian manager took his dredges on to
a run of good wash-dirt, which, in September, 1911,
gave 149 tons of oxide. This year, four weeks to
September 31 only gave 116 tons. Still metallic
tin is more than £5O per ton higher than it was 12
months since. Tongkah is paying dividends.
* * * *
Adelaide Tramways Trust’s latest report shows that
it has 84 miles of track, Goodwood being the only line
on which the horse still does a bit at the outer or
suburban end—and that bit shows a heavy loss. In
the past half-year, the Trust carried 20,000,000 people,
and ran 2i million miles, earning over £140,000.
Working expenses amount to just about two-thirds of
the takings. During the last year there have been
895 1 “third party” accidents (of which 460 were due
to passengers getting out of a moving car). Five
of them were fatal, and £1383 compensation was
paid. In four years, the Trust has had 310 accident
claims made against it. Just half of them it repudi-
ated promptly, and the rest it settled, bar nine.
This meant nine lawsuits, of which it won two and
lost the rest. Juries always think a Trust —-even a
Municipal Trust —fair game to shoot at.
* * * *
The great advance made in the construction of In-
ternal Combustion Engines has now been further
demonstrated by the fact of Petrol, Benzine, Kero-
sene and heavy Oil Locomotives being brought into
daily and successful commercial use. The machines
are thoroughly reliable, and are cheaper in very
many instances than steam or electric locomotives.
Messrs. Ironside, Son and Dyckerhoff, of London,
have supplied a great number of their famous “New
Century” locomotives nearly all over the world, and
their machines are even working continuously and
safely in “gassy” coal mines. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne, will be pleased to supply further par-
ticulars.*
