A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 30, 1912.
My Dear Arini.—
A fiddler in the land of George Washing-
ton (sometimes called G. Wash, for short) is
reported to have written to the G.-G. asking
if there is any hope of a permanent billet for
him in this part of the globe. Wild rumors
of the uses to which the State Government
may put Sydney’s stately old pile in Mac-
quarie-street had doubtless floated over to
the listening ear in Philadelphia. And by
the time the report reached there it probably
ran to the effect that a Ministry consisting of
Six Hatters and a March Hare had decided
to hang the Governor-General, and turn ms
residence into a College of Music, wherein
the Professorship of the Concertina would
carry a salary of £3OOO per annum, with
£2OOO per annum for the Professorship of
the Tin Whistle. The last word has not
been said, however, on the stormy subject of
old Government House. Minister for Hay-
seeds Trefld has a perfect right, to demand
the site for some pet hobby of his own. The
Carmichael Conservatorium or the Holman
Museum might give way to a nursery for
young artesian bores, or a forcing house for
early asparagus. The spicy humor of a
U.S. fiddler appealing to an evicted and
wholly temporary Governor-General for a
permanent billet in this State suggests Ber-
nard Shaw standing on his literary head in
order to place his tongue in his cheek.
The tumult of the fray over the Governor-
General’s expulsion from N.S.W. is begin-
ning to rumble amongst the quiet hills of
country townships. Prom a business point
of view, the presence of a wealthy peer, who
spends his cash with both hands, ought to
be encouraged. Monsignor O’Brien, the be-
nign-looking prelate in charge of St. John’s
College, suggests old Government House,
Parramatta, as a desperate remedy for the
eviction. But the ancient residence is swal-
lowed up within the realm of the King’s
School as a house for the smaller fry.
Whether _ the English public school system
of breaking up the central administration
into residential camps, more or less removed
from the headmaster’s eye, is without de-
fects, only the fry can say.
_ Some wiseacre once said that after ex-
cited public speakers had furiously banged
the table and made the habitual water-bottle
lean up against the tumbler for support, the
mildest-looking man on the platform would
usually deal the most smashing blow without
raising a finger. At the uproar in the Town
Hall, the other night. Mayor Walker (North
Sydney) only raised his voice a few feet
above the reporters’ table, and dealt the
State Government the unkindest cut of all.
“At the very time they say they can’t afford
to pay the rent (£3300 a year) for Govern-
ment House, they put an extra £lB,OOO on
the estimates for the increase in members’
salaries.” Then the meek voice of Mayor
Walker floated away into the infinities, and
the most biting remark of the evening was
removed in shorthand.
Governor Chelmsford has laid quite a large
clutch of foundation-stones during the past
three or more weeks. On Saturday after-
noon, his Ex. again performed his favorite
feat without misadventure. A new pavilion
for the Royal North Shore Hospital (St.
Leonards) was the primary cause, and loud
was the joyous cackle when the foundation-
egg was deposited. Mr. J. Randal Carey
who is something of consequence on the hos-
pital committee, lifted up his voice to welcome
the sub-Governor Chelmsford (the G.-G. being
still in this port), the ex-Sub-Lieutenant
Governor Cullen, and a steady stream of
Mayors and Mayoresses, who poured in from
various municipal strongholds. The com-
mittee, by the way, is reported to be going
broody on the troubled question of £17,000
or_ so—the cost of the new buildings. It is
fairly easy to lay foundation-stones, but to
hatch them into sovereigns or young bank
notes takes more than three weeks I’m told.
Concerning the Kaiser, his peppery tem-
per, and confirmed habit of apologising after
making a breach of the peace, this anecdote
is only 14 or 15 years old. Probably it has
improved with age. It hasn’t appeared in
print before, to the best of my belief, for the
less important half of the story told it to
me years ago.
the handsome and dashing
Miss M , an off-shoot of the Macarthur-
Onslow tribe. Sent to a school in Germany,
specially dedicated to the daughters of naval
and military officers who served the Kaiser-
land. The school being of the official class
was occasionally visited by the Emperor if
he happened to be dashing through time and
space in that vicinity. He appeared with
his usual unexpectedness one fine afternoon,
cast his eye along the boarding-school mus-
lins, _ then celebrating some little German
fete in the grounds, noticed the tall beauty,
asked who she was, and “commanded” her
to his Imperial presence. The navy being
one of William’s pet hobbies (he has several
hundred hobbies, I believe), he rattled off a
brisk catalogue of the British fighting
strength on blue water. “And your father
Miss, is commander of gunboat So-and-so, of
such-and-such tonnage.” “No, sir,” said she.
My father is captain of So-and-so, of so
many more tons.” Rage and consternation
on the part of a fighting column of German
governesses, while flaxen-haired daughters of
the soil nearly fainted with shame. To con-
tradict the Majesty of Germany—ach Gott!
William persisted. The girl was firm. Wil-
liam swallowed his Divine right in silence,
and rode off speechless with indignation.
Then the wrath of everything in the school
leapt up like a hundred devils. “Fool—im-
becile —ignorant one —what can you expect
from the mad English?—wicked one who dis-
graced the high standing of the school,” etc.
Next day came a courier with a letter from
the Kaiser. “He was wrong—Miss M. was
correct —still, he would advise young girls to
be less ready to contradict their elders.”
Tableaux. Frauleins were even more enraged
when the sinner got the sacred apology than
they had been at the original sin. The
good, the meek, the gentle German maidens
(perhaps they were of the homely type) had
not been so much as noticed, while—!— !• —■!
Organising Sec. Grace Burrows (Girls’
Realm) deserves three cheers, and any super-
fluous cheers that may be lying around, for
arranging an all-Australian wild flower show.
The parochial joy of a show which interests
Manly and the North Sydney settlements of
tiled villas, and even gets a few miles away
from the coast to Gosford, doesn’t thrill the
man at Darwin, or grip the interest of the
Westralian girl, but it has good points all
the same. For the first time, so far as I
know, the shy beauties of the bush were col-
lected from every State of Australasia to lift
up their heads together as the federated flora
of one nation.
Every section of the flower rally stalked
the wild blooms in their designs. The Arts
and Grafts’ exhibits were solid on the wattle,
gum-leaf, gum-blossom end waratah. Balla-
rat (Vic.) sent some of the finest examples
of metal work, with native flora designs, that
have been on view here. Miss Gwenda Mann
(Tech. Art School, Ballarat) harpooned the
first prize for a large copper coal-box, with
wild flora in relief. The National Gallery
trustees bought this beautiful piece of metal
work on sight. Miss Coralie McLeod, Mr.
Robert Caldwell and Miss Margery Mitchell,
also of Ballarat, were strong exhibitors in
this section. The National Gallery also
secured 18 pencil drawings, as fine and clear
as good etchings, of Australian vegetation.
Miss Annie Burrows (North Sydney) was
responsible for this work, which swept away
the first prize for its botanical accuracy. Some
wild and startled looking blooms in oils and
water-colors, ranged from moderately bad to
somethin’ awful,” as they say in the
classics. The worst offenders in the still life
display, I regret to say, were local splashers.
The ‘ artists” are quite still and dead every
year in some of our bi-annual picture shows.
The trustees of the National Gallery made a
further effort, and invested some good Corn-
stalk legal tender in a healthy and upstand-
ing jug, painted by Miss Ada Newman, of
Sydney.
Westralia sent along a wonderful collec-
tion of its pressed native trees (not its big-
gest trees, of course) —wonderful, because
nearly every specimen missed that dreadful
expression of having had its face mangled
under a steam-roller, which is peculiar to
dried forests. Another advantage the West-
ern roses of Sharon and lilies of the valley
seem to have over some of our paler beauties
is their fixed color —guaranteed to wash and
boil, I imagine. _ Anyhow, they sat up in
firm sprays of vivid pink, blue and crimson
flowerets, after the pressing machine or
botanical boot had stamped them flat, on
paper mounts. Miss Julia Sewell (W.A.)
garnered a prize in this section.
. Ethel Turner (Mrs. Curlewis) lent her
aid to the judges’ department of the Aus-
tralian wild flower rally. Little Mrs. Har-
rison (Amy Mack) plunged vigorously as an
exhibitor, and got away with a solid helping
of prize money. As the only judge of the
juvenile human-orchid show on Saturday
afternoon, she had to dodge the wrath of
mothers whose infants missed the omnibus,
and crept warily out of the turmoil on
noiseless soles. The mauve orchid (a small
boy with a long green silk body like
a stalk) won the first lap. Wattle-maiden,
a sweet thing with golden hair, was highly
commended. A purple iris, also very young,
consoled herself by selling £3 worth of
penny floral postcards for the Cause. The
tea-room was a persistent money-spinner.
In this section, Miss Lottie Gilbert, as one
of the assisting Girl-angels, wore a muslin
mob-cap, and a tricky pleated apron of some
diaphanous nonsense over her white gown,
and did it w T ith immediate success. Aprons
of this frivolous persuasion, however, are not
built for the ordinary tea and bun business,
though they’d certainly attract Percy Augus-
tus Cuffs and co. in his search for a six-
penny lunch.
In the section devoted to literary competi-
tions (Australian Wild Flower Show) great
was the rush of matter thereof. So great
was the rush that some of the competitors
fell over the English language with heavy
slaughter. Going through the efforts in “T”
section, open to all ages ; one noticed the com-
parative affluence of imagination and the
poverty of composition. Good ideas were
strangled by words that were not approxi-
mate to the sense of the author’s great flight
across the inky lay. The Queensland com-
petitors were ahead of the scribes in this
State and in Victoria in local color. Girls
in Bananaland can fling Latin at their
native flora with both hands, but they often
step aside to chatter with every wild thing
that hides itself away in tropic jungles or
temperate highlands. The influence of bricks
and mortar was several storeys high in some
of the N.S.W. efforts.
The “Guild League of Bird Lovers” will
hold its second annual chirrup on October 9.
The Turner Hall (Technical College) will
be the meeting place of persons of all sizes
who are pledged by fearful oaths (a) “To
protect all birds except those that are
noxious, etc.” When the bird-lover gets to
“etc.” he takes on the contract of goading
his friends and enemies to hunt for bird
sanctuaries. Most people—excepting little
boys, savages and suburban gardeners—are
ready to offer free board and lodging to
nearly anything with feathers, so the “etc.”
section ought to fill up with enthusiasm.
N.B. —The starling ought to be excluded
from the free list (in gutterings and under
verandah eaves) with the business end of a
broom.
The M.L. Tourist Agency here, with the
übiquitous E. H. Montgomery in charge,
spread itself out for advertisement on
Dominion Day, under a shady forest of 10ft.
palms. These were planted in pots or tubs
on the primeval (I mean, tessellated) floor.
One of the main props of the repartee de-
partment was U.S. Consul-General Bray,
whose jokes trickled through a suave man-
ner, like oil-drops out of a plump petroleum
well. The handsome David Mills (Union
S.S. Co.), a trifle more immaculate than
usual in honor of the day, and the affable
Swedish Consul, Ivon Goss (his whiskers
are worn in the foreign style and trained to
grow towards the ears), also contributed to
the gaiety of the party. The long and cheer-
ful Government Bone-Grader, J. Laing (of
Maoriland), rendered first-aid when chest-
nuts fell into the champagne.
The breathless energy of the busy little
Mrs. G. L. Goodman is now on the track of a
charity fete for the Veterans’ Home. Mrs.
Rutter is hon. organising secretary. The
Admirable King-Hall (very firm in good
works) will be the lever to open the event in
Victoria Barracks on October 23. It is going
to chase the nimble shilling through the pre-
text of a naval and military clash of frills.
October is the month of flying hoofs at
Randwick, roses in every patch of likely soil,
and heavy growths of garden fetes for char-
ity. St. Margaret’s Hospital for Women
(Bourke-street) wants its annual appeal for
help to build up a record cheque. Mayor
Clarke will tear himself away from the
gloomy subject of lost and strayed tram-
tickets, the tightness of money (Scoffers sug-
gests that the tight money should take the
pledge after its orgie of borrowing), etc., and
open St. Margaret’s garden party on October
Mr. Owen Cox gave the glittering assort-
ment of silverware prizes to the progressive
euchre party on (M.L.) Dominion Evening.
The game raged with unabated fury for some
time, in the Hyde Park kiosk. The names
of winners cannot be mentioned above a
whisper. Some of the players got away with
trophies that might incite the industrious
burglar to gnash his crowbar with joy, if he
tracked them to their long home in the
suburban sideboard.
Members of the _ Royal Sydney Yacht
Squadron are sending forth invitations to
an afternoon party in the club grounds.
These are handsomely trimmed with grassy
lawns, that fall into the water off Ivirri-
billi Point.
. Reports of social hops, teas, muffins and
similar thrilling events, are likely to be
tripped up by the presence of two Misses
Molly Owen at the same frisks. One Molly
has been out in the feverish glare of society
for a year or two or thereabouts. She is
the younger of Mr. H. P. Owen’s two nice
daughters. Molly the second has just stepped
off the debutante’s white lily springboard
to plunge in the mazy waltz. She is a
young daughter of barrister Danger Owen
(Hunter’s Hill). H. P. Owen (Master in
Equity) and Danger are unrelated, but they
are close friends.
Miss S. Potter, the uncommonly good-
looking girl who was here about two or more
years ago (from London), is engaged. Her
young man is one Simon, a wealthy iron-
master on the other side. Miss Potter, I
fancy, dabbled in research work on the
rather stuffy subject of “social economy.”
The local Women’s Clubs, of all shades of
politics, fell upon the handsome Potter neck,
during the girl’s efforts to illuminate this
portion of the globe. She was a Girton
damsel, and is a daughter of the Potter who
collaborates with Birt and Hughes, as
owners of the Federal Line of steamers. A
brother, Roy Potter, is at present on these
shores.
Mrs. Edmund Playfair, mother-in-law-to-
be of George Reid’s pretty daughter Thelma,
went off to meet her son at Adelaide. A
Playfair daughter also tripped home in the
same boat with the brother who won the
Pie Commissioner’s daughter.
Mr. H. Spence, formerly collector of cus-
toms in Invercargill—where the wind whips
the Tam-o’-Shanter off the Scottish brow—
is passing through this town on his way to
the Cold Land. He takes up the expert work
of Government bulldog-in-chief at the
London end of the M.L. invoices, and will
check the faked statement with a warning
bark. Mrs. Spence and a daughter go for-
ward also to the Thames village, as per pas-
senger list.
Recent returns to this port are Mrs.
Mann (formerly of liandwick) and her son-
in-law and daughter, Dr. and Mrs. Burfitt.
The trio stay at Petty’s Hotel.
Mrs. Adams (who belongs to the Knox
clan) has packed up her youngsters and
other valuables, and departed with them to
join her Naval Surgeon Adams on the China
station. For some months she has been
visiting the spacious parental nest in these
parts.
The late Senator J. C. Neild, who gave the
first push to the Old Age Pensions Act in
the N.S.W. House of Bills (in the days be-
fore he bloomed in the Senate) was memor-
ialised in brass and bronze the other day.
Some of his Old Age friends in the Padding-
ton electorate contributed a handsome tablet
to the memory of the late ex-M.L.A., ex-
Senator and ex-poet. His family hung an-
other tribute in bronze on the walls of St.
James’s Church. This recalls the fact that
Mr. Neild was Colonel of the militia regi-
ment, which takes St. George and the Dragon
for its war whoop. Archdeacon Shields tore
aside the drapery of both memorials in one
day, so he had a crowded time.
Miss Fairy Burne, daughter of the well-
known dental-surgeon, Dr. Alfred Burne, of
this city, has recently joined the phalanx of
engaged girls. The lucky man is Mr. B. B.
Mills, of Queensland. Dr. Burne, by the
way, has an amiable reputation as a deter-
mined first-nighter, and one of the most cap-
able judges of gastronomy in this locality.
Mr. Chandler, who is the managing clerk
of the big legal firm, Minter, Simpson and
Co., acquired a very pretty wife this (Mon-
day) afternoon. She’s a Miss O’Farrell, of
Melbourne, well-known as a clever designer.
The bridal breakfast was served at Paris
House.
Mrs. Lena Conly (Vic.), a singing sister
of the renowned Mary Conly, is going to try
the adventure of a concert tour through the
Australian States. The party is supposed to
strike this collection of humpies about the
17th October. Of some interest will be the
first appearance of the lady fiddler of the
Conly co. Gertrude Summerhayes, a daugh-
ter of the well-known Mrs. Summerhayes,
one of the earlier identities hereabouts.
Walter Baker, who will tear the soul of
music out of a harp, has been a fixture in
Victoria for some years. He’s said to be as
good at his instrument as Lemmone is on the
flute.
Another small boy has added his howl of
anguish to the long list of “didn’t-know-it-
was-loaded” pea-rifle casualties. The victim
of the recent accident is a son of Dr. Muller,
and met his misfortune during a country
holiday the other week. The eye which was
in action has had its appearance wonderfully
renovated by the most ingenious devices of
modern science. Eye-Surgeon Odillo Maher
was the lancet in charge. The average pea-
rifle has such a habit of loading itself, or sud-
denly going on the burst (usually with fatal
results) that it ought to be invoiced
“Original Sin,” and sent back to Genesis.
Yours in dusty weather,
VANDORIAN.
“Old Soldier” : Opponents of the Australian
navy are pointing joyfully to the alleged fact
that the “voluntary” boys on the old train-
ing-ship Tingira, in Sydney Harbor are de-
serting “at the rate of about a dozen a
month.” It is probably a lie: but even if it
isn’t, there are precedents. If the Admiral
commanding-in-chief on this station would
publish the number of desertions among his
crews, it would give some people a shock.
When the Yankee fleet was in Australian
waters the men deserted at such a rate as
nearly to cripple the ships. It is the same
in all voluntarily enlisted navies and
armies. Men (or boys) join, not for
the pay, but because they are in
trouble, or out of a job, or fancy
they will like the life, or hope they will get
promotion quickly, or have adventures. Then
they come face to face with the reality, and
it is a disillusionment. Scrubbing tables
and forms and barrack-room floors is unro-
mantic, and drill is monotonous. It is a
curious fact that the French and Germans
have not the least trouble in manning their
navies with eflicient crews on a basis of three
years continuous compulsory service. Their
ships are just as highly mechanical as those
of the British, but they get their men older,
and from classes who are already prepared by
previous training for their work. They keep
their men because, in the eyes of a French-
man or a German, a deserter is a traitor to
his country, and hence a criminal and an
outcast. Voluntary enlistment is only a fad,
and if it fails Australia will have to
discard British rules, and fall back on the
universal system of compulsion.
In opening the Mitchell Library some
time ago, Governor Chelmsford, of N.S.W.,
alluded to pressmen as “penny-a-liners.” As
an acknowledgment, the Institute of Jour-
nalists entertained Lord Chelmsford, a
briefless barrister, and the son of a blun-
dering soldier, on Monday evening, at sup-
per.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 30, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Mrs. Liverpool, the wife of Maoriland’s
new imported Excellency, is a pleasant-look-
ing Irish dame with a twinkle in her eye.
She can cook, sew, mend a broken limb, knit
socks and ride a bicycle. Unfortunately,
though, she can’t mend boots or do plumbing.
You must remember not to talk of either of
these accomplishments when you meet them,
or if you do you will be the cause of embar-
rassment.
One day last week the holiday-tanned
face of Aide Nutting looked wearily over the
bulwarks of a motor speeding up Collins-
street towards the Melbourne Club. Society
is starting to oil and steam in this direction,
now that the horse season is looming ahead.
Nutting represents the first drawing-room
swallow of the year.
A brand new betrothal has just been
trumpeted from London to interested Aus-
tralian relatives. Clive, the 20-year-old
hopeful of M.H.R. Willie Baillieu, is to
marry Ruby Clarke, daughter of wealthy
William Clarke, the sharebroking partner of
Lionel Robinson. Australian gold makes the
match glorious. The Clarkes live at Hamp-
ton Gardens, London, and the disgracefully
rich Robinsons have settled themselves in
some castle vacated by poverty-stricken aris-
tocrats. The engaged two are both just free
from college and finishing processes.
Ex-Gaud Mayor Burston’s son is busily
preparing for the altar ordeal. Dorothy
Jowett, a Toorak maiden, will take his arm
to the wedding march, on Tuesday, October
22, at the local kirk.
“Pigmies Amongst Potentates” is the title
of a book written by Florrie Finn (daughter
of Edmund Finn and grand-daughter of
“Garryowen” ditto), who wandered round
the world for a year or two, and took many
photos by the way. The whole of the pro-
ceeds of the publication (less actual cost of
printing and binding) are to be given to the
Children’s Hospital, and friends of that de-
serving charity are asked to help by sub-
scription at ss. a copy, the edition being
limited to the list of subscribers. Mrs.
Edmund Finn, Punch office, Melbourne, will
receive subscriptions.
Several wrnmen, who were hunting race
gowns, recently gathered round a model gar-
ment in one of the best chiffon stores. The
lovely clo’ was marked “sixty guineas,” and
looked capable of crumpling into a handful
and going through a bangle. The group
started to argue snappishly about who had
seen _it first, and trouble seemed to be im-
pending, when the wisp of contention was
whisked awav by a shop-lady with the brief
remark, “This_ has just been sold to Mrs.
Clarke.” Society is now looking forward
breathlessly to the first hot race day.
Barrister Ah Yet (a sad, regretful name!)
is betrothed to Gertrude, daughter of the
wealthy Bullocks, of Ivew. They are to be
married at the end of the year.
Concerning Chinese engagements. Did I
tell you that The Bulletin’s Past Grand
Barmaid was recently proposed to by this
paper’s eminent Mongolian' contributor, Ah
Too Soon? Sue rejected him with the yard
broom, two props, an empty champagne
bottle, three lumps of coal and a picket
wrenched off the fence. Fortunately someone
had borrowed the axe, or she would have
rejected him finally with that.
Professor Baldwin Spencer, who is just
now peering into foreign laboratories and
carefully considering retorts, proposes to re-
turn to his University post next March. The
professor s dame will come on a month
ahead to dust the furniture and set the
house in order.
The Guy Sherwood couple, after wander-
ing round Japan, had the late Mikado’s
funeral as a last spectacle, before taking
ship for Melbourne. Many charity commit-
tees will rejoice to have active little Mrs.
Guy back again on the financial waraath.
The furniture at the Agar Wynne estab-
lishment was hurriedly pushed into corners,
last Thursday night, to make room for the
St. Kilda footballers. The little lawyer host
is president of the club, and more than 90
kickers of the windbag came to eat and
drink at his expense. They faced the food in
three detachments in the dining and billiard
rooms and back parlor, and the clashing of
silver and steel sounded like a Roman legion
engaging a band of Carthaginians. But the
footballers were not the only people who
enjoyed the Wynne hospitality. An inci-
dental prowler burgled a meat safe and got
away with two colossal joints of beef.
Mrs. Hudson Marsden, just back from Lon-
don, is now shaking hands with her old
friends, after a five years’ absence, and ar-
ranging with some builder to make her a nest
at Macedon. Son Edgar, of the silvery
tenor, has made good in English comic
opera, and is staying on in the Fog. But his
vivacious, dark-eyed mother is repeating
over and over again that there is no place
like Victoria. Her exile has made her very
patriotic.
Minister for Mines Mcßride was billed
to open the new State Geological Museum
on Saturday, but his illness postponed the
key-turning. However, a few interested
people had gathered, and refused to depart.
They stqrmed the back stairs, thereby gain-
ing admittance to the big new room and the
fascinating exhibition. The first impression
is not alluring—rocks, and more rocks tidily
arranged in rows of glass cases, and a grey,
quiet man, with a deep, preoccupied look in
liis eyes, hovering over all the rocks. But a
brief inspection discovers that the rocks are
beautifully colored. It seems as if every-
thing of value in the world can be dug out
of this corner of Australia. Some turquoise,
shining from a setting of black shale, is from
Hedi, on the King River. Red jasper lies
in the rough, a heap of dull blood-color, but
it polishes vividly. There are large deposits
of snowy-looking stuff, diatomaceous earth,
that will make either dynamite or antisep-
tics. Wolfram and sheelite, from beyond
Omeo, will harden steel for ships’ plating.
Dyke, from Dookie, mixed with straw, will
make paper. Glaze, for porcelain and china,
has so far been imported, but at Dunolly
there is felspar in quantities—a glaze that
will knock anything Chinese sky-high. Sand
from Bacchus Marsh (in Dog Trap Gully)
has already been made into beautifully clear
glass. _ The beknighted Clarkes have been
squatting at Sunbury on lots of buried alum.
A sample of phosphate that will electrifv
agriculture when a means of solubility is
found, _ is also on hand. Chemists are now
wrestling with the problem. Then there are
gold, silver and copper, and platinum and
tin. Lead from Buchan sits heavily in its
case. Wonthaggi contributes slack coal com-
pressed into convenient bricks.
The marbles are lovely things. Collector
Ferguson, who has been rooting and dig-
ging and cataloguing for 12 months, says he
has great slabs and hunks of marbles that
cannot be shown for lack of space. Fer-
guson and his chief, Geologist Dunn, have
packed several year’s work of search
into the year. Dunn is busy on a
report about some curious little black
stones which are much discussed. The
question is whether they are bombs
from a local volcano or are heavenly ad-
vents. A few of the marbles have most
plebeian origin. Ferguson calls them back
yard marbles. _ A green, knobby kind was
dug up in Collingwood, and some white and
pale-tinted bits of the classic stone were got
in Richmond, where residential palaces are
packed on blocks of land 30ft. x 10ft., or
thereabouts. Just out of the city, on the
Oakleigh, Black Flats (where ' Caulfield
horses sometimes exercise) there are pig-
ments for every kind of paint, and earth
that Bayley, of the Department, has experi-
mentally turned into beautifully baked and
colored tiles.
Charity shows are booming in Hawthorn.
The next on the list is an amateur attempt
at The Importance of Being Earnest.” Mrs.
Oswald Syme and sister Lucy Rowe are the
handsome parties responsible. The financial
position of some church is the excuse offered
by the co.
. The Lyceum Club, which consists exclu-
sively of blue stockings, and has Mrs. Deakin
for president, was entertained by Mrs. Ed-
ward Bage at Cranford, last week. Freda
Bage is now visiting home before starting
biology lectures at the new Brisbane Uni-
versity.
The golfing women are to have their own
particular innings next week. President
L. K. S. Mackinnon gave his big annual
party at the Sandringham Links, last week,
and the Best People attended in motor loads.
Mrs. Mackinnon is out of the social arena,
for the time being, on account of her sister's
death. In her absence, small daughter Mar-
got played hostess in a short mauve frock
and with fascinating black velvet tails
attached to her lace hat. The Mackinnon
himself wore a grey Tam-o’-Shanter above a
beautiful golfing suit. The day was grey
and blustery, and a marquee billowed and
flapped above the refreshments.
The Babies’ Home bazaar-fete, which was
to have happened at Whernside, the Millers’
Toorak house, on Saturday, October 12, has
been hurriedly advanced one day to October
11. Society, having undertaken to sell
things to itself on the first-named date, sud-
denly remembered that the Caulfield Guineas
outing clashed with its benevolent amuse-
ment, and fell over itself to postpone its
charity. Those in the forefront of the raid
include the Forrest dame, the Gaud May-
oress, Mrs. George Fairbairn, Mrs. Leding-
ham, and Mrs. Roystone Davey. Mrs. Miller
is dashing open the ballroom and drawing-
room, as well as the garden; and Maggie
Stirling and daughter will organise an indoor
concert for the afternoon.
The Whernside baby function is to be
loaded with an opening blessing by Arch-
bishop Clarke. The Fuller lady has pro-
mised to stroll in and buy some things dur-
ing the afternoon. Mrs. Roystone Davey is
not only acting as secretary for this show, but
is also deep in political and social organis-
ing. Her various jobs keep her whizzing
from point to point in her motor, and some-
times the gas engine works up such a speed
that it arrives at its destination before it
starts. She wrenched a suede heel off her
shoe at Mrs. Arthur Sachse’s political crush
last week ; or, perhaps, it dropped off there
just before she arrived and flew ahead of
her.
A sharp attack of heroine-worship wrung
the hearts and pockets of the Athenaeum
Club members, the other day, when they pre-
sented a fine watch bracelet, decked with
diamonds, to the leading lady in the Geoffrey
Syme burglar episode. It isn’t often that a
woman who lays her hand upon a man in
other than the way of kindness scores a
handsome piece of jewellery for her pains.
Mylie Egan, the promising young Bendigo
soprano, for whom three years’ foreign study
is projected, is to have a benefit concert, in
Melbourne Town Hall, after the racing fever
has subsided. A meeting presided over by
Gaud Mayor Davey discussed the matter last
week, and allotted the important jobs of
secretary and treasurer respectively to Mr.
McNamara and Mrs. A. W. A. Fossett.
The Victorian Artists’ dance proved once
more that the art students are nothing if
not ingenious in their picture frivols. Kero-
sene tins were wrought so cunningly into
armor that their own Oil Trust would never
have known them. Brown paper, varnished
and stained, looked so like leather that I
wondered why the manufacturers thereof did
not hurry into the boot business. Hessian
as wide as a billiard table and only sixpence
or so a yard is capable of becoming anything
under a clever brush. I saw bewildering
stuffs that looked as if lonely maidens had
stitched at them in towers for long years
while their lovers were away at the Crusades
skewering perfect strangers with turbans.
It was just hessian every time. The full-
fledged members of the V.A.S. put in a lot
of work; but the toil accomplished by the
students was simply amazing. They painted
acres of the stuff that bags are made of, and
made tapestries to harmonise with the Nor-
man donjon that hid the great organ. Cer-
tainly the steps that led to the keep were
fatted with modern iron railings; but they
were smothered in ancient draperies, while
the flashlight photographer was blazing
away. Mrs. Professor Ewart taught the
pageant people their madrigals, and Miss
Hyams regulated their stepping.
Lohengrin wandered —or swandered —
round the hall, and occasionally bumped
against a bare-footed friar clad in sackcloth.
Iwo dozen jesters, fitted with bells, rushed
from the castle and danced an old English
measure. Valkyrie maidens whooped in a
wavering way whenever they wished to
assemble their set from sitting-out corners ;
foresters _ and she-foresters, in brown and
green, with peacocks’ feathers, made a good,
bare-legged dancing troupe. The girls with
shapely understandings were mostly in the
smart page costume.
The unmasking was left till late. Some
of the leisured youth of the city drifted in
arrayed in mask and domino over ordinary
evening dress. One prettily feminine lad
purposely marred his good looks with a false
nose and detachable moustache. After
theatre hours, Ted Tait, of Her Majesty’s,
steered a motor load of stars in stage dress
to the revel. The management performed
the solemn rite known as supper on the top
floor, and carefully delayed it till the pageant
was past. Then a limited amount of beer
and weak punch made the motley crowd
merry in moderation for the last gay hour
or so.
The pretty son of a suburban medico went
to the Artists’ dance as a harem beauty. At
least, he attempted to go as such ; most of
the time he was adjusting his figure and
gathering himself together. The lashings on
his_ pads seemed to be of the flimsiest de-
scription, and, every now and then, he would
fall away to nothing and have to pick him-
self up again. His final collapse happened
in the ballroom. I don’t know whether he
reconstructed for the fourteenth time, or
went into liquidation at the bar.
The popular “On Our Selection,” at the
King’s, has been attracting a host of those
people who radiate importance and bounding
wealth—things to which the regular honest
patrons are not accustomed. But they will
not stand for too much “dog.” One night
last week, a stout, important man was
ushered into a box by an attendant, who re-
spectfully peeled off his overcoat and left on
tiptoe. The interested gods had been watch-
ing joyfully, and when Stout and Important
ponderously produced opera glasses and con-
descendingly swept their perch, they howled
and whistled an ironic greeting. He then
hurriedly depressed his glasses to the dress
circle, which section of the house remained
comparatively calm during inspection. But
when the splendid being started to telescope
the stalls, the inhabitants rose in a body,
waved their hats and handkerchiefs and
cheered. The people of the King’s have had
a good deal of amusement out of various
splendid and scornful strangers, but the
illustrious person of last week scored a gen-
uine furore. In a manner of speaking, he
paid his dog-tax with interest.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Returned a week or so ago, from Canada and else-
where, Archie, the youngest of the Yalumba Smiths.
He is not enthusiastic over the immigration system
of the Cold Dominion, regarding it more as a blind
acceptance of European rejects than a policy. But
the railways win his most deafening cheers.
The football finals attracted a record crowd to the
Adelaide Oval on Saturday, and the yelling was
something phenomenal. The football revival is re-
sponsible for the recrudescence of the feminine
barracker, a fearful person with a raucous voice and
a penchant for stabbing perfect strangers with hat-
pins. If you are out in quest of “womanly charm
and modesty,” don’t stop to interview the female
barracker. She never has the goods about her; in
fact, it is doubtful if she knows them by sight.
The J. H. Gorses are here again for another brief
visit. The fortunes of Wills and Co. leg-rope him
pretty firmly to W.A.„ but his wife insists on his
periodically transplanting herself to her native State.
The H. C. Caves—she was a Bowman and he a
keen hunting man and M.F.H. for a year or so—are
back from nine months of England. They are stay-
ing at the Grand Central for a week or two before
going back to their station. They did some charm-
ing swimming—I mean motoring—on the famous
Ensrlish country roads.
The Cecil Brays—she also was a Bowman—have
been in Edinburgh with Miss Jean Stirling. They
will have dried themselves and got back here (d.v.)
by November.
Dr. C. Yeatman, of Auburn, an old St. Peter’s
boy, is engaged to Miss Mignon Jacob, of Hackney
Another wedding fixed for the near future is that of
Miss Duff, of Glenelg, to Mr. F. J. Fisher.
Mrs. Frank Downer gave what was universally
acclaimed as a successful garden party at Knights-
bridge on Thursday. By the way, I wonder what an
unsuccessful garden party would be like? A W
tio-n, 1 take it, where someone publicly kicked the host
and a waterspout fell on the guests, and burglars
made off with the family plate, and someone gave
the hostess’s grandfather in charge for pocket-pick
mg. Such fiascos are fortunately rare among our old
nobility. u

regalities dancing nervous breakdowns in the public
streets. Which would be unseemly to a degree and
would also interfere with the traffic. So you see the
State saves handsomely by providjng the big country
The Dudley Haywards are leaving on a year’s trip

Miss Anderson, of the Y.W.C.A., is off to America
for six months to see what further tips she can col-

The engagement is announced of Miss Barbara
Andrews, daughter of Canon Andrews, to Mr Nut-
ting, who is by trade a cleric.
There is joy among racegoers—not wild hilarity
and delirium, you understand, but the safer, matter-
gratification which one sees in the man who is
paid a ss. debt that has been owing for about four
years. ’The reason is the S.A. Jockey Club has de-
cided, at last, to double-deck its grandstand in the
half-crown enclosure. Also, the smaller of the lawn
stands is to be replaced by a 160 ft. erection in time
for next year’s Adelaide Cup.

DARK LAGER.
DOCTORS MEDICINE FAILED.
“I had influenza with congestion of the liver, took
the doctor’s medicine for some weeks and got no
relief,” said Mr. John S. Sweetman, Deep Lead,
Vic. “Then- I tried Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy,
and got immediate relief from the first dose. By the
time I had finished a large bottle the disagreeable
hacking cough and the pains in the chest disappeared
altogether.”*
IMPORTANT CATTLE SALE.
News that the Bushy Park stud Herefords are to
be dispersed comes to us from New Zealand. This
fine herd of cattle was, as most pastoralists will know,
founded upon Mr. James Stuckey’s Te Rangitumau
herd. It has been still further improved from time
to time by importations from the best strains of
Great Britain. Its present owner, Mr. Frank Moore,
is disposing of the whole of these famous cattle by
public auction in April, 1913, absolutely without re-

animals are practically all eligible for entry in the

POLITICAL
POINTS
According to one Smith, of Belfast, if the
British Parliament attempts to give Ireland
a local Legislature such as every Australian
State possesses, the rifles of Ulster “will go
off by themselves.” It was an unlucky
phrase. “The chassepots will go off by them-
selves,” was a proud and frequent remark
made in France just before the Franco-
German war. They didn’t go off by them-
selves. Still they went off. Some of them
went off in the air. Some went off to Ger-
many along with French prisoners. Some
went off to the rear in the general retreat.
And those that went off in the regular way
didn’t hit enough Germans to count. The
man who digs un an ill-omened remark like
that is worse than Poe’s raven —the “never-
more” animal.
THE MENACE OF THE BLACK HAND.
The facts, however, may he left with our readers.
Here is a Government which has just been forced to
declare that nothing appearing in our leading
columns last Friday week criticising the Speaker was
a breach of the privileges of Parliament, turning
round on the eve of a momentous decision by the
Speaker to shake a menacing finger at the press.
S. M. HERALD.
A hastily conceived tableau in which Granny
“stars” herself as the persecuted heroine — who,
in desperation, throws herself on the mercy of a
cold concentrated publicity.
Cabled last week that Pie Commissioner
Reid had been elected a vice-president of
London Sphinx Club, a newspaper adver-
tiser’s waddv which occasionally elects some-
one outside the puffing craft whom it con-
siders “has done signal duty for the Empire.”
Greid is too sound a sleeper for signal duty,
but he has certainly done great dinner
service.
An eminent French anti-militarist and
passive resister, just out of gaol, was given a
great reception the other day in Paris. Four
thousand people were present, and gave
12,000 cheers. Then, somehow, trouble
arose. The most intensely peaceful people
started shooting the less peaceful people for
not being peaceful enough, and brother smote
brother, to make him more brotherly, and
passive resisters trod actively on their best
friends. Few' people are madder than a
passive resister when he really runs amok.
Queensland, Westralia and Victoria show
the same symptoms as N.S.W. in the de-
crease of their railway profits. Queens-
land’s net revenue (out of which interest
has to be paid) fell from £1,167,311 (June,
1911), to £1,115,592 (June, 1912) ; that is
to say, from £4 10s. 2d. per cent, on capital,
to £4 os. 3d. per cent. Westralia’s net
revenue slumped gaily from £627,942 to
£540,627. Victoria’s net income went down
from £1,799,739 to £1,778,893.
N.S.W. Minister Griffith has decided to
send a heap of money abroad to pay for
imported iron bridges on the ground that
they can be made more cheaply in foreign
parts than by local labor. Thus Griffith
pushes up local wages manfully, and expects
great kudos in consequence, and at the same
time he declines to pay the wages because
they are too high. Which raises the ques-
tion : “What shall it profit a man if his
remuneration is 10s. an hour and he doesn’t
get it?” Of course it may be set forth that
the Australian has little experience at build :
ing iron bridges, but how is he to get the ex-
perience if he isn’t allowed to build bridges?
How would Griffith have got a start as a
Minister if, when it was first proposed to
make him a Minister, he had been turned
down on the ground that, never having been
a Minister, he was obviously unfit to be a
Minister ?
A DEVOTED AND PERSISTENT
NURSE.
During the debate on the Maternity Allowance Bill,
two pleas were made on behalf of colored inhabitants.
Needless to say, Bruce Smith was one who paraded
his piebald sentiments before an unsympathetic Par-
liament.
“Heavens I Is there no escape from this perio-
dical persecution? ”
Black, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), has a motion on
the business paper re the need for “national
parks in the interior, for the preservation
of wild beasts, birds, trees, flowers, grasses
and herbs now in danger of becoming ex-
tinct.” It is hereby moved that among the
beasts be included one non-borrowing State
Laborite —a species which seems more liable
to extinction than any other at present above
ground.
The Tasmanian Assembly has rejected the
Government’s proposal to authorise Hobart
municipality to buy out the Private Enter-
prise tramway co. for £210,000. The House
of Ass. lost its temper on two points: (1)
It was alleged that the price was too high;
and (2) it was mentioned that, under the
antiquated town franchise, only one-third of
the citizens would have any voice in the
management of the trams if they were
municipalised. The Government struggles
along with a precarious majority of about
one —that is to say, it has a majority of
one dissatisfied and hostile supporter; and
when a bitter friend deserted it over this
measure the scheme fell through. The Gov-
ernment is now going to negotiate for the
purchase of the trams on better terms, and
if nothing can be done, there are dreams and
visions about an opposition service of motor
’buses or something.

The new-horn republic makes the acquaintance of
an ancient friend of his older brethren, over
his first bottle.
The N.S.W. Upper House of life nom-
inees, which it is the business of the Labor
party to obliterate, but which it doesn’t seem
to obliterate worth twopence, has been tak-
ing a good many risks of late. The
McGowen Government can hardly go up the
stair to the gilded halls of Fat without
meeting one of .its pet Bills coming down
on its ear.
“Looking Backward” writes :
Senator Simon Fraser,
who viewed the turning of
the dust at Port Augusta,
was one of a firm of
three that built the line
from Port Augusta, 200
miles northward. They took
five years to do the job,
yet it is estimated by King
O’Malley’s roosters that
the 1060 miles between
Kalgoorlie and Port
Augusta will be done in
three years. On this point
I am Didymus. It means
a mile a day—half a mile
at each end; and there’s
nothing to show that it can
be done more than twice
as fast as Fraser and co.
(who worked from one end
only) did their task.
Fraser’s crowd worked
their men nine hours a
day (it was in 1878), and
paid them a bob an hour.
Princely conditions those
in an epoch when men
toiled with ox-like energy for a couple of dollars per
diem.
One of the most unlucky events in the
history of Australian trades-unionism was
the recent strike of municipal employees at
Broken Hill. The Coupcil, which is largely
a Labor body, found itself driven to re-
trench, so it decided to reduce the number
of its employees by one. It was never under-
stood that economy should be illegal or that
an employee’s job should be immortal, yet,
and despite the fact that they were working
under agreements by which they pledged
themselves to regard strikes as wholly out
of the question, the whole municipal force
dropped its shovel, and left the aggregate
backyard of Broken Hill to breed typhoid in
the early summer sun. The strike didn’t
last long, but it is one more of those un-
happy episodes which make the solemn
league and covenant of a trades union look
of just as much value as a Japanese per-
son’s oath.
Professor Rentoul, of Melbourne, has been
clamoring gaily to the Presbyterian brother-
hood upon “race suicide” in Australia; about
how “race murder outweighs the increases
by immigration,” and also about how the ten-
dency to limit population “is more deadly
to the British Empire than would be a long-
continued war.” It is a pity that wild but
well-meaning fossils qf the Rentoul stamp
don’t know more about figures. The popu-
lation of Australia is growing faster by ex-
cess of births over deaths than that of any
other country on earth which keeps records,
save, perhaps, Bulgaria. And as for the
British Empire—well, poor old England and
Wales had 153 people to each square mile in
1801; 175 in 1811; 206 in 1821; 239 in
1831; 273 in 1841; 308 in 1851; 344 in
1861; 390 in 1871; 446 in 1881; 498 in
1891; 558 in 1901; and in 1911 thev had 619
folk to each square mile. From sheer over-
crowding the race is beginning to degenerate
visibly in England, and, if the process goes
on it will soon begin to suffocate as well,
and the place will look like a ship’s fore-
castle. What does Prof. Rentoul want?
Melbourne’s Board
of Shirks has made a
move. It has decided
to refuse to sewer al-
lotments that it con-
siders too small for
human habitations,
and the occupiers of one
whole street in Foots-
cray have been told
they will have to pay
for putting down the
street sewer or go
without. This, of
course, doesn’t tend to
boost up the value of
recently cut up pad-
docks, and the thrifty
landowner who wants
to put three Queen
Anne villas where one
Mary Jane cottage
ought to be is cursing
the Board fervently.
Mrs. Berry, conspic-
uous in the Females’
National League, glee-
fully told a Ballarat
(Vic.) audience the
other day of one
woman in the Wan-
non district who had
said: “Fisher needn’t
think he is going to
stop me from voting.
I am going to vote if I have to
swim to the poll.” Probably Fisher had
never heard of the lady in question, and as a
matter of fact, she is given precisely the
same facilities for going to the poll as every
other woman in Wannon. The natural in-
ference is that the Females’ Irrational
League had been telling this simple creature
that the Prime Minister was responsible for
the floods.
Practically every member of the present
N.S.W. Government, but especially Griffith
and Trefle, is now an open and unabashed
apostle of borrowing—large, swift, and
abundant borrowing. Yet this Government
was elected on its solemn pledge or word of
honor —whatever that might be worth —that
it was going to reduce borrowing, and to
splash about a modified amount of loan
money only on works that would pay, from
the beginning, full interest and sinking fund.
There is something magnificent about the
brassy effrontery with which the McGowen
Ministry repudiates any of its principles
which don’t suit its inclinations, and brags
of its apostasy as if the Iscariot business
were a special virtue.
“ J.”: If proof were needed of the hopeless
provincialism of the Sydney press, the same
might be supplied by a consideration of its
reports of the turning of the first sod of the
transcontinental line at Port Augusta. Here
was a function as epoch-making as any that
has occurred in Australia in the last 10
years. All sorts of prominent folk speech-
ified, and all sorts of arresting and suggest-
ive things were said. Yet none of the papers
gave more space to the affair than they
would to a bolting cab episode. or a street
row between a couple of inebriates —which
is to say, about half a dozen lines. A prem-
iere at a third-rate theatre gets half a col-
umn in a prominent position; the transit
premiere of a continent, so to speak, is
accorded a stick of type in a stray corner
among the advts.

THE GENTLE JESTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AVhen Deakin tells the Women’s. League
His drollest little stories,
The dames, forgetting their fatigue,
Are gay and frisky Tories;
Their hearty laughter rends the air,
Their smiles are sweet as honey ;
It is delightful to be there
When Mr. Deakin’s funny.
. No grim and scornful critic he,
No snorting “tush”- and “pish”-er,
He cuts his jokes in boyish glee
At foolish Andrew Fish-er.
He gives the girls a merry glance
Whene’er he speaks of Money,
Or mentions Government finance.
Oh, Mr. Deakin’s funny.
He gaily hints at projects rash
On which the Caucus fritters
Immense, uncertain sums of cash
(Loud cheers and storms of titters).
Perchance he turns a household word
To purpose frankly pun-ny;
Then fifty fat old souls are heard
Protesting—“He is Funny l”
AVhat joy, what joy to hear him say,
His face with humor beaming,
“The Government has lost its way”—
Which sets his hearers screaming.
And if he speaks of “tart replies,”
Or calls a rabbit “bunny,”
The Leaguers mop their streaming eyes
And gurgle, “He’s too funny.”
The Government, there’s not a doubt,
Despite false Labor rumor,
Will take the knock and totter out,
Expelled by Deakin’s humor.
The land, now dark ’neath Caucus Rule,
Will soon be bright and sunny,
So pungent is the ridicule
Of Deakin, when he’s funny.
VICTORIA.

Melba, Irving, Hugh Ward, George Musgrove, Nellie Stewart and Paderewski are
gems of humor. The volume is a memorable one, and is the humorous classic of the

“About twelve months ago,” writes Sara
Drysdale, 338 Bourke Street, Sydney, “I
suffered from indigestion very badly. I also
thought, like my sister, I was developing
signs of consumption. As a remedy I
thought I would use Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills, and on the recommendation also of
my son I took them, three at a dose,
and afterwards reduced the dose to one
each night. As the result I found that there
was a wonderful improvement. Whereas _ I
got terribly thin before, I began to gain
flesh, and all symptoms of indigestion have
since left me. They, have improved me all
round. I was a terrible sufferer at the time
I speak of, and the J shooting pains in the
back and loss of appetite reduced my health
until I became a wreck of my former self,
but thanks to Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
I have not a moment’s illness, and can do
my work with pleasure. _ You can use this
if you desire for publication.”

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Each of four sires has two promising re-
presentatives in next Saturday’s A.J.C. Derby, anc
even if all those eight first acceptors do not go to the
post, Ayr Laddie, Malster, Wallace and 1' lavus will-
bar accidents, boils-over, and suchlike acts of God
make a great effort to add the gorgeous prize to the
pile of stakes already won. At time of writing, ana
with final acceptances not due till d hursday after-
noon Ayr Laddie has Cider (from Praleen) and
Cagou (from Tartar) in the list. Malster’s two are
Symetris (from Symmetry) and Bellara (fiom
(Amera). Wallace has Wolowa and Perdita, from
The Infanta and Eloping Lady respectively. The
hopes of the line of Flavus are Aunfer (from Queen-
bird) and Buneewa (from Bonding). In the same
stable as the last-named is Burn (Miner—Black
Bess), winner of the recent Rosehill seven-furlong
Guineas, but Buneewa beat him at Randwick last
season, and on form can beat him again when re-
quired to do so. Anyhow, the other Plavus one,
Aurifer, should beat either or both in the Derby.
Big events are so often won by the sick and the
halt that Victorian colt Wolawa claims support on
account of his recent dental and pedal troubles.
Symetris won the Hawkesbury Guineas, one mile, on
Saturday, but Aurifer, Cider and other good ’uns
were absent, and, besides, fillies have very rarely
annexed the azure ribbon. The same fact is against
Perdita, though Wallace’s daughter looks a stayer.
Cider has been doing the most Derby-like work, and
in some big event or other this spring should run
away frofn the curse of his sister, Apple Pie, the mare
of many minor places. As usual, a lot depends on
how the race is run. If the rivals, Aurifer and
Cider, cut each other’s throats in front from the
start, a late dash by something behind will probably
pip both on the post. And if the field loafs for a
quarter-mile or more, as it often does, the ensuing
charge upon the'post will, likely as not, result m the
iudge having to consult an oculist before and after
giving a verdict. Perhaps Aurifer will score the
A.J.C. Blue Ribbon, for Flavus and Fred Merton,
with Cider, Perdita and Cagou closest to him, after
which, Cider should annex the more difficult Vic.
Derby at headquarters.
***■*
“The Mug”: Since the A.J.C. Epsijm
weights appeared there have been four first favorites
for Saturday’s Mile dash. Havocorn was the first
popular pick, but gave way to Popinjay, who, in
turn, w#s outed by Cisco. And this week the list
is headed by the resurrected Westralian, Jolly Beggar,
who on Saturday won the mile race at Hawkesbury,
as mentioned above. He won it in Imin. SSisec.,,
with 81b. more than his Epsom burden. Blairgour,
however, would have won the race had his rider not
foolishly eased a bit, and, like Lot’s wife, looked
round, when well in front and near the post. Then
he turned to a pillar of mud. Other Epsom candidates
well cared for by the layers have been Sunlike, Vol-
sloane, Gigandra and, loli. Consequently, the race is
voted “very open,’’ which means that no one nag
sticks out as one of those paper and newspaper certs
which generally go down, and on which we have all
dropped good legal tender. In such a predicament
the average winning weight, brethren, for an event
will sometimes point to the winner. Well, 27 Ep-
som Handicaps have been run, and their aggregate
of winning weights is 210 st. 81b. That gives 7st.
111 b. and a fraction as the average winning weight
for the Epsom. But nothing is handicapped at 7st.
111 b., so it is necessary to go next door above__and
below At time of writing that procedure, collects
Hartfall at 7st. 121 b. on the one side, and Malthusian,
Blairgour and Sandbatli, at 7st. 101 b., on the other.
Tons of Westralian gold dust are reported to be in-
vested on Jolly Beggar for the Epsom, and on the
Epsom-Metrop. double, Jolly Beggar and Artesian.
They are in the one stable, Jolly Beggar belonging
to “Blue Spec’’ Connolly, and Artesian to J. O.
Peterson. Also, J. “Baron” is said to have backed
his Sunlike and Duke Foote to win a fabulous sum.
Other stable doubles have received similar advertise-
ment. Patrick Galilee gathers his richest harvest
from the public mania for backing Spring Doubles,
and newspapers never fail to aid and abet that form
of robbery.- To this gambler’s way of thinking,
Volsloane, with 9st. 61b., and one of Sculley s
Lochano (7st. 81b.) or Cisco (Bst. 71b.)—will fight
out the Epsom, and one of the same pair has as
good a chance in Monday’s Metrop. as anything, no
matter how much additional gloom may be shed upon
that event by Saturday’s running. #
Up at Clarendon, where oranges grow,
the Hawkesbury Racing Club drew a mighty crowd
on Saturday, to see the final display of form on
which the fatuous punter will decide how to skin
Christian Iscariot at Randwick’s big meeting, com-
mencing on Saturday. The supreme event was ot
course, the historic Guineas, the 10 entrants of which
are all up to the neck in the big events of the future.
The public packed its coin enthusiastically on Careel
and Cagou, at twos and fours respectively, and
sprinkled the dregs of its pockets over a couple of
others, and ignored the tempting 12 to one offered
against Symetris. But that 12 to one chance upset
the apple-cart, and beat Careel easily by three
lengths. Cagou ran third last, or thereabouts. So
passeth hard-earned splosh!” as the noble Roman
said. A week before, at Rosehill, the Rosehill
Guineas ended in a boil-over for Burri; so the Lord
knows how the punter will go about the skinning
of Abraham Smith in the £5OOO Derby on Saturday.
It’s a lean year among the three-year-olds, and things
are mixed.
As a guide for the Epsom Handicap the
Rowley Mile provided an interesting revelation.
Jolly Beggar, the prad which is at the head of the
illicit betting lists in- those Sydney dens where the
light of Yarrington has not yet penetrated, was so
heavily weighted with coin that Patrick Galilee s
odds against its chance of winning stopped at
five to four. But the Jolly one was equal to the
occasion, and gathered in the wagers, the prize, the
applause, and the confidence of the public, and all
others. His bundle in the Epsom is Bst. 41b. —eight
pounds less than he carried on Saturday ; so, provided
the brutes are honest and the humans who control
them square (nobody ever heard of them being other-
wise), Jolly Beggar’s chances look rosy. He holds
the Australian seven-furlong record (lmin. 25 2-5
sec.), which he put up at Perth . (W.A.), so he
should not find the big mile handicap beyond him.
Owner Connolly is said to have risked a mountain
of gold on his stable double, Jolly Beggar and
Artesian ; and if the first leg gets home there will be
many terrified Bags next week.
Lady Medallist, missing all her A.J.C. en-
gagements, has gone to Victoria with a view to win-
ning another Caulfield Cup, seemingly, not to men-
tion the Melbourne Cup in the event of her failing
to get penalised for a victory in the earlier race.
Lady Medallist’s chance of doing what Paris did long
years ago is easily summed up. The mare carried Bst.
51b. in last Caulfield Cup, and came clean away from
the field in the run home. Another 121 b. would not
have stopped her then, it was thought, and her weight
this year is 9st. 31b.—only 121 b. more. Assuming
that Lady Medallist, as a six-year-old, can repeat her
last year’s form, and that the field will be no stronger
than before, the excellence of her chance is question-
able only on the supposition that, being a rather
light-waisted mare, she will be more affected by the
extra 121 b. than her friends suppose.
At time of writing, a lot of Victorian
punters hang on tenaciously to the hope that Blair-
gour, the inbred son of Wakeful, will strike form on
Saturday, and run away with the Epsom Handicap
in the way that made him such a red-hot favorite
for the Newmarket Handicap 18 months ago. It was
in the Newmarket struggle that Blairgour blew out,
never to go again, thus far. But within the two or
three weeks previously he had looked invincible. He
flew the 51 furlongs for the Oakleigh Plate, and then
he led from end to end of the Caulfield Futurity
Stakes with Bst. 121 b. up. Broadsword and Comedy
King ran second and third in the Futurity, and both
of them did great deeds soon afterwards. For in-
stance, Broadsword ran third for the Newmarket,
wherein the hot favorite blew out so strangely, and
then went to Sydney and won the Doncaster. Blair-
gour will have to run a few more bad races e’er his
old Melbourne worshippers will withdraw their faith.
The memory of his Futurity Stakes victory dies hard.
By the way, Popinjay captured the same race last
year, and the Melbourne man in the street mostly
talks Popinjay or Blairgour for the Epsom.
The Adelaide Hunt Club has set the
veteran Seth Ferry, formerly M.F.H., remin'seing
to the Register about the first Cup, run for in 1869.
It was on the old Tliebarton course, where the S.A.
Jockey Club of that era had temporarily thrown in
its towel. Adam Lindsay Gordon rode the favorite,
Lancelot, owned by Blackmore (afterwards Clerk of
Federal Parliament). Ebenezer Ward, another mem-
ber of the State Parliament, also rah (or the horse
beneath him did), and laid Ferry 100 to two against
his Gipsy Girl winning the double, Cup and Hunters’
Steeple. Ferry took the same from three others, in-
cluding Charles Mann (afterwards Attorney-General)
and won the lot. Pat Auld (of the McDouall Stuart
expedition), who died last month, was another rider,
but Ferry and Ward and one besides are now the
only survivors of the big field.
“Query” : As a Victorian in N.S.W., I was
staggered the other day to read in a Sydney news-
paper that the “outer gate” for a Saturday race
meeting had been sold to a Mr. Solomon for £ 800-
odd. I would ask why—in the sacred name of the
Profit! —is it necessary to drag an outside speculator
into a racing club’s toll-gate business? True, the
V.R.C. does a little in this way as regards one or
two carriage gates at Flemington, but the amount
of money paid for the privilege of toll-collecting is
small, and the probable profit or possible loss on the
purchase is hardly worth worrying about. The outer
gate at Randwick is a very big consideration. The
speculator pays 17,000 shillings, or thereabouts, for
his chance of making a big profit on the outer gate
receipts at an ordinary Saturday meeting. Is the
same system of farming the gate adopted at a big
four days’ meeting, and if so, why doesn’t the club
manage its own turnstiles instead of dedicating- them
to the glory of Solomon? Of all the gamblers on
the Sydney turf, Solomon strikes an amazed stranger
as being the one who is backing the deadest bird, or
betting on the surest wicket. Is he an individual
or a syndicate ? Is the job of managing outer-gate
collections reckoned so arduous that the club is too
tired to undertake it? Anyhow, there can be no
good reason for selling the job. The J.O.W. Com-
bine, or the proprietary of any other well-organised
show, would be glad to supply all the necessary labor
and do the gate business on a small commission.
The great £lOOO international tug-of-war
commenced at the Mclntosh Stadium (Sydney), on
Saturday night last week, and proved an interesting
brand of struggle. The scene of the conflict was ; a
long, narrow platform running north and , south
across the centre of the Stadium. An indicating
machine stood in the centre, with a dial facing
every side of the enclosure. This indicator was ad-
justed to show the movement of the rope to one-
eighth of an inch. The teams lie down on their
sides, right and left of the rope alternately, with
their feet resting against the cleats. And then, at
the word “go!” they haul for their lives. Each
captain stands at the end of the line and watches
the rival team, and with his hands behind his back
signals to his team when and how to pull. The
daddy captain was he of the Scottish team, and he
handled his men like a solid machine. The Carlton
Brewery team, which has done such wonders, had
the unfair advantage of 1 pulling on short, six-inch
cleats. They did not let go of the rope or change
their grin upon it as the others had to. They just
worked themselves backward with their feet on the
short cleats, and the result was such a huge advan-
tage that nothing up to time of writing has been
able to stand against them. The mighty Heckenberg
family made a big pull with Fitzpatrick’s Australian
team, but, owing to lack of training and faulty
skippering, was beaten by 9in. after a desperate
struggle. At the end of Monday night the Inter-
national struggle had seen Ireland, Italy and Sweden
killed off. Scotland, Russia and Holland were run-
ning neck to neck with two points each. On
Monday night Fitzpatrick’s Australian team, which
bad defeated the Heckenbergs on Saturday, went
down in its turn before the Victorian team, lfed by
Captain Smith.
A record crowd of 54,463 swarmed into
the Metropolitan Cricket Ground on Saturday, to see
the grand final of Vic. League hoofball for 1912. Of
these about 10,000 were M.C.C. members, 4000 came
in on South Melb. F.C. tickets, 3500 on Essendon
do., 1000 on Geelong do., 500 on complimentary
tickets, and about 2000 were boys, who paid half-
fare. Total, £1950 2s. 6d., nearly £2OO more than
the largest sum hitherto recorded. And the game
wasn’t worth a quarter of the price. Both Essendon
and South were over anxious. A great part of
the time they dug their hoofs in the soil and
hung back in the harness. The ’Dons opened out a
bit and the Southerners indulged in some concerted
rushes in the last ouarter, but, by that time, the
game was gone so far as South was concerned, and
it was only striving to avert disgrace. In brief,
the ’Dons were faster, cleaner and surer than their
opponents, but any team-such as ’Roy or ’Wood—
that would play its ordinary, systematic game, with-
out bustling itself, would have made hacks of the
pair of them. The marking was poor, with the excep-
tion of that of the backs on either side, and the goal
sbootmg marked by stupid selfishness and awful in-
effectiveness. There was no outburst of unseemly
violence, but the clenched fist was seen at times,
and the elbow-iab frequently got in its deadly work.
Details are hardly worth recording. ’Don had an 11-
point lead at half-time, and, making the third quarter
all its own, added 11 points to South’s two. Start-
ing the last term thus, 20 points in arrear, the
Southerners had to do some strenuous dashes or die,
and the dashes weren’t there. So the uproar ended
with Essendon as Premiers for ’12 —with five goals
17 behinds to South’s four goals nine behinds.
The Ass. grand final at North Melbourne
also provided a record crowd and “gate”—32,ooo and
£405 respectively—and again the game wasn’t worth
the candle ; also, another Essendon stood the “minor
premiers” on their heads. As in the League game,
the ’Dons, by strenuous rather than skilful play,
gained a small lead, which they nurtured and cher-
ished till, at half-time, it amounted to five little
points. Reproducing their League brothers’ tactics,
they made the third quarter their own, reaching the
last spell with 50 points to ’Scray’s 21. The last
term was merely a matter of staving off disaster.
’Scray died hard, but a five-goal lead is too. much for
any team to wipe off in a close and hard-fought
final, and when the bell finally rang down the cur-
tain on the season’s comedy, Essendon was still 21
points ahead —62 to ’Scray’s 41. During thV game
David McNamara, who, three years ago, left St. Ivilda,
in a hurry and a violent temper, to .ioin Essendon,
kicked three goals, making his tally for the season
108, a record that is likely to stand for many years.
Many of these goals were booted, at the rate of a
dozen per afternoon, in matches against hopeless
teams like Melb. City and P’ran, but as other kickists
had similar easv chances, the record, such as. it is,
stands. Mac is 29, weighs 14st. 51b., and is 6ft.
2in. long.
* * * *
On the Australian football ground, Syd-
ney, on Saturday, another Rawson Cup was the bone
of a mighty contention, under Australian rules, be-
tween Sydney and Paddington. The referee who was
to have held the packs in the leash sprained his
ankle, and was unable to run for his life, so a sub-
stitute took on the job. It was beyond him, and the
teams lost their tempers, and the game became more
willing than scientific. At half-time another referee
was discovered and placed in charge, and he set to
work to smooth things out. But the blood of the
clans was up, and the game continued to be unduly
savage, with the result that injuries . were numerous
and varied. One gentleman sustained a broken
collar-bone. Sydney led from the jump, and gave an
exhibition of pace and combination that staggered
the minor premiers very badly. Sydney has held the
Rawson Cun for two years .running, and if it could
win this time it would be the permanent possessor.
So, naturally, it played its darnedest —and so did
Paddington. But vim and energy are useless when
opposed by just as much of the same sort of thing
combined with disciuline and combination, and Pad-
dington’s chances slowly withered away under the
deadly kicking of the Sydney feet till, at the final
bell-ringing, the score was 43 points to 15. A
crushing defeat. So Sydney retains the last of the
Cups donated by dead and gone Harry Rawson.
Balmain is walking the diamond ridge of
fame in the Soccer competitions, its B team having
raged right through two seasons without being once
defeated. Thereby it has annexed for all time the
coveted Association Cup, and has a right to carry it
into eternity. Wanted, now, a philanthropist to put
up another Cup to take the place of that which Bal-
main team has permanently annexed. West Sydney
was runner-up in this competition, and was, prac-
tically, the only other team which had a show of
gathering in the mug. The scores of the two clubs
stand thus : - . .
Balmain B —l 4 played, 13 Won, lost none, drawn
one; points 27.
West Sydney—l 4 played, 12 won, lost one, drawn
none ; points 25.
In the Rawson Cup Soccer competition Balmain is
also on velvet. It holds the utensil, and if it can
defeat Sydney next Saturday it will take permanent
possession of the well-beloved old Viking’s drinking
machine ; and it sets more store on Harry Rawson’s
Cun than it does on the Association Jug. Sydney and
Balmain A are dead level on points. Only Death can
decide between them, and one of them must die on
Saturday next. Both these teams stamped their op-
ponents flat on Saturday, Sydney overthrowing New-
town by two goals to one, and Balmain routing the
Navy by four to nil. So, on the form, Balmain’s
chances of annexing both the coveted Cups in the
same season are rosy.
First-grade cricket started in Sydney on
Saturday. Weather divine, wickets excellent, and
players in, apparently, good fettle; and, although
no century graced the perfect afternoon, some solid
scoring was done in the aggregate. University, out
at Waverley, fell on the local men, and banged out
369 for the loss of seven wickets. Glebe put
up 231 in spite of the struggles of Balmain, and
Sydney made 215 against Paddington at Rushcutter’s
Bay. Paddington found time to knock up 22 without
losing a stick before time ran out. Central Cumber-
land, otherwise Parramatta, heaped up 140 against
North Sydney, which replied with 172 for the loss
of one wicket only. A sharp reply indeed. Burwood
accumulated 197 against Middle Harbor, which made
a rotten start by losing six wickets, for 52. Peter-
sham brought capital into the country by scoring 196
off the bowling of Gordon, which retorted with 54
for the loss of two wickets. Among the individual
performers the best were Single, of University, who
got within two of the century. Rogalsky, of Sydney,
carried his bat for 89, and two other timber carriers
were Cooper and Moore, of North Sydney, who did
themselves proud with 84 and 68 respectively. Among
the bowlers the best were Dunain, of Burwood, who
took three for 16 ; Shortland, of Gordon, who got
seven for 62 ; Hordern, of North Sydney, whose bag
was six for 61 ; McCloy, of Paddington, with three
for 30; and Noble, of the same club, with four for
49.
* * * *
Young J. W. Hearne was right up to his
best Australian form in the two “Gentlemen” v.
Players’ matches this English season. The youngster
scored 260 runs in four innings, carrying his bat
three times. Also, he bowled usefully, taking eight
wickets in all. On Ena-lish wickets Hearne can do
a bit as a googler. Talking of slow cricket, London
Referee says: “Sussex v. Lancashire was a melan-
choly ceremonial. Vine occupied three hours in mak-
ing 45. In smin. under two hours, Sussex compiled
80 runs before lunch, afterwards maintaining much
about the same rate of ‘speed.’ The long and the
short of it is that people will not go to see this sort
of thing.” The short and the long of it is, that
cricket, to maintain its grip on intelligent public
interest, must be played under conditions which will
provide a three to one on chance of any county or
jnter-State or Test match being played out in two
long days. It has got to be made a strenuous game,
in which the spectacular incidents and surprises come
closer together, instead of being strung out at inter-
vals so wide apart that the average ’ “enthusiastic
supporter” is apt to be either nodding on a bench,
or boosing at the bar when the occasional sensational
incident occurs. Cricket has got to be played as
though time were a matter of some importance to all
concerned, and Beer were not the necessary brain food
of its chief exponents and supporters. Something of
the nervous force of lawn tennis must be introduced
into it, by, adding height and width to the wickets,
and taking a heavy discount off the score of the side
which doesn’t amass a given number of runs in a
stated time. Why not penalise the slow goers? It
is done in horseracing. The full stake offered to the
Champion Stakes winner is subieet to the condition
that three miles is covered in 5f minutes.

Sydney Sailing Club and the Flying
Squadron combined on Saturday to open the sailing
season in style, and succeeded admirably. ltaces
were started promptly and in a most business-like
manner, and run off excellently. The wind was trom
the E.N.E., and well behaved. The important race
of the day was that of the 18-footers. The umpire
got on the warpath early, and Mona started the
season by being disqualified. Two new boats, Kismet
and Desdemona, made first appearances, but tailed
to set the harbor on fire. Still, they have plenty ot
time. A rather pretty race resulted in Golding beat-
ing the fleet, followed by Scot, Livonia, Nimrod and
the new boat Kismet. These five divided £2O among
them, so, after all, it must be admitted that Kismet
did fairly well on its maiden trip.
N.S.W. bowling team, which went clamor-
ing through Queensland, came back over the border-
line, tanned to the color of mahogany, and walloped
black and blue. Not one solitary win was registered,
even against the last, least and most humble Queens-
land club. Still, the tour was a moral success. In
fact, The Bulletin lias it on the word of one of
the returned escapees that every game was practically
a victory, and the defeat was purely technicaL
The Victorian cycling difficulty has been
settled by the formation of a Road Board, which will
in future have charge of all matters connected with
road racing in Victoria. The Board will consist of
four representatives of the Cycle Traders, four of the
Inter-Club Cycling Association, and three from the
League. The new body will be under the suzerainty
of the League, but, apparently, the trade interest is
going to have a big voice in the actual management
of it. And, although this case may be an excep-
tion, the influence of the trade and stock house is
almost always evil.

An extract from an Amurkan paper about
this “Cafe de Champion,” which needs no comment:
“An extremely witty commentary on the opening
of Jack Johnson’s sporting place in Chicago appeared
the other day in the Minneapolis Record, penned by
John Ritchie, a clever local writer on sporting topics.
After first printing the despatch telling about the
silver cuspidors, the handsome oil paintings, the
silver and gold bar and the souvenir pictures of
himself with the tribute to his mother, Bitehie
writes: —-
“ ‘All of this grandeur-and wealth of Golcpnda is
possessed by. a youthful dinge, who a few years ago
looked upon the copping of a mess of pork and beans
as a modern equivalent to the miraculously bestowed
manna, and ham hocks and cabbage as little less than
a personal bestowal of a special Providence.
“ ‘ Tis a far cry from the hump and rattle of the
brake beam and the uncertain findings of the free
lunch to personally monogrammed cuspidors at
867.50 per cusp. And “real handpainted oil Paint-
ings” in the entertainment room is stepping along a
bit as well. We imagine that among the handpainted
treasures of art may be found copies of “Desde-
mona and Othello,’’ “Cupid and Psyche,” and that
time-honored and conventional bar-room canvas, “Paul
and Virginia,” with Paul done as a Senegambian
prince eloping with the accepted Virginia.
“‘We commend the champion for bis restraint in
publishing only 32 portraits of himself in the pro-
gramme. Such modesty is most becoming to a man
wbo has reached the pinnacles of fame occupied by
Mr. Johnson.
“ ‘We cannot find heart to gibe the homily directed
toward the unoffending Mrs. Tiny Johnson —but why
the discrimination against Old Man Johnson? Why
do we never hear anything of the father of the
Chicago Polyphemus? We know not whether he be
living or has passed on, but taking the son for a
sample of the sire, it is a 100-1 shot that few mokes
there were who ever sought to tamper with the lock
on the chicken house door when Old Man Johnson
was around.
“‘The Caf6 de Champ., though, seriously consid-
ered, stands as an inspiration to the obscure and am-
bitious young scrappers of the land. Mr. Johnson
has demonstrated that the Elysian Fields, the Isle
of Calypso, the gardens of Hesperides and the
Golden Apples are attainable. He has served his time
and generation as a living exemplification that the
Alps can after all be scaled, and also —as the Sauk
-Centre sweet girl graduate is wont to proclaim—
beyond the Alps lies Italy.’ ”
London Daily News, which belongs to
Cadbury, the cocoa millionaire ' manufacturer and
peace-at-any-price Quaker, gives no countenance or
space to sports into which the gambling element may

stance, one of them, describing a match between

bull: “'Woolley adopted the policy of tempting the
batsmen to hit the rope with which to hang them-
selves.” ■ ■*

“spirit of friendship.”
Offer it to your friends as a
beverage or as a smooth and ex-
quisite liqueur ! Pour it —a liba-
tion on the Altar of Friendship.
There is none more fitting.
Martell’s is, moreover, a potent
medicinal spirit. It is the bene-
ficial purity of Martell’s that gives
the ailing, who are compelled to
take stimulants, faith in its splen-
did virtues.
