“Brass Button”: A whole shipload of
interesting items has been rising to the sur-
face in the Royal Commission’s inquiry into
the life-saving appliances of the Port Phillip
Bay (Vic.) steamers. All the particulars of
overcrowding the boats, of lifeboats and
rafts for two or three dozen people out of
the many hundreds on board, and other
clamorous details mentioned from time to
time, were naturally known to everybody
except a Royal Cornish. It appears that the
police have no power to prevent overcrowd-
ing on these boats. They can count the
people as they surge aboard and watch them
perch on the funnel or sit on the wheel and
cluster like flies on a lump of sugar wliere-
ever they can find foothold; and they can
watch the boat crawl out to sea loaded down
to the gun’l with humanity; and they can
then make a report, and a prosecution may
follow. That is well enough in its way, but
it won’t be much consolation if a collision
occurs out in the bay in the meantime and
99 per cent, of the passengers are drowned.
The Marine Board certainly has powers to
stop the ship if it thinks there is dangerous
overloading, but the trouble is that the
Board either isn’t there at the moment or is
somewhere else. A demonstration was given
in court re the method of fitting on a life-
belt. It took a couple of men three minutes
to fasten on one belt, and as the Judge
watched the performance and eyed the cum-
brous looking arrangement he feelingly re-
marked that after this exhibition he’d feel
safer without a life-belt. A nice problem is
presented here. If it takes two men three
minutes to fix a life-belt on in the sleepy
security of a courthouse, how long will it
take one woman in shrieking hysterics to do
it in one minute when there is a probability
of the ship foundering in half a minute? If
you stir a dark night, a whipped cream sea
and a howling gale into the mixture the pro-
blem is even more complicated. Witnesses
admitted that if a life-belt was placed too
low on the body the swimmer would float
safely, but feet uppermost, and an Annette
Kellerman herself might then find it an awk-
ward job to rise to the occasion right side
up. It was also admitted that if the neck
tape came untied the belt would most likely
slip down and the passenger slip up the
Golden Stairs. As the average man never
saw a life-belt put on, and the average
woman never saw one at all-—except neatly
stowed in a rack somewhere, when she pro-
bably took it for a part of the ship’s furni-
ture or a spare sail or a rat trap—there is
a fair chance of the belt either being ignored
or put on wrongly, in which case it is a cer-
tain and sudden death belt. However, a
recommendation was made that pictures be
put in the cabins showing how to fit the
thing on, so all is well. Other cheerful items
floated up. Some of the steamers were
shown to be built of plates from 5-16ths of
an inch to |in. thick. That sort of boat
couldn’t reasonably be expected to float half
an hour if it tore its underskirt, and even
with watertight bulkheads (which many
haven’t got) it would sink in a collision.
Anyhow, the inquiry may do some imme-
diate good, and any odd passenger who
hasn’t got his life insurance policy in order
may ston nshore and the next boat put out
with only 1999 passengers instead of 2000.
From “Henry Horsecollar” :
In my opinion the most futile thing that has yet
happened in the way of suffragette agitation was
that matter of the lady who “picked up a large
worm and threw it in the face of the First Lord of
the Admiralty.” In the first place, the lady
slinked, with great trouble and difficulty, into the
Balmoral “goff” links where Winston Churchill was
“goffing.” She apparently had no written or illumin-
ated address or plan of campaign with her, and de-
pended Wholly on the chapter of accidents and the
inspiration of the moment. She might drive Mr.
Churchill’s hat hard down on his head, or she might
throw grass at him, or she might simply rush past
him calling out “Votes for Women.” She was a
disorganised and leaderless army of one, advancing
without ammunition. Then she saw the worm, and
' threw it —and probably broke it. Doubtless she
hated the worm a great deal worse than he did, for
he probably goes fishing, and she probably doesn’t.
People who fish are acquainted with worms. She
had to hold the shuddery thing in her fingers for a
few seconds, while it simply struck Churchill and
rebounded, so as far as unpleasant association with
the worm was concerned the plaintiff was a great
deal worse off than the defendant. Probably the
worm had recoiled some feet before Churchill knew
what had happened, so he escaped the actual un-
pleasantness, while the lady experienced all of it.
To be hit with a worm, when you don’t know till
afterwards that it is a worm, is no worse than being
hit with a fragment of meat the day before yester-
day, but to carry a worm when you know it is a
worm is plain horrible.
There is room in this suffragette movement for a
genius, like these bogus men of mystery Sherlock
Holmes and Arsene Lupin, or even like that crude
but genuine humorist, Theodore Hook. The suffra-
gette body should circumvent its enemies and under-
mine them. It should move in a mysterious way its
wonders to perform, and leave no footprint behind.
It should be a voice under the bed in the middle of
the night, yet the most earnest search under the bed
should reveal no one there. It should be a ghost
and a spook and a hidden influence —an unseen hand
feeling in the pocket of its enemy—an invisible
power upsetting his inkbottle over his most valuable
papers, and putting candle-ends in his tea, and large
dead frogs in. his soup; and cutting the bottom out
of his hansom cab, so that he will fall through into
the street, and be left lying there amid public deri-
sion while the cab drives on with nothing in it save
atmosphere. It should know its political foe’s ap-
pointments with his best girl, and send his wife
there to keep the pair company. When the First
Lord has arranged to make a great speech about the
navy, this vast but silent conspiracy of women should
abstract his notes at the last second and replace
them by comic songs and snuff. When he attends
church in a devout spirit it should be the utterance
of an able ventriloquist, making him say “hooray”
or “cock-a-doodle-doo” when he, meant to say
“amen.” And if it does resort to the crude device
of throwing things at its opponents it should select
its missiles more carefully. Two gallons of indelible
black ink poured over the Chancellor of the Ex-
chequer on the night when he had arranged to de-
liver his budget would make far more sensation than
the throwing of worms and axes and such-like ob-
jects.
To return to the worm episode-—what earthly good
is one worm? Suppose Winston Churchill had gone
to bis bath-room in the morning—he probably does
go—and had locked the door, and hung his kimono I
on the nail, and stepped gaily under the shower!
Suppose, instead of water, hundredweights of worms
had fallen on him! Suppose that he had got out
with a shriek and a howl and a bellow and a curse,
and had rushed for his kimono, and it had mysteri-
ously vanished! Suppose he struggled like a mad-
man covered with worms to open the door and
escape naked to his bedroom, and the door was un-
accountably fastened on the outside! Suppose he
hollered through the keyhole for Jeames Yellow-
plush, and Tummas Breeches, and John Henry
Calves, and Mary Ann, and even the cook! And
suppose nobody came, and nothing happened, save
that a paper was pushed under the door bearing the
mysterious words, “Votes for Women!” That would
be something like an episode. It would make Par-
liament sit up. It would arouse a shriek of ridicule
that would be heard all over Europe. The story of
how a nude distracted First Lord threw tons of
worms out of the window and called wildly for the
police would create such ribald mirth that the minis-
terial career of Winston Churchill would be ended.
And even if the perpetrator of the crime were dis-
covered she would merely be offered a big music-hall
engagement. But the suffragettes never work like
that. A wonderful crowd of ’bus-losers are these
’gettes.
“Harrison O.”: A knowledge of certain
legal subtleties might save the modern crim-
inal a heap of unpleasantness. Alan Moore,
one of the few misdemeanants captured in
Melbourne of late (he was caught, not by
one of the O’Callaghan minions, but by
civilian Geoffrey Syme), has been sentenced
to death on the capital charge of burglary
with wounding. A man who merely wounds
somebody, however dangerously, in the chest,
or street, or broad daylight, is not liable to
the death penalty, but he who inflicts a few
trifling scratches on anybody while engaged
in a burglary, is liable. But then a really
superior, well-educated “crook” would be
careful not to commit a burglary. For in-
stance, if W. Sikes enters a house through
an open door or window, and removes a silver
cruet, a gold coal-scuttle, a diamond-studded
horse collar and a bowl of goldfishes, he is
not a burglar. Unless it happens that the
window was not open quite wide enough and
he shoved it up a trifle, or the door was not
sufficiently ajar and he gave it a push, in
which cases he burgles. That is the case, |
except when he conducts the pushing or shov-
ing operation prior to 9 p.m.. for in that
case, since time is the essence of the contract, !
he would be a 'mere housebreaker, and any
wound he inflicted would happen during a
housebreaking, and not during a burglary.
A crook citizen may appropriate the ancestral
jewels with honor, together with the inf ant’s
money-box and the family tree, before nine
o’clock at night, and be a comparatively
minor offender, but if he merely annexes the
family skeleton at 9.5 p.m., he is a desperate
criminal within the meaning of tlie Ad. So
the confisentor who knows his business doe®
a little comparatively innocuous housebreak-
ing before 9 p.m., while the family is prob-
ably at a theatre or a lecture on Christian
Science, instead of perpetrating a dastardly
offence late at night, when everybody is at
home and armed with a revolver. The law j
on these points should be printed on a dozen
small cards, and one handed to each of
O’Callaghan’s prisoners, to read in his cell.
The so-called ethics of racing, especially
pony-racing, are curious articles, which
should be kept in a cool store or a museum,
for Wowsers to inspect when they require a
tonic. The Melbourne action in which Sin-
clair Adkins, owner of the pony Ladylike,
sued citizen Wren for alleged libel, dis-
covered a cheerful person, named Kings.
Kings swore that he had trained the pony
with others, for the absurdly small sum of
10s. per week, and knew her quite well, and
that anyone who asserted she was in reality
the disqualified pony Truelove was a pre-
varicator and a weather-prophet and a bal-
ance-sheet. He admitted in cross-examina-
tion that his reputation wasn’t exactly lily-
white ; in fact, he had been disqualified more
than a score of times all over the country
for “doing what the owner told him.” He
always did what the owner told him to do.
Sometimes the owner’s plans didn’t work
out too well, as at Jubilee Park, where he
rode a prad named Fly in a race in which
there were but three starters. He had orders
not to win; but, unfortunately, the two
other jockeys had similar instructions, so
the three of them pulled their mokes up and
tried to see which could walk the slowest.
The race wasn’t run out, but the three riders
were, temporarily. The witness, however,
was speedily in the saddle again, at Nar-
nar-goon, where he persuaded a four-months-
old pony, called Gracie, to cover four fur-
longs in 52(1 sec. Kings was such a casual,
happy-go-lucky cuss that he raced all horses
in his charge, no matter who was the owner,
in his own colors; and as he didn’t have to
give any pony’s pedigree he never gave one.
One nag he raced at Richmond he had got
at Ballarat from a “man called Tom.” He
seems to have trained all and sundry beasts,
at 10s. ner week, and to have taken all the
responsibility of entering and racing them,
in his own colors, just for the fun of the
thing, and, presumably, a small wager or
two. He was a remarkable product of pony
life, and it was no wonder that C.J. Madden,
on the Bench, inspected him carefully and
thoughtfully as he left the box.
WAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ten by ten we went our way,
Ten by ten in the dawning grey.
One by one we came at night,
One by one in the dull twilight,
Ten by ten we went with pride,
Ten by ten in the last wild ride.
One by one we came with pain,
One by one and never again
Ten by ten will we go our way,
Ten by ten to the fighting gay.
One by one they shot us down,
One by one, leader and clown.
Ten by ten the graves they fill,
Ten by ten they lie on the hill.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
I whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.
' E.W.H.: “Bosom” jokes have to be very
carefully selected. Yours is a random and ill-chosen
bosom joke Cynicus: You ask: '“When will woman
realise that her sphere extends to the home, and no
further?” Dunno! Certainly, Quden Victoria didn’t
realise it, nor did other sovereigns, including Semir-
-5 amis, Catherine the Great, Maria Theresa, Mary of
Scotland, Isabella of Castille, Elizabeth of England,
Mary 11. of England, the late Empress of China, and
a lot more. And the fact didn't dawn on Florence
Nightingale, Elizabeth Fry, Joan of Arc, the Maid of
Saragossa, and others. Anyhow, when next you are
laid up, get a male nurse. And next time your wife
is ill get a male nurse. Then you will live up to
your theory S.H.W.: These remarks about the
birth-place and origin of Albert Dorrington, the
clever story-writer, were based on his own statements
made to a man who is still on The Bulletin staff.
So when you say that you knew him in infancy in
One Horse Gulch, in Nevada, near Gippsland, close
beside Fiji, on the edge of Brazil, you are probably
confusing him with someone else.... Scutcher: The
joke that is made up of question and answer must
have some air of probability about it. For instance,
when it is necessary, in order to pave the way for the
jest, that a man in evening dress should stop a
policeman with a cocked hat in front of a tram car,
and ask if the street lamp is a new moon, the position
is too laborious.... liowhai: The Maoriland gauge is
3ft. 6in. .. . J.G.C.: Dunno. Try one of the local
libraries. . .Bellerive: Glad to hear from you again:—
A WOMAN’S REVENGE.
Oh Wilfred why do you shun me
Appealed a woman as her blue eyes met his own.
Begone Ileen said the artist
My love for thee like a bird has flown.
A cry broke from the lips of the unhappy AVoman
The false deceiver had ruined her life
As he turned with contempt towards his easel
Into his side she plunged a knife.
’Twas a fatal thrust his brow turned livid
As he sank convulsively to the floor
She had fulfilled her deed of revenge and horror
Into a pool oozed the victim’s gore.

years. Norman Lindsay, taking a line by your black
and white work, averred passionately that you could
not be more than seven. In the end a quarrel en-
sued between these estimable toilers, as a result of
which they have not taken gin together for some
hours... .J. 5.8.: By a marvellous effort of per-
spective you have made the lady look as if she was
walking on a roof and the two men were standing
on a pavement about 80ft. wide.

AND ALL STATES
Pinero has been expressing regret that the Ameri-
can drama is not better appreciated in London. Plays
that have been huge and pronounced successes in the
States prove lamentable frosts when brought across
the Atlantic. “It is not,” says Pinero, “that Eng-,
lishmen are prejudiced against plays that illustrate
American national character and ventilate American
problems. They are. just not interested —that’s all.
It is the misfortune of Englishmen to be so slow
and self-contented.” Pinero praises the Americans
for being just the reverse. They are alert and re-
ceptive. His own plays all deal with English life,
which is very different from American, and yet not
a single one of his has failed in America because it
was not understood. As a matter of fact, Pinero
declares, “delicate little points which I have tried to
make for my English audiences missed them com-
pletely, while Americans grasped them immediately.”
Pinero says American actors speajc better than Eng-
lish ones. They enunciate their words: more clearly.
English actors are not trained nowadays. A return
to the old stock company system is badly needed.
That was the most perfect training school for the
actor. In writing his plays, Pinero tries to forget
“the innocent young person and the tired business
man.” He writes from within himself, and therein
lies the chief pleasure of his work. He, also likes to
vary his themes. Latterly he has been rather serious,
but’ some day he may return to the lighter subjects
in which he scored his earliest successes. At least
he thinks he will, but he won’t. When age crawls,
upon us,, one of the first things we lose is the capac-
ity to be humorous. The oldest inhabitant may still
cackle at the old jokes, but he doesn’t build new
ones.
“F.C.”: I don’t know whether this is an instance .
of casualness or one of the reasons why some of our
infants fail.to reach,a mature, age, ; but— , The, other
Saturday afternoon an attendant broke into the
middle of a Melbourne suburban picture show, with
a loud remark to the effect that there was a small
infant in a go-cart bawling lustily on the pavement
outside the entrance, and, “did any of the ladies pre-
sent own the child?’’ A young woman, old enough
to have known better, straiglitly arose and stepped
outside and presently returned with the comforted
mite, and the pair settled down placidly to consume
the rest of the , programme. As the iron roof had
given indications of two smart hailstorms, as well as
some rain—and the- child’s condition showed it had
caught most of it —I don’t fancy the nipper’s health
would be improved thereby. But the lady didn’t
worry ; the pictures were the chief consideration.
One of Hugh Mclntosh’s first deals after taking
charge of the Rickards circuit was to re-engage
Will Whitburn. And the move was a sound one, as
witness the amazing reception that was accorded
William the other night. He is the most successful
comedian who ever appeared at the Opera House,
Melbourne, though, curiously enough, in Sydney he
has never drawn worth three penn’orth of birdseed.
Huge Deal Mclntosh stipulated, and wisely, that
Whitburn should do no new stuff, but just repeat the
old successes and the old gags.
How many “God’s Own Countries” are there? So
the late Dick Seddon and Tom Bent were wont to
sum up Maoriland and Victoria respectively, and
doubtless there are people in Patagonia and Kams-
chatka who also consider their respective lands the
special favorites of the Almighty. There is, in fact,
an Icelandic proverb: “Iceland is the best land on
which the sun shines.” Oscar Hainmerstein, the New
York Jew, who has dropped a quarter of a million
in giving the Londoners an opera house that they
didn’t want, has returned to America a sadder and a
wiser man. His first words to the New York re-
porters were: “By Jove, boys, it’s good to be back
again in God’s Own Country.” He added that he
would rather be dead in New York than alive in
London. Well, it is better than being of the Stink-
ing Fish persuasion, and calling your native land
Dog’s Own Country, as some do.
“U.S.”: Belasco was sued for damages, a few weeks
ago, by Abraham Goldknopf, a barber, on a charge of
piracy from a drama called “Tainted Philanthropy.”
Belasco rejected this, but Goldknopf claimed that it
had been embodied in a piece called “The Woman.”
The case was exactly similar to Oomvns Carr’s com-
plaint, that Pinero, in “The Squire,” pillaged his
adaptation of Hardy’s “Far' From the Madding
Crowd.” Belasco gave the court a most amusing
performance of the outrageous extravagance in
“Tainted Philanthropy,” not a bit like “The
Woman.” Then he turned to Goldknopf with, “My
play is political. You crazy dramatist, there ar.e no
politics in your play. The two are as much alike
as ‘Hamlet’ and ‘Bertha, the Sewing Machine Girl.’”
“Aha!” said Goldknopf’s lawyer, “You read his
play?” “Read it; of course I did! It is no more
like ‘The Woman’ than an elephant’s ear is like
your mouth. Your Honor, I’ll • produce both plays
for you to come and see, and decide.” Judge Holt:
“Rather costly that.” Belasco: “I’ll willingly .sacri-
fice $2OOO to show this fraud up.” Goldknopf: “Oh,
that’s all right. But you must give me actors as
good.” Belasco: "Better! better! Your butcher’s
plot shall be staged.” Judge Holt appointed a date
in October when he would see both plays at Belasco’s
theatre.
“Harrison O.”: A short time ago a Poverty Pointer
desired to be informed of a play which contained a
journalist who was not represented as entitled to the
unqualified disrespect of a right thinking audience.
Since then tvvo Australian-made plays with a journ-
alist as hero have been inflicted upon Melbourne.
The first was Mularkey’s “For Homestead and
Honor.” and the second is Cosgrove’s “The World,
the Flesh and the Devil,” at present exploding at
the Bijou. In this piece too, the heroine announces
in clear, ringing tones:' “Journalism is one of the
noblest professions”—a stirring line which never
fails to refrain from winning loud applause. Neither
of the dramas mentioned ever possessed the remotest
prospect of success. Does an inkslinger hero kill a
play ; or is the damnation of a still-born drama likely
to be mitigated by an attempt of this kind to
flatter the faithful followers of the daily press? Also,
is it with the latter object in view that Cosgrove
make's his Perth pressman-hero a person of unblem-
ished frock coat, and the possessor of a tall hat calcu-
lated to fill Bruce Smith with envy?
A COMPLAINT OF MUCH MUSIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I want to be in pleasant places old
Where some lone shepherd tooted,
Or Marsyas in a Phrygian vale of gold
At qui;et evening fluted;
I want to feel as those whose hearts were bold,
Ere German bands were mooted.
A wandering minstrel, drunk with horns of mead,
Could set his wild notes ringing,
The fame of beauty or the valiant deed
To hall and castle bringing;
I think I could endure at utter need
His most envenomed singing.
But here, the dull suburban atmosphere
Piano strains are cheating,
Shrill, tuneless fiddles fill the dark with fear,
Wild phonographs are bleating,
And, by the corner sacred to brown beer,
Salvation’s drums are beating.
Somewhere the poet sits with maddened hair,
His lines to cuss-words chiming,
While up the ladder of the tortured air
A thousand discords climbing,
Make all his thoughts one elemental swear,
Reflected in his rhyming. ,
I want to be in pleasant places old,
To hear Apollo flinging
His splendid music from a lyre of gold
Down vales with rapture' ringing,
/ want to feel as those whose hearts were hold
Ere Cissy started singing.
N.s.w.
GEORGE STREET.

AMONG THE TROTTERS at VICTORIA PARK.
SITUATED at Zetland, close to the city and easily accessible by tram from Central or the Quay,
Victoria Park Racecourse is especially attractive to Sportsmen who patronise keen, exciting
racing. The extensive Grounds are artistically laid out with flower-beds—at present in full bloom
and are the finest in Australasia. They rival Flemington and Randwick for beauty and up-to-date
appointments, and equal the best Courses of the European Continent. The appointments of the
buildings are modern in every respect, no details for the comfort and pleasure of visitors being overlooked.
The two Scratching Towers are distinct features of Victoria Park, and equal any similar buildings in
other parts of the world. The Paddock Stand has beautifully furnished Retiring and Sitting Rooms for
the use of ladies —the sanitary arrangements being equal to those of the best City Hotels. Half of the
Ground Floor is occupied by a well-appointed Bar—the remainder reserved for commodious Dining
Rooms capable of seating 200 guests.
VICTORIA PARK possesses four racing tracks, the Trotting Track being installed at a cost of over
£lO,OOO. This track is used for Trotting Meetings under the auspices of the Australian Trotting Club.
On Thursday next, October 10, the next Meeting is to be held, the following events taking
place: Encourage Handicap of 35 Sovs., Flying Mile Handicap of 50 Sovs., Progressive Handicap of
35 Sovs., Unhoppled Handicap of 45 Sovs., and the A.T.C. Handicap of 60 Sovs.
Full information regarding Handicaps, etc., may be obtained from the A.T.C. Secretary,
20-21 Imperial Arcade, Pitt Street, City:


Women Who Shave.
DESPERATE MEASURES NECESSITATED
BY SUPERFLUOUS HAIR.
A CLEVER WOMAN COMES TO THE RESCUE.
To assert that many ladies in this country are*
compelled to resort to the razor in order to rid
themselves of superfluous hair on face and arms will
no doubt cause much surprise. It is, nevertheless,
true ; and women who, from time to time, shave off
one or two hairs really act as foolishly as those who
apply a depilatory to the whole chin, lips or arms,
to burn off the humiliating growth.
Of course, in either case the hair is only got rid
of until it can grow anew ; and the stiff, bristling re-
appearance of it causes tears of hysterical humilia-
tion. Then razor or depilatory is applied afresh ; for
what else can the poor sufferer do? There are
women whose mental and physical breakdown is
attributable to the misery of superfluous hair, which
they have no means of conquering.
A clever beauty specialist in Edinburgh, Mme.
Tensfeldt, has already saved some hundreds of
women from almost unspeakable distress by her in-
vention of a portable, self-acting electrolyser, by
which any woman can secretly rid herself forever of
every superfluous hair. There is no renewal of the
gro\Vth. Once electrolysed the root, is' dead. The
process (which is painless and absolutely infallible)
can be applied to any lady in the privacy of her own
room. Once used, the misery of superfluous hair is
gone for ever. The expense has been purposely fixed
low. Mme. Tensfeldt, 120 a, Princes Street, Edin-
burgh, Scotland, will send the whole apparatus in a
plain package for only £5 ss. When its good work
has been done, she will take the apparatus back and
allow £1 ss. for it second-hand if returned within
six months. Many ladies in this country will no
doubt be glad of the offer, and Mme. Tensfeldt guar-
antees the success of her electrolyser.*
Martin’s Fills are sold by all Chemists and
Stores in Australasia. Cadies recommend
them. Be sure you get the genuine with the
signature. Martin, Chemist, Southampton.*
Missing Friends.
HARRIS.
JAMES HARRIS (son of JAMES HAR-
RIS, of Cardiff, in the County of Gla-
morgan, Wales, Builder), is requested to
communicate with the undersigned to his

WIFE CURES HUSBAND OF ASTHMA.
“My wife is iny doctor ; she understands my com-
plaint very well,” says Mr. Alfred Burnett, 67 Wick-
steed Place, Wanganui, N.Z. “When I had an
attack of asthma she gave me a rubbing with
Chamberlain’s Pain Balm, got me to take Chamber-
lain’s Cough Remedy at the same time, and the
relief was wonderful. I am satisfied that this
treatment is the best I have ever tried, and I recom-
mend it to all sufferers from asthma.”*

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
IN BANANA LAND
“ Kobbi ” : Bananaland’s Legislature
wrestles with tremendous items at times. I
fear that the strain will be too much -for it.
Lor instance, the Bill to amend the Local
Authorities Act provides that it shall be no
longer necessary for a man to run in front
of a steam roller with a red Hag, scaring
horses out of their wits, and causing car-
riages and wheelbarrows to shy, and making
dogs flee into the next parish. Furthermore,
local bodies are no longer to be liable _ for
the loss of stock poisoned by the noxious
weeds which the local body should have
killed but didn’t. And —this is the real
thundering proposition—the age of the small
boy who is allowed to bathe in the ladies’
compartment at the seaside is reduced from
eight years to five, iu the best interests of
morality.
Adversity brings strange railway termini
as well as strange bedfellows. (Queensland,
though it has less than 700,000 people, has
more mileage of railway at work, and more
under construction, than any other State.
Now the slump has struck the loan market,
and items like this begin to happen: —
Instructions have been issued to the resident en-
gineer on the Wallal-Tobermory railway construction
works that operations are to be suspended at the end
of the permanent way, which is 52 miles-.out in
barren, dry country, but the line is to be brought up
to date and made fit for traffic before entirely ceasing
operations. A number of men will be thrown out of
work.
Lines are stopping every here and there —
at a corner peg, or a dead tree, or a bees’
nest, or an ant-heap, or any old place at
which a line can stop without falling down
and breaking its neck. Everything possible
will be done to turn the lines into revenue-
producers so far. as they have gone, but the
line which only goes as far as it has gone,
and there stops unexpectedly up against no-
thing, is seldom a first-class revenue-pro-
ducer.
The Bulletin receives a strenuous and
most interesting letter, and the heading on
the notepaper, of all the marvels in the
world, is “Young Men’s Christian Associa-
tion of Brisbane”:—
Silly Bull, builds a great fleet, and depends on his
enemeys and foreign trusts, suplying him with food,
a little of what will happen, if he has a scrap with
Unkle Sam, over the Panama ditch, the cliping
(enclosed) shows, Germany joining in, result, result
famine for Bull,
A point in the increase of insanity in Britain, not
noticed in a late Bulletin, continental nations are
quietly shipping their feble minded to Britain, which
receives them with open arms, a few years ago Canada
was guled into taking a few thousand feeble minded
of Russias hands, the doborookas, imigrants, or some
name like that, the croud that turned out their horses
and cattel, and hitched their women folk to the
plowes, under free imigration, Australia, is geting a
big percentage of similar goods, no continental nation
wants to part with its sound saine people,
The finger print fake, the police just grab the
first, known crimnal, put his fingre prints in their
I >ooks, get some artical from the scene of the crime
put his finger prints on that, result a cohviction,
thats all they want, the Police dog, that so much fus
s made of, worries any solitary person that comes in
his way, this is kept dark, he never wories a crimnal,
he is ready for it, and shoots it when it overhauls
him.
The 3rd degree is simply the torture system of the
ancients, under it the editor of the Bulletin and Hop
cpud be made confess, to murdering Adam and Eve,
cooking Cain on a gridiron boiling Abel in a billy,
up a dry gully, covered with water, ten feet deep,
and eating them for lunch like a kangaroo, that had
become fosilised, a million years ago,
Re posible scrap betwen Bull and sam, the Jap will
be round this wiv, whats our national Government
doing, nothing, killing time, jawing with the opposi-
tion, who want to get comand and do a darned sight
less than nothing, the point seems to be is there any
sanity, among the rulers of the British dominons,
Abolish the law shark, and crime will disapear, he
is the erimnals righthand, the hold he is geting on
everything, its dangerous to buy a box of matches,
or a pound of tripe, without consulting a solicitor,
buyer may have a law suit lasting years brought
against him,
To healthyfie cities, shoot all aldermen they are a
tie with flies and dogs for spreading sickness, applies
to Brisbane mostly, which ties with Pekin for filthi-
ness, evidently this city is trying to cultivate an
oubreake of plague, or smalpox object, to forse the
Goverment to make the sewers, and charge the costs
to the country, save property oweners from paying,
the cleanest premises in Brisbane are bungs pubs,
these wougers woud. abolish, Wouser and filth are one,
Bung is a dull man or he coud give the wouser hell,
per medium of the picture showes, where he coud be
showen in all his hedious vileness, they are splendid
subjects for filims, in Brisbane pubs and • boarding
houses no bell is now rung at meal times, as the
flies when they heard it, rushed from the filth in the
rear premises, straight on to the tucker on the table,
without wiping their feet on the doormat. —A. Blok.
Q. aboriginals have £20,153 to their credit
in the State Savings Bank, and the Denham
crowd is said to have a special missionary
touring the various camps to warn Warrigal
against the danger of putting his money in
the Commonwealth institution.
Just 50 years ago this month, the Govern-
ment of Bananaland introduced a Militia
Bill to provide for the raising of an effective
force of 50,000, with 50,000 of reserves. The
Bill was rejected. And even to-day there
are some people who yell loudly that “the
time is not yet ripe.” It makes one feel very
old.
Douglas shire council has just had the
bum-bailiff put in. The habit of loan crept
on it up to £3411 55., and its assets were
mainly the audacity to ask for more. Now
the Government wants money so badly itself
that the Treasury would almost boil down
a sheep that had died a natural death. So
it has appointed the shire clerk as receiver,
and whenever he sees tuppence in the dis-
tance he is instructed to chase it till it drops
-—or he does.
This is the recent utterance of Queens-
land Labor-leader Ryan—
Not all the legislation in the world will compel
men to handle goods produced by scabs.
It is a noble sentiment. .And it is a pity
that just now, when .Labor is preuoimua.u
in the Australian Parliament and in two
State Legislatures, and when it is within an
ace of getting a majority in two more State
Legislatures, men are handling more “scab”
goods, cheap labor goods, long-hour labor
goods, nigger goods* Chinese goods, leper
goods and every utterly accursed kind of
goods than they ever did before in Aus-
tralia’s history. This is the circumstance
which makes real Democrats want to swear at
a lot of the so-called Labor leaders. Because
they have neglected to push up the tariff,
while pushing up local wages, imports have
risen to a figure never before dreamt of.
These imports climb over the tariff barrier
solely because of their cheapness. They re-
present the output of the sweated worker,
and the non-unionist, and the colored per-
son, and everybody else who is horrible and
objectionable. And the Labor party (God
rest its 50u1.!.) hasn’t one word to say against
the miserable business. It handles “scab”
LENNON , M.L.A,
(Queensland)
goods without a semblance of objection, so
long as they are the goods of tne foreign
“scab,” who works, in many cases, at one-
half the wages of the Australian “scab.” Gn
the plain evidence of figures, Labor, in Aus-
tralia, has become the ally of niggers and
Chinamen and sweaters to an extent that its
worst Tory predecessors never accomplished.
It is good to see Labor-leader Ryan strike a
noble attitude, but it isn’t good to know that,
even while he was attitudinising, Ryan’s
constituents and supporters on the wharves
were acting as beasts of burden to the “scab”
and the cheap labor person and the sweated
worker and the Cnow and the Missing Link,
on a scale that was never heard of in the
worst days of old Toryism.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 28, 1912.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
Early next month we are to lose the Mac-
gregor chieftainess. The lady is to travel by
train to Melbourne, where the White Star
liner Medic will gather her in, and under-
take the delivery of the vice-regal goods in
the Cold Country.
The many tongues of gossip are now wag-
ging, to the effect that our Governor chiel
himsel’ has pit his bawbees into a heather-
covered estate in the land of theology and
oatmeal, and leaves us next March to get
on speaking terms with his crofter tenants.
I give the alleged information for what it
may or may not be worth.
Contralto Kirkby Lunn arrived here on
Friday, by the steamer Eastern. Almost
everything that was musical or unmusical,
or merely curious, was lying in wait for her
at the Centennial Hall, in order to extend
to her the hand-clap of welcome. Baritone
Shanks and the violinist with the floating
mane duly arrived, and were swallowed up
in eager, chattering waves of femininity, but
no Mrs. Lunn appeared. Then, when the
mob had begun to grow restive, a perspiring
secretary person rushed in and declared,
with great emotion, that some base-born car-
rier of luggage had gone astray with all the
Kirkby frills and the Lunn flounces, and that
he couldn’t be found, traced or discovered
anywhere on the limitless plain. Naturally,
the singer couldn’t show up until she got
some clothes, but the moment she had a rag
to wear the crowd was assured that her
smile would be in evidence. After another
pause of an hour, or thereabouts, a lace-clad
vision, topped off with a chic rose-colored
dream in straw, glode like the ghost of
melody into the tumult of greeting which had
been gathering force with each expectant
moment. M.L.A. Macartney murmured no-
thing worth speaking of in a pleasant way,
and then the Kirkby Lunn hand began to
shake the multitudinous naw of those as-
sembled to do her honor. After that, every-
body went home hungry, hapoy, and carry-
ing her proud fingers daintily before her.
To-night, the lady with the bulbul in her
throat is warbling to a well-filled Exhibition
Hall.
George V.’s understudy has been following
the dreary treadmill of show and church
bazaar. On Thursday he had samples of
both reputable sorrows —one at Brookfield,
the other at Sandgate. On Friday he spoke
words of cheer and comfort to the Beenleigh
farmers.
Premieress Denham looked in on the folk
in the hamlet of Dalby during the week, and
stayed under the mayoral roof. Mrs. Victor
Drury hostessed a bridge-party in the lady’s
honor, and her visit included such official
turns as opening a bazaar and smiling upon
the sanatorium and hospital.
Pretty Mrs. Leonard Turner and her equ-
ally pretty daughter left on Tuesday for
England. On Monday they gathered bunches
of their pals at Eschenhagen’s, and fed them
softly with the sandwich of farewell.
The N.S.W. bowling team returned to its
own green places on Friday, after spending a
thrilling week in our city. Some serious
business was done with the bowls; also, the
dissipations of the week included a euchre
party, a drag picnic—at which two of the
picnickers were gladly lost, and only recov-
ered with great difficulty—and an evening at
“Arcadia,” Hugh Black’s new show at New
Farm. At the euchre party, Mr. P. 0.
Sapsford captured a healthy silver rose-bowl
after a fine run, and bore it away with suit-
able and becoming blushes.
The consuls, vice-consuls and consular
agents went glad on Monday afternoon, and
took a large assortment of their frilled and
skirted friends down the river in the good
old ark Lucinda. The Wee Macgregors were
there, and most of the Very Best decorated
the decks and drank ozone in a social, inter-
national way.
Miss Marian Darvall, the pretty and popu-
lar damsel, whose engagement to Mr.
Matthew Wilkinson was recently made
known to public fame, was handed a cabin
trunk and suit case by the staff of the Rail-
way Department, with which she has held
a position for the last 12 years. Yesterday
she gave up her job, and all who saw her go
said they were sorry to lose her, and very
glad she was leaving, because she deserved
something better.
The Macgregor is at present at Rockhamp-
ton, whence he will be lured to preside at a
meeting of that mysterious and somewhat
cheerless body, known as the Public Morality
Council.
The Foxton aide is week-ending at Pike-
dale, where the Whites have gathered to-
gether much youth and beauty of the female
and other sorts.
The Symes lady, who recently got up a
tennis tournament, slaked the thirst of a
number of girleens with tea on Wednesday,
in order to mark the fact that the incident
was closed.
The Camerons of Lochiel offered sacrifice
to haggis and other strange gods on Thurs-
day. The affair was by way of “Gordspeed”
to the Dicksons, who are going to live in
your suburb.
WORM
THROWING.
A suffragist eluded the police on the Balmoral
golf links, and threw a worm in the face of Mr.
Churchill, First Lord of the Admiralty.
A suggestion for Coastal Defence..
The Q.W.E.L.-ers have been out ©n the
rampage. I really can’t tell you all the
things they resoluted, because they were
quite beyond the comprehension of your
Jane, but I know that four males,, .and
enough female enthusiasm to go a long way,
were fed to-day at Dutton Park, with re-
marks from the lips of Barnes and Wyn
Williams, together with logic ©f ham and
wisdom of roast fowl.
Yours breathlessly,.
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Treasurer Barnes (Budget speech) : “'"'We
must resist the encroachments of the Federal
Government.” Home Secretary Appel: “We
don’t want to put our savings to foreign
uses, anyway.” This was apropos of the
Savings Bank squabble, and illustrates ad-
mirably the State Rights view of the Aus-
tralian Government, which is alluded to as
a “foreign” Power.
Dr. Elkington, Q. Health Commissioner,
after a visit to the Far North, remarked that
“infantile mortality was a great test of
healthy climatic conditions.” Just notice
how Bananaland stands the test by latest
figures. Infantile mortality for the whole
Commonwealth (1910) was 74.81 per 1000;
N.S.W., 74.73; South Aus., 70.70; Vic.,
76.85; W.A., 78.18; Tasmania, 101.68
Quensland, 62.9 per 1000. For a country
that is supposed to be unfit for the white
race to live in. this is not too bad, especially
when one recollects that the infantile death
rate for England and Wales for 1909 (latest
figures obtainable) was 109 per 1000.
Bananaland’s “Liberal” Government
opened its little purse to the extent oL
£23,108 “to improve the position of teachers,
financially and otherwise,” as the Budget-
speech has it. As the State has more than
2700 teachers, the much-boomed improve-
ment amounts to less than £9 per annum.
It isn’t much “financially,” and it doesn’t
seem to be anything “otherwise.” But it is
so long since the pedagogue in Bananaland
saw a lift that he is gripping the mite with
both hands. He has grave fears that it will
be a long time before he gets another £9,
financially or otherwise.

PRELIMINARY NOTICE
Important Cattle Sale
BUSHY PARK STUD HEREFORDS,
KAI IWI, WANGANUI,
NEW ZEALAND.
"THIS FINE HERD was founded upon Mr. James
I- Stuckey’s renowned TE RANGITUMAU HERD.
It has even been still further improved from time to
time by importations from the best strains of Great
Britain. Its present owner, Mr. FRANK MOORE,
can confidently claim that it is unrivalled in the
Australasian Colonies for EVENNESS OF TYPE,
CONSTITUTION, EARLY MATURITY, and GRAND
RICH DARK COLOR.
The whole of these FAMOUS CATTLE are to be
sold by PUBLIC AUCTION in APRIL, 1913.
ABSOLUTELY WITHOUT RESERVE.
The Sale is a Genuine Clearing one, and is NOT
merely a reduction for culling purposes.
The BUSHY PARK HERD includes,
approximately:
101 COWS now in calf, and which will be again
in calf. From these are expected about forty-
five Heifer Calves this season.
29 HEIFERS, two-years-old. They will be in calf
at time of sale.
23 HEIFERS, yearlings.
5 COWS, old, in calf.
These latter are the only OLD cows in the lot.
The, foregoing constitute an aggregate of
203 FEMALE STUD HEREFORD
CATTLE.
To this total must be added a
LARGE NUMBER OF STUD BULLS
of different ages. The majority of these will be rising
two years at time of sale.
These MAGNIFICENT. CATTLE are practically all
eligible for entry in the ENGLISH HERD BOOK,
and they will be in-splendid condition when sold.
The. Sale of the BUSHY PARK STUD HERE-
FORDS is a unique opportunity for New Zealand
breeders to improve their Herds, or for Australian
and South American buyers to introduce a new
strain of blood.
In the conduct of the Sale there will act in con-
junction—
MURRAY, ROBERTS AND CO., Ltd.
FREEMAN R. JACKSON AND CO.,
WILLIAMS AND KETTLE, Ltd.,
Auctioneers.
Cablegrams of inquiry can be addressed: “Pinfire,”
Wellington, New Zealand.

persons willing to Sell Land in OXFORD

100 ft., with a depth of not less than 150 ft.

necessarily be accepted, should be addressed
to the Commonwealth Works Director, Cus-
toms House, Sydney. /

would come out on my face

“One night I asked a

pimples, and he advised me
to try Cut!''lira Soap and Cuticura Ointment
which I did. I would wash my face first

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching' scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
1 had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time—yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, 1 fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell mb that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a: quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been.as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world —I am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest. .

“Rimu”: Whaling seems to be a reviving industry
in these latitudes. Once the ships of American New
England hunted the sperm animal all over the Pacific.
Others bent on like adventure followed, and the man
with the harpoon was in some sense the herald of
progress and civilisation. Then the whales got
scarce, and the discovery of mineral oil reduced the
market for tried-out blubber, and slowly the fleets of
highly-flavored vessels faded away, and the shore en-
campments were forgotten. To-day the game has
been resumed on a better wicket, and the latest re-
sults suggests that it will pay something more than the
cost of overalls for the crew. The Ilananui 11. has
come back to Auckland (M.L.) from the Campbell
Islands, where, in spite of bad weather and indif-
ferent luck, it collected 19 whales. The Norwegians
at Jervis Bay (N.S.W.) have also been making oil
while the blubber hums. In 10 brief days they cap-
tured 15 of the animals which take a shower bath
from their own blow-holes. Old records and fish-
stories look as if they were going to be broken up
into very fine dust before such achievements as these.
* * * *
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : The death of Charlie
Kaufman removes a one-time prominent figure from
the sphere of Westralian mining and finance. He
first came here as assistant to London and Globe Fox,
who was drowned soon after as a result of a boating
accident on the Swan. Kaufman stepped # into Fox’s
shoes, and was instrumental in getting Whittaker
Wright and other heads of the L. and G. to palm the
now defunct Mainland Consols off on J. Bull.
Flushed with this success he and his principals
formed the then comparatively small Lake View and
Ivanhoe cos. into leviathan London corporations, with
much profit to themselves and those who got the tip.
Later on, Kaufman brought the Golden Horseshoe
under the notice of Whittaker Wright, but as the
latter wouldn’t look at it Kaufman got one of the
Reitlingers in tow, and the two formed it into a
London company, with a capital of £1,500,000, and
divided the plunder. After a lapse of some years,
Kaufman went into business on his own account. His
first big deal was the purchase of Merton’s Reward
from Fred Merton. It was in this flotation that
Rothschilds had their first and last deal in Westralian
mining by subscribing for 30,000 £1 shares, but
when the mine fell far short of representations their
money was returned. Kaufman’s last appearance on
the Westralian promotion stage was when he bought
and floated the Phillips River gold and copper mines.
Latterly he has been living quietly in London, and
when the writer saw him in Austin Friars two years
ago he confessed to being a physical wreck. While
things hummed he amassed a big fortune, but he is
said to have died poor.
The Sydney Gas Co. struck opposition at least
once in its life. Away back in 1869 a rival institu-
tion was projected, but the old concern bought up a
majority of its shares and ate it. It was a case of
dog consuming dog.
* * * *
Informative fusillade of barks from “Vic.”: The
Government has decided on certain power-dep6ts for
Melbourne railway electrification. One scheme (that
of Manton and Dare) is to harness the Big and Kiewa
rivers, Bogong High Plains. They have strong flow
always. Fed by range snows, and some fancy by
artesian sources. Steep courses, dropping 2000 ft. in
a few miles. Two water-courses would give a mini-
mum yearly supply of 32,000 horse-power, enough
for all Melbourne railways. Locality some miles
east of Bright. Huge turbines proposed. Power
carried 140 miles to Melbourne by copper cable. Un-
affected by industrial troubles, which may interfere
with coal. In Mexico electric power is transmitted
over 300 miles. San Francisco and Los Angeles are
lighted by power from vast distances. The Pacific
Electric Co. has water plants up to 93,000 horse-
power, also steam pla'nts of 96,000. At Niagara,
which supplies New York, there is available only
180 ft. fall (with immense volume), against 2000 ft.
(gentler) in Victoria. Water-power can always be
conveniently augmented by steam. In cheapness,
Manton and Dare challenge all rival schemes.
* * * *
On the authority of the Government Statist, the
shire of Deakin struck the lowest rate of Victoria’s
207 municipalities, viz., 9d. in the £, while Wal-
halla levied the highest, 2s. 6d. in the £. Seventy-
two councils were content to raise a rate of Is., and
lean on the Government for the rest of their in-
come. The, richest, the shire of Colac, last year
struck a rate of Is. 4d. in the £, and got the big-
gest State subsidy, viz., £l2OO. The City of Mel-
bourne, with rateable property, estimated to be
worth over thirty-six millions, levied a shilling rate,
although ratepayers paid 4d. more by way of a light-
ing rate. Melbourne City is the most brazen ex-
ample of a Socialistic paradise. Thirty per cent, of
its revenue comes from the sale of electric light, 14
per cent, from rental of its markets, shops and other
city property, and 23 per cent, from market fees,
licenses, etc. The service of the city loans
swallows 30 per cent, of its expenditure, street clean-
ing absorbs 8' per eefit., lighting 7 per cent., and
salaries and miscellaneous eat up 11 per cent., in-
cluding the Gawd Mayor’s income of £l5OO. Out
of a total expenditure of £364,760 a year, the City
Council gives the unsensational sum of £2485 to
charitable institutions.
* * * *
“F.”: The combined incompetence and infrequency
of the Victorian John Hop is rapidly turning the
local criminal into a gay and care-free leg-puller.
He mixes humor with crime as never before. Take
the case of Mrs. Green. She owned a superb asparagus
fern, which lived on her front verandah. Sikes,
beating the bounds, saw and collared the vegetable.
I Being too languid to take it up the street, he walked
round to Mrs. G.’s back door, and sold it to her, as
“a good match for the one on the verandah.” Then
having given three cheers for the system of sweating
| the police, he passed merfily on.
POLYSYLLABLES AND CHIFFONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Bourke-street draper advertises his sale as being
“gemnipolarie, galliardrous and gravitative,” whatever
that means.
Hail!
Soft-goods’ man.
Attendant to eternal feminine fancies,
Their fads and foibles, and whate’er enhances
Feminine beauty; to us ’tis educative,
Your sign, of words colossal,
Etymological apostle,
Inviting ladies to ambulate, peregrinate, penetrate
In search of bargains, “executors’ instructions, great
reductions,” provocative of ructions
Dear to them; truly your sale is “gravitative.”
Hail!
Encyclopaedia Britannica,
Inquirer into Eleusinian mysteries,
Delver into antiquated histories,
Pray make the task for us a bit less arduous;
I’ve searched in vain, for nary
In my Pears’ soap dictionary
Can I find a trace, but Galliard, ’tis bally hard, Oh,
tell me pard
Where can I find it, elusive polysyllable, my desire
is all unkillable, and as long as I can still able
I’ll worship the creator of that sesame—“Galliard-
rous.”
Hail!
Swear-word man,
Truly your profanity is exquisite,
’Tis the chance of a life time lost to those who miss
it.
I am enthused, my heart beats wild, hilaric
My vocabulary’s extended,
Full of words expressive-ended,
And when, suppose, some lout low-born, steps on my
corn—emotion-torn
I’ll paint the air blue with sulphurous adjectives,
devilish expletives, cursing his relatives,
Call him all kinds of fool, but cap with “Gemni-
polarie” !
Gemnipolarie, Galliardrous Gravitative
Good Lord ! ! ! ! !
VICTORIA.
W. H. M. PHILLIPS.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad
to hear of a simple and scientific device which will
restore your hearing, no matter how old you are.
There is no mystery or secrecy about it; we describe
it fully and explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and in the
same way you may use this sound magnifier as an
aid to weak hearing. But, unlike spectacles, the
Wilson Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and
comfortable. You can’t tell you are wearing them.
No one else can, either, because they are not seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off
them—the strain of trying to hear dim sounds.
What a blessed relief to have done with that
tiring strain 1
Being made of the purest and softest Para rubber
they cannot be felt, even when the head rests on a
pillow. For the same reason, a blow on the side of
the head will not hurt the ear. In fact, they pro-
tect the ear, the tender inner parts that are so
senstive, from cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing
sounds. ,
So that you may clearly understand why these
facts are true, and why the Sound Magnifier restores
the hearing, we give an illustration of one as it lies
in the ear. You will notice that the Sound Magm-

right to the

ed to carry the faintest sound right down to the

To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not only
trying to have people shout at them, but it hurts
the ear-drum. When age comes the ear-drum some-
times gets thickened, or loses its tightness, and
shouting does not act properly on it. It only
makes a confused and tiring noise. But the
Sound Magnifiers concentrate the sound in the best
spot for perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-nerve
completely, or were bom deaf, wo cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could do that, in spite
of some of the absurd claims made to-day. But if
you can hear the faintest sound, do not despair.
You will never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people in
Australia and New Zealand, who have been cured,
and you can be cured, too. You may wear the Ear-
Drums day and night, and take them out whenever
you wish. Children can wear them (we have spe-
cial sizes for children from six to twelve). Even
a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they
are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover and
also an inserter. After the first pair is bought you
may purchase a single drum at any time for Bs.,
but the pair you get at first will last quite
two years. Do not ask for a trial pair. We do
not send them out on trial, and so they are never
secondhand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent you at once, with the fullest
instructions. Don’t delay, but have the comfort of
good hearing as soon as you can.

Dyspepsia
Tortured by Wind Round the Heart—Skin Became
Sallow —Awful Complications Made Life a Burden.
Bile Beans are Invaluable in Mature Age
FOR all complications arising from
stomach and liver trouble, Bile
Beans are a sure and effective
remedy. Mr. H. Bannister, of Potts
Street, East ' Brisbane, Q., says:—
“Severe attacks of dyspepsia and acute bilious
bouts, followed by terrible pains in the head,
made my life a misery. My . whole system would
be shaken and strained by fits of retching. Bile
accumulated until it overflowed and caused me
much unpleasantness. My skin assumed an un-
natural yellow tint, and I always felt drowsy,
especially so after meals. Wind around the
heart, and pains in the chest and shoulders, all
added to my suffering, and, while these bouts
were on, my condition was very bad. After try-
ing a number of medicines, from which no good
resulted, I was persuaded to commence a course
of Bile Beans. I did so, and soon noticed that
the headaches and pains quickly disappeared.
After each dose I felt a corresponding benefit,
until, sffter a thorough course of Bile Beans, I
was once more enjoying perfect health, and I
have never been troubled with sickness since.
Although I have led a very strenuous life, now,
at 62 years of age, I feel in splendid condition.

The Men Who Sleep with
Danger.
[For The Bulletin.]
THE men who sleep with Danger
Are mostly quiet men;
And one may use a rifle,
And one may use a pen.
And some meet wrong with patience,
And some arise and strike ;
But in the big essentials
They're pretty much alike.
The men who go with Danger
Are mostly dreamy-eyed ;
Upon the swooping fo’c’s’le,
Or by the camp-fire side,
You’ll find them grouped together;
And always where they are
You’ll see a pipe-bowl glowing,
And savor a cigar.
The men who camp with Danger
Have jests that are their own,
And songs that you’ve forgotten,
And yarns you’ve never known.
There’s little you can tell them
Of deeds good, wicked, mean,
That men who’ve lived with danger
Have neither done nor seen.
The men who sleep with Danger
Sleep soundly while they may,
But always wake at midnight
Or just before the day.
A Something in the darkness
That shudders at the dawn —
A side-mate softly wakened,
A pistol swiftly drawn.
The men who sail with Danger,
Are actors, at command —
They lightly laugh to fool you
When Terror is at hand.
The men who sail with Danger
A wondrous insight have ;
They know if you are timid,
They know if you are brave.
The man who’s lived with Danger
Has knowledge all his oNvn ;
The foresight of a woman —
Of men who fight alone.
He learns from peace and comfort,
. He learns from care and strife;
j: He studies, night and morning,
The book that men call Life.
The men who live with Danger
See sermons in a log ;
They have the horse’s swiftness,
The instinct of the dog.
When illness comes to loved ones,
They know, where’er they roam —
Have you seen, ■without reason,
A bushman start for home.
They know and feel our “warnings ’’
As only gypsies do.
They know the Norse Fore-Goer,
Have heard and seen it, too.
They know when Death has passed them,
And the Death Watch is at end.
And they know when he is coming —
The Unexpected Friend. ■ ■
The men who live with Danger,
They take things as they go —
In seeming unpreparedness,
To those who do not know.
- They sleep when work is over,
And mind and body ache ;
But Danger whispers geiitly.
And they are wide awake!
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
Old Jim.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FOR the first time in all my busy,
beautiful life I found myself abso-
lutely without work to do. It was
not a comfortable position. I wandered
round the streets of Napier killing time, and
wondering where to look for a billet. The
grinding slavery of the railway department
seemed paradise now I had left it for ever;
and standing there in the autumn sunshine
I remembered only the bright spots of my
eight years of servitude.
Then a familiar voice and a strong whifl
of spirits roused me from my reverie, and
looking round I found myself face to face
with Du Callion, a surveyor who had,
rightly or wrongly, _ the reputation of being
the worst engineer in the country. I knew
him by sight—everybody did; but had never
spoken to. him, and I wondered what moved
him to address me.
“How is it?” I inquired, affably.
“Dry—very dry;” he replied. “They have
a new brand of soda water over at Dekler’s.
Shall we sample it?”.
As we sauntered across he spoke of our
last interview, and remarked cheerfully that
he had about a week’s work he could put in
my way ; and it began to dawn on me that
Du Callion, although it was only 11 a.m.,
was three sheets in the wind, and had mis-
taken me for somebody else. I had always
felt that Providence took a special interest
in me, and here was proof positive. We
tried the new brand of whisky, and sat down
to talk shop. '
“I’ve got the subdivision of that le
Hauke estate,” said Du Callion; “and a
sweeter little job a man never struck.
Here’s the plan, and I want you to hunt out
the co-ordinates for me by Friday. Will
you do that?”
I undertook the feontract, and called tor
more whisky, and that was the start of it.
The end of it was about 3 p.m. when I
landed Du Callion home, and handed him
over to his enraged wife. After enduring
her cutting remarks for about 10 minutes, I
started out to find a pump to put my head
under so that I might find out what co-
ordinates meant.
It took an afternoon of surf-bathing to
clear my top-piece, and. about 5 p.m. I met
Maroney, the second in command at the
local survey office. We had dinner together,
and I took him into my confidence. He swore
he would see me through; and he did, and I
started work for Private Enterprise at 10
a.m. next morning.
When I had fossicked out the co-ordinates
I wandered down to Du Callion’s office and
asked for him. A haughty clerk said he
wasn’t sure he was in, but he would oblige
me bv- looking. Then he returned, and said
Mr, Du--Callion was in, but what the devil
did I want to see him about, anyhow ? I
said I had some co-ordinates for him. The
aspect of the clerk changed. He unfroze
right away with a didn’t-know-you-belonged-
to-us air.
“Oh, flop right in then!” he said brightly,
throwing open the door and ushering me
into the presence of Du Oallion, who sat at
a table wearing a Calvinistic expression. He
had evidently forgotten me, and with a dis-
tant nod pointed to a chair.
“What can I do for you?” he said coldly,
resting his elbows on the edge of his table.
“I brought along those co-ordinates,” I
said cheerfully. . ■
“What co-ordinates?” he demanded with
a bewildered look.
“Te Hauke estate!”
“Oh, you’re from Mr. Kemp.”
“No, I got them myself.”
“For Mr. Kemp?’!
“No, for you. Don’t you remember ask-
ing me to do so at Dekler’s the other morn-
ing?”
“Oh, that was the day I was drinking,”
he said, unbending a little. “I thought I
told Kemp to get them for me —you are very
like him, and my sight not being too good,
and being in grog I suppose I took you for
Kemp. No matter —let’s have a look at em.
“Very nice, very nice, indeed,” he re-
marked. “A good clean bit of work. Very
nice.”
“I’m glad you’re satisfied. What about
another whisky?”
“Er—no-o; too early. After dinner drop
round, and if you are looking for work I
might be able to put some in your way.
Have you had much experience in the
field?” . . „ t
“Was brought up in it, I assured him
cheerily.
“Good! I may want a reliable man to
practically take charge of a road survey
through the King Country and the sub-
division of native tribal lands. Could you ,
undertake the job?” .
“I’ll undertake any job,” I replied, throw- ;
ing my chest out.
“It’ll take some of that fat otl: you before ,
you get it through,” he grinned. “Revert-
ing to that whisky idea of yours, I think the
scheme is a good one. Where’s my adjec-
tive hat?” . .
When I left him I got <?ut ot Napier as
quickly as possible—he might want refer-
ences, certificates, or the Lord knows what.
But if he never saw me he would not think
of the business till the last moment—and,
meantime, I must get into a quiet place and
swat up mensuration, trigonometry, and a
few dry subjects of that sort. So I hied me
away to Petane, where the willows are, and
in the camellia garden for which that place
is famous, I settled down to hard study.
I had been hard at work about three
weeks when I got a telegram asking me to
be ready to start for Wanganui next day at
11 a.m. I left in a hurry, and. collected
what I thought would be necessary. Then
I called round at Du Callion’s office and saw
the affable clerk. Du Callion had gone to
Wellington, and I was to pick up the men
at Wanganui and proceed up. the river to a
place called Atene, which is as near to
Athens as the Maori tongue can get, and
from there make a trial survey to Munga-
whero Gorge, and report at the earliest pos-
sible moment. Then the clerk handed out
money for fares, gave me some useful hints
as to the best people to get supplies from,
and wound up with a query? “What about
it?”
By the time we had seen about it, I had
just time to catch the express, and t9gether
we walked down to the railway station. I
slunk on board like a criminal. It was my
first great fraud.
* * * *
But it was not till I got to Wanganui and
found myself up against it that i realised
exactly how manv different varieties of ass
I was. I sat in the little pub parlor and
thought it over, half inclined to throw up
the sponge. And then my courage came
back to me, and I determined to see the
thing through. Meanwhile, I had to see
some men. ' , ~ ..
There were five of them, all evidently
born bushmen. tough, wiry, with plenty of
lung depth. I engaged them on the spot.
But I was still one man short, as my in-
structions were to secure six. I asked them
if they could recommend another.
And one of them, after thinking for a
moment, remarked that “Old Jim” had just
got over “a burst”; but he supposed I
wouldn’t give him a show.
I said I would, provided Jim knew the
work.
“Know the work —he’s a full-feathered
surveyor. One of the best. Worked on the
geodetic survey of the Island. But when
he’s in charge he drinks; gets on the booze
an’ deserts for days, and gets the job tied
up. But when he ain’t in charge he’s good
as gold. Give him a show, boss.”
“All right. Bring him along as soon as
you can. I want to get fixed up.”
* * * *
It was about 2 p.m. when “Old Jim”
arrived. The ancient one was a well-set up
man of about 50, tough as wire, with clear
cut features and a kindlv mouth fringed with
grey beard and moustache. I asked him if
he had worked on a survey before, and he
smiled.
“Oh, yes, a good deal. In fact, I’ve been
at it all my life, and I’m a qualified sur-
veyor. But I think my humble friend ex-
plained why I am reduced to this condition,
and it is a matter I do not care to talk
about. I should be exceedingly glad of a job
in any capacity, for I’m dead broke, and
very anxious to get away from the town.
Town has too many temptations for me.”
And he smiled sadly. I wondered if _it
was good enough to make the plunge. Jim
seemed- my last hope, and if he wouldn’t
come to terms, then I was certain to be
blown out as soon as we arrived at Atene.
Handing him a cigar I looked him straight
in the eyes with an earnest expression. He
lit up, and looked me as straight. Then he
spoke:—
“You’re tryiug to make up your mind to
say something, Mister, and wondering if I
can be discreet. Well, fire away. No mat-
ter how drunk I may be I never blab
secrets.”
Then I plunged.
And as I talked Jim’s eyes grew rounder
and rounder, till suddenly he exploded in a
wild laugh.
“You’re not pulling my leg, are you, sir?
It seems quite impossible.”
“It is only possible with a fool, Jim; but
I’m too hard up to be wise these times, and
(Continued on next page.)
