A LASTING CURE BY
DOAN'S OINTMENT.
Mr. Edgar Wood, Coogee Street, Randwick, says: “For 17 years I suffered with itching and
protruding piles. During that time I was treated by several well-known Sydney doctors. I also
tried chemists’ prescriptions, and used every remedy advertised to cure this complaint, but nothing
gave me the slightest relief. The irritation was so great that it caused me annoyance during the
day, and kept me awake for hours at night. I almost despaired of ever getting anything to
relieve me, as I had experienced so many failures. However, I saw an article in the paper stating
that Doan’s Ointment had cured a well-known Sydney man, who had suffered with this com-
plaint for 20 years. I at once got this Ointment, and am very pleased to tell you that the first
application gave me relief. I continued using it until I was perfectly cured. .My cure means a
lot to me, and is wonderful after suffering for 17 years. I wish you to publish this statement,
so that people suffering with Piles may know the value of Doan’s Ointment.”
Mr. Wood, says nine years after the above was given: “I am still free of Piles. Doan’s
Ointment made a perfect cure in my case, for I have been free of Itching and Protruding Piles
for the past nine years.”
DOAN’S OINTMENT is sold by all Chemists & Storekeepers at 2/9 per pot.
with your help I think I can bluff through.”
“Oh, that’s dead easy. A man who has
the hide to do what you have done '■"n bluff
through anywhere on his own. Of course
you have had some experience at the busi-
ness?”
“Never looked through a theodolite in my
life, Jim!”
“Well, I’ll be d d ! Well, don’t cave in
now. I’ll see you through, and when you
come back you’ll be a practical surveyor.
When do we start?”
“To-morrow, six o’clock. Now, about
pay. Of course I’ll pay you extra myself
for what you are going to do for me,
but”
“Never mind that. I don’t want money.
But I’ll tell you what I would like you to
do for me. When the job’s over I must have
a four-weeks’ burst, and then I want you to
look after me, after the fourth week, when
I’ve ‘got the rats.’ That’s the time one
needs a man to lean on. If you’ll do that
for me, I’ll see you through the gates of
Hell itself.”
“It’s a bargain,” I said.
And there and then he started to see me
through. He made out a list of the things
we would require, and accompanied me to
the store to see that I wasn’t fleeced. After
that he saw the goods safely stowed on the
river steamer, and came along and had din-
ner with me. That over, we went up to my
room, and he put in a good four hours
grounding me in the rudiments of field work.
I felt quite learned when I went to bed.
That survey I shall never forget. Of all
the frauds and hypocrites I was the great-
est. Every night in my tent Jim would
map out the programme for the next day,
and explain the why and the wherefore.
And every morning I would come out to
breakfast wearing a thoughtful expression,
and deliver my instructions with all the
easy confidence of a veteran.
After a few days Jim complained that he
was not feeling too good, and if it was all
the same to me he would stay in camp that
afternoon. I became alarmed, and when
the others had gone, hurried round to in-
quire what was wrong.
“Nothing, only I must do some calcula-
tions. You’ve got to produce a map, you
know, and all these figures have to be
worked out.”
“I thought they did that in the office!” I
remarked, aghast at this new complication.
“Not much —that’s got to be done here, in
camp, on wet days and odd afternoons.
And if you have ’em all fixed up ready for
Du Callion when he arrives he is not likely
to ask you anything about it. If they
aren’t, he’ll want you to start right away,
and probably stand over you while you do
it. So let’s get busy.”
It took the best part of four weeks to run
the trial line to Mungawhero Gorge, and
during the whole of that time every mail
brought me a letter explaining that Du
Callion could be expected two days later.
We were camped just below Munga-
whero’s biggest waterfall, amidst a wilder-
ness of clematis and wild convolvuli. Erom
my tent I looked up that wondrous gorge,
flashing with the broken sunbeams tossed
back from four waterfalls, and felt that I
was in Heaven. Every day I went out and
shot enough pigeons to keep the stock pot
going, and we fared sumptuously on pigeon
soun. potatoes and mutton bought from the
Maoris. An old native, who lived alone
with two wives just across the river, had
four cows, and he kept us supplied with
cream and milk. It was a picnic, and if
this was surveying the game was worth
learning.
I came back to camp one Sunday after-
noon about four o’clock, and found Du Cal-
lion sitting on a log smoking. He greeted
me affably, and informed me he had been
through everything and was more than de-
lighted.
“I was consumed with anxiety all the way
up,” he went on; “for about four days ago
I heard you had been a railway clerk or
telegraphist, or something of the sort, all
your life, and had never seen a theodolite.
Lord, I nearly went mad ; and away I came.
However, I have been through, and am more
than satisfied. I must apologise for doubt-
ing you.”
He slapped me on the shoulder, and told
me three funny stories in quick succession.
I ordered an early tea that night, and as
soon as it was over I asked Du Callion to
let me know what he wanted done next, as
it was my intention to get to bed early and
have a good sleep. He explained his scheme,
and I took notes of what he said —-they
would be handy for Jim. He was much im-
pressed with my careful methods. I saw
him again at breakfast next morning, when
he remarked that as I was just as good as,
if not better than, he there was no necessity
for him to waste time there. He would drop
in from day to day and keep an eye on the
work. Whether he did or not I cannot say,
but I can swear I never saw him again for
nearly three months. Then he came flying
through the bush-land, like a stormy petrel,
to inform us that the Government Inspector
was checking our work. And when he had
finished the long list of things he hoped
about, he stove me right in with: —
“And, by the way, old man, you might let
me have your particulars. Where you
qualified, and the date and number of your
certificate.”
This was awful, and this man was said
to possess a most violent temper. But the
sooner it was over the better.
“Well, as a matter of fact, I am not a
surveyor. Knew nothing about it till you
engaged me. Then I bucked in, and got
some slight smattering of it.”
There I stopped, for Du Callion was
showing symptoms of apoplexy. But with
a mighty effort he recovered himself.
“Look here, don’t you know that unless
this work has been dons by a qualified sur-
veyor it will all have to be done over
again?”
“I don’t know anything about the legal
side of the business, I’m afraid.”
“It’s no good trying to make yourself out
to be a fool. You’re a rogue, sir, that’s what
you are. I’ll be ruined —dashed well ruined
if old Sourface hears of this.”
“Why should he hear of it? Does he
know of your movements? Can’t you let
him think you have done your own work?
I don’t see anything to get excited over.”
“Very well. I’ll try and be calm. But if
vou are so woefully ignorant, how does it
come about that you do the work so well?”
“Comes natural like. Any fool who un-
derstands trigonometry can survey. There’s '
nothing in it.”
Again Du Callion showed signs of apo-
plexy. But he recovered as a horse’s head
appeared through the trees, and presently a
sour-faced man rode into view. He alighted
alongside Du Callion, and threw me the
reins as though I were a stable boy.
“I’m glad to see you are doing better
work than is your habit, Du Callion. In
fact, I hardly recognised it as yours at all.
Who’s assisting you?”
“Assisting me—no one. Damn it, it was
through having assistance that I fell in so
often. In future I do my own work, and
i’ll know it’s done properly.”
“A very excellent idea —very excellent
idea. It is evident that you can do good
work when you like. I am agreeably sur-
prised.”
* * * *
Du Callion gave the Inspector three days
start, and then he, too, went back to the
fleshpots of civilisation. We were glad to
see him go, although I must admit he never
interfered with the work, and spent all his
time making water-colors.
Then Jim began to" recover his thirst. He
moaned at nights, and advised all manner of
dodges to expedite the work. I never let him
out of my sight lest he should slip off and
make for the pubs at Taupo. The law for-
bade us to bring liquor into the King Coun-
try, and nobody would forward it had we
sent for it, as the penalty was so heavy that
only a wholesale order could be made profit-
able.
To make matters worse, the Inspector
suddenly reappeared, and Jim’s starved nose
caught the odor of whisky. It drove him
nearly crazy, and I shivered with apprehen-
sion. How the Inspector had got through
without drinking every drop of his liquor
supply I could not understand, for it had
been raining for three solid weeks. The
ground was in a horrible state, and the men
were carrying bundles of pegs on their
shoulders up the steep and slippery ridges.
Jim said he would go out and give a hand.
As bad luck would have it he found the In-
spector’s horse tied up at the foot of a par-
ticularly villainous hill. He dived into the
bag at once and drew forth the best part of
a bottle of real Scotch. Tim Ryan came up
with a huge swag of pegs at that moment,
so Jim dropped the whisky into a fern and
suggested to Ryan that he should comman-
deer the “hoss” and make him take the pegs
to the top. Ryan, already in a state of in-
surrection, clawed at the chance, and bring-
ing up a second lot of pegs, he slung a
bundle on each side of the Inspector’s saddle,
and with much cursing, dragging and kick-
ing. got the noble animal to the top of the
ridge. But get him down again he couldn’t,.
and he wasted two valuable hours trying to
do the deed. Then he gave it up, and, de-
scending, hunted round for Jim to get ad-
vice. Jim had vanished. Thoroughly
scared, Tim made back to camp in search of
him. There he found the Inspector foaming
at the mouth over the disappearance of liis-
horse. Tim got a brilliant inspiration, lie
said he had heard some Maoris whooping
and shouting, and perhaps they had taken
the horse to the top of the ridge. He offered
to guide the Inspector to where he had heard
the riot. The Inspector assured him it
would be to his lasting benefit to do so, and
golden visions of a permanent job on the
Inspector’s staff began to swim before Tim’s
eyes. So he led the officer straight to where
his horse was marooned on a pinnacle.
The Inspector dived his hand into the
bag. and when he pulled it out empty he
seemed terribly upset. He looked into the
bag, and then he examined the saddle care-
fully. He found the tree broken where the
horse had rolled on it when Tim was trying
to urge him down an impossible slope. Also,
he noted the marks of the pegs upon the
saddle flaps. After a brief excursion along
the ridge he grasped the whole scheme.
Then the storm burst, and he accused
Tim of robbery, destruction of his saddle,
and death by starvation to the horse which
could not be got down again.
I took all the men off the job, and set them
to work to clear a way for the horse; and
there was nearly a strike. But after about
10 hours hard labor the work was done, and
the horse brought down safely. When I got
back to camp Jim was asleep in the tent,
and the place reeked of whisky.
I anticipated that the Inspector would
condemn the whole survey, but he contented
himself with denouncing the pegs which Tim
had carried upon his saddle, and ordered
them to be burned. So Tim had to make
fresh ones and carry them up on his shoul-
ders.
At mid-day Jim appeared, clean and
bright, in a newly-washed canvas suit. He
made a clean breast of it, and after a con-
sultation we decided it was best to let Tim
remain under a cloud for a while. It would
check his arrogance. Of course we would
put him right later on.
* * * *
Back in wet, windy Wellington, I tried to
keep Jim off that burst. The only result
was that, instead of “getting the rats” in
three weeks, it took him six to reach the ani-
mals he had anticipated. I locked him up in
a room at the hotel, and went out to see
about getting more work. When I came
back Jim had gone. He was run in three
days later for having blue devils in a public-
place, and he had been in the “jug” two davs
when I heard of his fate. I went along and
offered to go security for his good behavior,
and as he was in bad health they handed
him out to me. I landed him in a private
hospital.
Three days later Du Callion met me, and
said he had effected a partnership in the
biggest surveying firm in Wellington, and he
had another subdivision of Maori lands on
hand. He offered me a good billet. I agreed
to accept it if I could take who I liked with
me, and was much gratified when Du Callion
said I might take Satan with me if I chose.
I started off to acquaint Jim with the good
news, and when I reached the hospital I
learned that he was dead.
So I wandered back to Du Callion and
handed in my resignation. I declineO to ex-
plain why. Du Callion was a fraud, who
had started to boom on, the strength of the
good work I had done up the Wanganui
River: and I was a fraud who had worked
up a reputation on the strength of the good
work Jim had done. The base or ground-
floor of the swindle was dead. The middle
or second storey, which was myself, wisely
retired from the scene. As for the roof,
which was Du Callion, I left him to work
out his own salvation —which he didn’t.
C. A. JEFFRIES.
